Summary 0:
Cross-border distribution of collective investment undertakings
Cross–EU cross–border distribution
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1160 — requirements for marketing communications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down minimum requirements for the marketing communications* and pre-disclosure* documents* of collective investments* (UCITS* and AIFs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to marketing communications, including:
a key investor information document (KIID) for a collective investment undertaking;
a marketing communications document for a UCITS management company;
the marketing communications of a qualifying social entrepreneurship fund.
It does not apply to the marketing of investment products.
The marketing communications must:
be published in a language easily understood by investors;
be clearly identifiable as marketing communications;
notify the competent authorities of the EU country in which pre-marketing is taking or has taken place;
provide information on the marketing activities that are taking place on that country’s territory.
EU countries must ensure that marketing communications comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
Ex-ante verification of marketing communications is carried out by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA).
In addition, marketing communications may be subject to ex-ante verifications by the competent national authorities of EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
 
The regulations apply from 2 August 2021.
BACKGROUND
Directive 2009/2011/61/EU on cross-border marketing
EU rules on marketing communications and marketing documents
The European Investment Bank Regulation (EuVECA) lays down rules on the use of marketing materials and marketing communications.
For more information, see:
European Union marketing communications (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1286/2014/EU of 2 June 2014 on the application of Directive 2009/65/EC with regard to cross‑border marketing communications in respect of the cross‐border distribution and marketing materials of collective investing undertakings.
REFERENCESMA Implementing Decision 2014/1286/EU
2.6.2014:
EU Member States must establish a notification portal into which competent authorities should upload all documents regarding the cross‑international distribution of UCITS and Aifs.
1.7.2015: EU Member States are required to establish a portal to upload documents regarding:
cross‐border marketing materials;
marketing communications for UCITS;
EU-wide marketing materials for AIFS;
ex‑ante verification.
KEY TERMS
* Collective investment undertaking: an investment undertaking that is externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form.
* AIF: a fund that is managed by a management company.
ACT
Council Implementing Resolution (EU, Euratom) No 652/2014 of 20 May 2014 on common rules on cross‐national marketing communications relating to the cross–international marketing communications for collective investments in the Member States.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Commission Delegated Regulation (EU), Euratom and Euratom of 20 April 2014 on marketing materials relating to marketing materials related to cross‐international marketing of marketing material for marketing materials in relation to marketing material related to marketing campaigns for marketing material relating to investment products (OJ L 347, 20.5.2014, pp. 1-2)
Regulations (EU and Euram) No 596/2014, Euram No 597/2014 and (Euram No 696/2015) of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 April 2014 laying down common rules for marketing marketing materials concerning marketing communications related to investment campaigns for investment products and on marketing campaigns relating to research and development.

Last updated: 22.07.2014



Summary 1:
Ensuring the proper application of EU harmonisation legislation on products
Establishing a market surveillance system
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/746 on market surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system for market surveillance in order to ensure that the harmonisation of EU legislation on goods is applied correctly.
It sets out the procedures for the market surveillance of products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products:
from manufacturers to importers;
from traders to consumers.
EU countries must establish a single liaison office.
The liaison office must:
conduct market surveillance;
report to the national market surveillance authorities;
make available on the market any information it receives on risks or non-compliance;
take corrective action if necessary;
ensure that the corrective action that was to be taken by economic operators has been taken.
National market surveillance strategies
Each EU country must draw up a national strategy on the application of the legislation. It must include the following information:
the identity and areas of competence of the national authorities designated under Article 25 of the regulation;
the product classification;
a description of the risks and non-compliances that the product presents.
Joint enforcement
The legislation provides for the setting up of joint enforcement authorities between EU countries.
If a product presents a serious risk to the EU’s financial interests, an applicant authority may submit a duly reasoned request for enforcement measures to a requested authority in that other EU country. These measures must be proportionate, effective and dissuasive.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that adequate financial resources are always available for the appropriate staffing and equipping of the authorities.
Implementing measures
EU governments are required to:
establish a national information and communication system (NICS);
establish the following procedures in connection with products subject to the legislation:
procedures for following up on complaints or reports on issues relating to risks or violations of the law, including:
reporting to the competent authorities of the EU country concerned;
checking that economic operators have taken the necessary corrective action;
monitoring the implementation of the NICS;
assessing the effectiveness of the joint enforcement activities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 16 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 July 2022.
BACKGROUND
The EU is one of the largest trading partners in the world. It has the largest single market for goods in terms of value added tax (VAT) and gross domestic product (GDP).
For more information, see:
‘Market surveillance’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Importer: any person, company or organisation responsible for placing or making a product on sale in the EU.
ACT
   
Commission Regulation (EC) No 746/2017 of 16 June 2017 laying down market surveillance procedures in relation to the application, on behalf of the European Union, of the Union harmonisation law on products (OJ L 326, 29.12.2017, pp. 1-2)
last update 14.02.2019



Summary 2:
EU rules on rights in retransmission of television and radio programmes
EU rules in respect of rights in broadcasting programmes
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 — rules on the clearance of rights for the provision of online services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve cross-border access to the broadcasting of certain types of television programmes and to ensure that the rights of the copyright and related rights in the works or other protected subject matter used in the programmes are protected when providing ancillary online services.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all rights that are necessary for a broadcasting organisation to be able to communicate to the public or make available to the general public its programmes when providing online services, including:
the clearance of any copyright, related rights and the rights in phonograms or performances;
the rights in audiovisual works and performances.
It does not apply to the rights exercised by broadcasting organisations for the transmission of their own transmissions.
The scope of the directive does not cover:
rights exercised by the broadcasting organisation regarding authorisation for broadcasting of broadcasts;
rights exercising by the collective management organisation (collective management organisation) of broadcasting organisations regarding authorisations for retransmissions of broadcasts.
EU countries must ensure that, when setting the amount of payment to be made for the rights to which the country of origin principle, as set out in paragraph 1, applies, the parties take into account all aspects of the online service, such as features of the service, including the duration of online availability of the programmes provided in that service, the audience and the language versions provided.
Remuneration
Rights holders must receive appropriate remuneration for the right to transmit their works and other subject matter.
Rightholders should be entitled to a direct share in the remunerations paid by the operator of a retransmitting service. The amount of this share should be calculated according to the revenues of the broadcasting organisations generated by the retransmitter, which are used, in particular, by radio broadcasting organisations.
In addition, the directive provides for a transitional period to allow broadcasting organisations, signal distributors and rights holders to adapt to the new rules on exploitation of works and subject matter through direct injection.
References
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal

Summary 3:
The.eu top-level domain (TLD) and the.eu Multistakeholder Advisory Group (MAAG)
The.u Top-Level Domain and the MAAG
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 on the.u TLD
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a contract between the European Commission and the European Registry of Top Level Domain Names (Registry of the European Union).
It aims to improve the organisation, administration and management of.eu TLD by the Registry in accordance with the instructions of the Commission resulting from the Commission’s supervisory activities as provided for in this regulation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes:
a registry for.eu;
an advisory group for the management and organisation of the TLD;
a multistakeholders advisory group.
The registry may reserve or register a number of domain names considered necessary for its operational functions under the contract referred to in Article 8(4) of the regulation. These names are reserved or registered by the Commission and may be transferred to the registry by the EU countries.
Registry responsibilities
The Registry must:
ensure that:
the.eu domain name is used only for legitimate purposes;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to manage the.eTLDN;
carry out its tasks according to the rules and procedures set out in the regulation;
ensuring that the registry is independent from any third parties.
It may reserve, register or transfer any domain names it considers necessary for the operation of the domain name.
In addition, the registry may:
assist the Commission in the implementation of its tasks;
make available to the Commission information it receives from the registry;
use the registry for its own purposes, as well as for the purposes of its advisory group;
take the necessary measures to protect the confidentiality of the registry and its data.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulations apply from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for managing and supervising.eu since 1 January 2013.
For more information, see:
‘.eu’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 4:
EU funding for smart, interoperable, sustainable, multimodal, inclusive, accessible, safe and secure mobility: the European Commission’s Connecting Europe Facility (CEF)
European Union (EU) financing for smart and interoperable mobility
The European Commission is establishing a European Commission Communication on the European Communities’ Common Fund for Mobility.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2014/EU of 21 December 2014 on the EU funding for mobility.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a European Communities' Common Fund, the European Union's (EU's) mobility fund.
It sets out rules on how the CEF is to be used.
KEY POINTS
The CEF supports projects in the following sectors:
transport:
smart mobility;
infrastructure;
smart energy;
municipal transport;
information and communication technology (ICT);
information, communication and publicity;
health and safety;
science and technology;
education and training;
environmental protection;
sustainable urban development;
access to affordable housing.
Scope
Actions contributing simultaneously to the achievement of one or more objectives of at least two sectors, as provided for in Article 3(2), points (a), (b) and (c), shall be eligible for EU financial support under this regulation and to benefit from a higher co-funding rate, in accordance with Article 15% of the funding rate.
EU countries must develop their own national multiannual investment strategies in the areas covered by this regulation. The CEFinance strategies must include a description of their priorities.
The Commission must:
identify the priority areas for investment in order to achieve the objectives identified by the Commission;
draw up a list of priority areas to be supported by CEF funding;
provide the Commission with an annual report on the progress made by EU countries in achieving the objectives set out in this regulation;
make recommendations to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) on how to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the EU's mobility policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a member of the World Trade Organization (WTO) and the World Economic Forum (WEF).
For more information, see:
European mobility fund (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Smart mobility: projects that aim to improve mobility by:
improving the mobility of goods, goods, capital and services;
interoperability of transport networks;
motorways: networks that are open to all modes of transport, whether by road, rail, air, sea or other means of transport.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Communication of 20 December 2014 establishing the European Community's Common Fund: Mobility (COM(2014) 552)

Summary 5:
Removal of terrorist content: hosting service providers’ obligations
Removalling terrorist content
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/784 — hosting service provider obligations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s digital single market by removing terrorist content.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all hosting services in the EU.
It does not apply to:
the hosting of websites or hosting of online services;
the content hosted on these services.
The hosting service must:
remove terrorist content or disable access to terrorist content in all EU countries as soon as possible and in any event within one hour of receipt of the removal order;
ensure that the content it removes complies with its terms and conditions;
provide the complainant with the reasons for its decision.
Removal order
A removal order must be issued by the competent authority of an EU country. The competent authority must adopt a decision on whether the order comprises an infringement. If it considers that it does not, it must issue a removal order.
If the order does not contain an infringement, it may be revoked. The decision must be published in the Official Journal of the European Union (OJ).
The competent authority may issue a temporary or permanent suspension of a hosting service if it considers it is necessary for the hosting service to take the necessary measures to ensure that the order is complied with.
Requests for information
Hosting service providers must receive requests for information from the competent authorities of EU countries. These may include:
a description of the content they host;
information on the nature and content of terrorist material;
a list of websites hosting the content;
links to other websites hosting terrorist material.
A request for information may also be made via a contact point. The contact point should serve only for operational purposes.
In the event of a complaint, the hosting provider must provide a copy of the complaint to the complainant. The complainant must also provide the information on the content that was removed and the reason for it, as well as a description of how the content was removed.
When a host fails to comply with its obligations, the complainant may ask the competent national authority to issue a ‘removal order’. This order may be valid for up to 6 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to strengthen the fight against terrorism, including:
Directive 2014/46/EU on combating online terrorism;
Directives 2014/47/EU and 2014/48/EU establishing a European Union Agency for Combating Online Extremism and establishing an Agency for the Suppression of Terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting online terrorism’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EU, 2021/783) of 21 December 2021 laying down rules on the hosting of services and on the enforcement of removal orders.
Successive amendments and corrections to Regulation (EEC, EEC, Euratom) No 2101/2011 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/45/EU of 20 December 2014 on the establishment of a European External Action Service for combating online extremism and combating terrorism.
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation of Council Directive 2014/43/EU concerning hosting service provision obligations.
Commission Communication from 20 December 2013 on the strengthening of the online environment against online terrorism and combating terrorist content (COM(2013) 596 final of 21.12.2013).
last update 04.02.2016



Summary 6:
Scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact of Horizon Europe 2021-2027
Scientific and technological, environmental, social, economic and societal impacts of Horizon European Research
SUMMARY OF:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021/2027 — rules on participation and funding
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE FRAMEWORK PROGRAMME?
It sets out rules on the participation of the European Union (EU) in Horizon Europe, the framework programme for research and innovation (R&I) and the European Innovation Council (EIT).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers:
research and innovation in the fields of science and technology (R & I) and innovation* (I);
science and technology policy (S&T);
environmental and social impact;
health and well-being.
It is funded by the European Research Council (ERC), the European Commission, the European Economic and Social Fund (EFSF) and Horizon Europe (Horizon Europe).
Programme objectives
The framework programme is designed to:
support the EU countries to increase their R&I activities;
improve the competitiveness of the EU’s economy;
contribute to the sustainable development of Europe;
promote sustainable development.
Programmes
The Framework Programme is divided into 4 pillars:
Global Challenges and European Industrial Competitiveness;
Health and Well-being;
Science and Technology (S & T);
Environmental and Social Impact;
Innovation.
The Horizon Europe programme covers the following:
Research and Innovation in the field of science, technology and innovation:
1.
European Research Fund
The European Research Fund (ERFSPF) is the main funding body for Horizon Europe. It is responsible for:
investing in research and innovative activities in the areas covered by the programme;
providing financial support for research projects;
supporting research projects in the area of environmental and social impacts.
2.European Innovation Council
The EIT is the body responsible for the implementation of the Horizon Europe programmes. It:
provides financial support to research projects and projects in fields of S & T;
coordinates the activities of the EIT;
organises and implements research programmes and projects under the EIFRSF.
3.Seals of Excellence
Seals are open to EU countries and non-EU countries. They are designed to support research projects, projects and activities in areas of S&T, such as:
biotechnology;
biomedicine;
medical research;
aeronautics;
materials and materials science;
information technology;
space research.
4.European Research Council
This body:
is responsible for implementing Horizon Europe and its programmes;
is chaired by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE PROGRAMME APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
Horizon Horizon Europe is one of the most important instruments of EU policy. It aims to contribute to achieving the EU's objectives of sustainable development and to improve the quality of life of its citizens.
For more information, see:
‘Hoya Europe’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 7:
EU programme for the development of the European Union’s space industry
EU programme to develop the European Space Agency (ESA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 — EU programme for developing the EU's space industry (European Space Agency)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU programme ‘ESA’.
It sets out the objectives of the programme and the rules for its implementation.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The programme is designed to:
promote the development and use of space technology;
contribute to the creation of a competitive and dynamic European space industry;
improve the safety and security of sensitive national infrastructure;
provide for the protection of the interests of the EU and to comply with EU rules.
Eligibility and participation
ESA is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Commission. The programme is open to any EU country wishing to participate in it.
EU countries are required to apply the eligibility and participation conditions laid down in the regulation.
The European Commission must inform the programme committee of all EU countries’ views on the scope of application of and the justification for those eligibility conditions, as well as the reasons for those conditions.
A security monitoring structure is set up by the Commission when appropriate after the risk and threat analysis has been carried out. It is responsible for:
monitoring the programme;
ensuring that the programme complies with the requirements laid down by the regulation;
assisting the Commission in its decision-making process;
;
preparing a report on the results of the security monitoring;
examining the programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU programme is one of a number of EU programmes designed to develop and develop the space industry. The other programmes are:
the EU programme on space technology (see summary);
the European Union programme for space research and development, which aims to improve the performance of European research institutes and research centres;
the ESA programme for research and innovation in the field of space.
For more information, see:
European Union programmes (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 establishing the European programme for development and operation of the Space Agency, and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Commission Implementing Decision (EU), Euratom and Euratom Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2015/852 of 27 November 2015 on the implementation of the Programme for the Development of the Union's Space Industry (European Union programme) (European Agency for Space Technology) (ESA programme).
Decision 2014/853/EU of Euratom of 26 November 2014 on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European Parliament and the Council on the establishment of a European Union Space Agency and on the European Agency for Research and Innovation (ESA).
Council Decision 2014/955/EU on the signing, on behalf of the Commission, of the agreement between the Commission and the European Atomic Energy Community on the organisation of the ESA programme (European Atomic Energy Agency).
last update 04.01.2016



Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Hubs programme (EDI) 2021-2027 (see summary)
European Digital innovation hubs programme (See summary).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 establishing a European digital innovation hub programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The programme establishes a network of European Digital Innovation HUBs to support the development and deployment of cutting-edge technologies such as artificial intelligence (AI) and high-performance computing (HPC) in Europe.
The aim of the programme is to create a European ecosystem that provides the necessary HPC and data capabilities for Europe to compete globally.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EDI programme is designed to support:
the development and acquisition of the advanced digital skills needed for the deployment of new technologies in the field of AI or HPC, in cooperation with relevant industries;
the advanced skills part of Erasmus+ complements the programme, addressing the acquisition of skills in all domains and at all levels through mobility experiences.
Objectives
The objective of the EDI is to:
provide access to digital capacities for any business, in particular SMEs, in any region across the EU;
ensure the interoperability and interoperability of the EU’s information and communication technologies (ICTs) with other EU countries;
support the development programmes and initiatives in the area of information and communications technology (ICT);
promote the use of the best use of digital capabilities and interoperability in the context of the digital single market;
;
encourage the sharing of best practices and best practices between EU countries.
Programme objectives
The programmes are implemented in the following areas:
High Performance Computing:
supporting the development of high performance computing infrastructure;
advanced digital skills;
research and development in the areas of artificial intelligence and HPC;
science and technology policy.
Innovation hubs:
establishing a European Innovation Hub in the territory of an EU country;
developing and deploying innovative solutions in the fields of science and technology;
promoting the exchange of best practice and best practice between the EU and non-EU countries in order to improve the quality of research and innovation;
exchange of experience between the national and EU-level innovation hubs.
Evaluation of the Programme
The European Commission will evaluate the programme every 3 years.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Decision (EU) 2021/2027
 
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The ‘Digital Innovation Hub’ programme was established for a period of 7 years to align its duration with that of the multiannual financial framework laid down in Council Regulation (EU), Euratom.
For more information, see:
European digital innovation hubs (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* High performance computing: a system of supercomputers capable of executing 1018 floating operations per second.
* Advanced digital skills: a set of skills that are needed to deploy cutting edge technologies.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2020/2021 of 21 December 2020 establishing a programme for the implementation of the European strategy on HPC (OJ L 183, 21.12.2020, pp. 1-2)
last update 04.02.2021



Summary 9:
EU programme for the development, production and dissemination of European statistics for 2021-2027 (European statistics programme) (European statistical programme)
EU programme to develop, produce and disseminate European statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/523 — European statistics programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European statistical programme (ESP) for the period 2021–2027.
It aims to provide the overall framework for developing, producing and disseminating European statistics.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is designed to:
provide the European Commission with the necessary resources and expertise to develop and produce European statistics;
ensure that European statistics are developed, produced and disseminated in accordance with the EU’s statistical code of practice (ECP);
improve the quality and reliability of EU statistical data;
promote the exchange of information between EU countries’ statistical institutes;
contribute to the improvement of the quality of European statistical data.
Objectives
The ESP aims to improve the quality, reliability and accuracy of EU statistics by:
developing and producing European statistics in line with the European Statistical Code of Practice;
improving the reliability and comparability of the EU statistical system;
providing European statistics with a high degree of accuracy;
reducing the number of illegal and non-compliant goods entering the market through targeted awareness raising and training, support for Product Contact Points (PCPs) and better cooperation among competent authorities for mutual recognition.
Sustainable development of the European statistics system
The European statistics must be based on:
the European statistics code of practices (ESDP);
the EU statistics for sustainable development;
the quality of the information and communication systems (ESSPs);
EU statistics for the sustainable development of European tourism and tourism-related activities;
European statistics for research and development (ESDs);
research and development in the field of sustainable development.
The objectives of the ESP are:
to develop, apply, assess and monitor international standards in the fields the specific objective referred to in Article 3(2)(c)(i) of this regulation;
to improve the accuracy of European and national statistical systems.
In order to achieve these objectives, the ESPs must:
be based on the best available international standards;
develop, apply and monitor EU statistical systems;
have a high level of statistical accuracy and reliability;
be open to the participation of non-EU countries in the programme;
participate in the activities of national and international organisations and bodies, such as the World Health Organization (WHO) and the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe (UNICEF).
The Commission may adopt implementing acts establishing lower co-financing rates than those of other EU funding instruments.
Implementation
The regulation sets out the following forms of EU funding:
debt and equity instruments established under the SME policy window of the InvestEU Fund;
investment funds established under Regulation (EU/523/2020;
exchange-traded funds (ETFs) for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs); and
investments in research and innovation (R&I) programmes.
EU funding may be shared with other Union funding instruments if the envisaged actions under the programme pursue objectives which are common to various funding instruments, excluding however double financing.
Eligible actions
EU programmes may be eligible for funding from one or more EU programmes. They may also receive support from the European Research Council (ERC) or the European Statistics Office (ESO).
EU countries may participate in the EU programme on the basis of their national laws and regulations. The Commission must consult the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on the Functioning of the Union (Euratom) on the eligibility of EU programmes and projects.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU programme is part of a package of measures designed to support the development and production of statistics in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
European Statistics Programme (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* European statistics: European statistical system: a system of national, regional and sub-regional statistical systems designed to ensure that EU statistics are accurate, reliable and comparable.
* EU statistics: a set of statistical rules and procedures for the collection, processing, transmission, analysis, interpretation, evaluation, interpretation and verification of EU and national statistics, as well as for the dissemination of statistics, including for the purposes of EU policy-making.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC) 2019/515
1.1.2021

Summary 10:
European Union rules on the transfer of firearms and their essential components (EU-wide system for tracing)
European Union (EU)-wide system on tracing firearms and its essential components
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/2403 — transfer of certain firearms and essential components from government stocks to permanent civilian use
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect the public and the economy of the EU against the proliferation of firearms.
It sets out rules on transfers of firearms, their essential parts and their traceability.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all types of firearms:
automatic firearms;
machineguns;
automatic pistols;
battery-operated rifles;
conventional firearms; and
automatic revolvers.
EU countries must ensure that:
the transfer of a firearm or its essential parts from a government stock to a permanent civilian stock complies with the directive’s technical specifications;
the firearm is accompanied by a certificate of authenticity and a unique marking.
Transfer of firearms from a national stock to another EU country
The transfer must comply with the technical specifications for deactivation of firearms set out in Annex I to the directive.
The Commission must ensure, at the time of transfer of the firearm from the national stock, that the unique marking is affixed to the firearm.
National authorities must check that the transfer is carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex II.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE IN FORCE?
From 8 April 2016.
In accordance with Article 1 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU), EU countries must transfer firearms from their national stocks to the permanent civilian stocks of the other EU countries.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has a system for the exchange of information between EU countries on firearms and the essential components.
For more information, see:
‘Transfer of Firearms’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 11:
Maritime National Single Window (EMSWe) for the reporting of ship-to-ship activities
Maritime Navigation Information System (EMSWe)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 supplementing the Maritime Navigation Single Window
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a single database for maritime transport information.
It aims to improve the efficiency of the EU’s maritime navigation system (EMSW).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the maritime transport of goods and services between EU countries;
the transport of passengers and cargo;
passenger transport;
ship security procedures.
EU countries must:
ensure that the data they collect are accurate and reliable;
provide the European Commission with the information it needs;
use the information they collect in accordance with the relevant EU law.
National coordinators
Each EU country must designate a national coordinator, who is responsible for:
monitoring the operation of the EMSW;
ensuring that the information collected is accurate;
assisting the Commission and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) in the implementation of the regulation;
keeping a list of the national coordinators.
Reporting obligations
The European Commission:
is responsible for the collection and processing of the data it receives from the EU countries.
The Commission must ensure that:
data collected are:
accurate;
relevant;
representative of the relevant national laws and regulations;
inform the Commission of any new reporting obligations that have been adopted;
have been incorporated in the data set;
identify the data elements that are subject to reporting obligations;
notify any new data elements to the Commission without delay.
Data protection and confidentiality
The data collected must be stored in a secure database. Personal data relating to ill on board ships must not be stored on that database.
Implementing acts
An implementing act is adopted by the Commission to supplement the regulation by supplementing it by establishing and amending the EMSWe data set and by determining definitions, categories and data specifications for the information elements, and in respect of the Annex to incorporate reporting obligations existing at national level as well as to take account of any reporting obligations adopted by EU legal acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 15 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EMSW is one of a number of EU-wide systems designed to provide the EU with information on maritime transport.
For more information, see:
‘Maritime Transport Information System’ on the EU Commission's website.

Summary 12:
E-Tolls: rules on the application of the EU’s electronic toll system (ETS)
E–Tolls
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/852 on the operation of the Electronic Toll System (EETS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the functioning of the European Electronic Toll system (ETS) by:
ensuring that EETS users are charged a toll on their vehicles when they use the ETS;
improving interoperability between EETS providers;
reducing administrative burdens for EETS service providers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
vehicle registration data;
road fee information;
information relating to vehicles;
data relating to the owners or holders of the vehicle.
EETS providers must:
provide toll-charging authorities with access to the following national vehicle registration data, with the power to conduct automated searches thereon;
ensure that these data are made available to the public;
cooperate fully with toll chargers in their enforcement efforts;
use the information they receive from the national contact points of other EU countries’ national toll charging authorities in order to establish a failure to pay a road fee.
Requests for information
EU countries must ensure that:
the competent national authority or entity responsible for levying the road fee forwards the information it receives to the competent authority of the EETS provider within 60 days of receiving it;
the relevant authority forwards it to the relevant authority within 30 days of receipt of the information.
The competent authority in the information, if requested, must provide the relevant information within 15 working days.
In addition, each EU country must make public the contact details of that office, and provide them, upon request, to interested EETS-providers.
Toll chargers must cooperate fully with EETS providers in enforcing road tolls. They must also cooperate with toll-chargers in the calculation of applied toll on the vehicles of EET users by the EET providers, so that the applied tolls are kept to a strict minimum.
Rights and obligations
EETS service providers have the following rights and obligations:
to comply with the rules and obligations laid down in the directive;
to ensure that the toll charger does not disclose such data to any other toll service provider.
to cooperate with the authorities responsible for enforcing road charges.
To ensure that they comply with these obligations, the competent authorities must ensure:
that:
they have the necessary information to identify the vehicle and the owner or holder of that vehicle;
that the vehicle is registered in the EU country in whose territory there was a non-payment of road fees has been established;
they are able to search for the vehicle in the national register of national toll charge offices.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2018.

Summary 13:
Joint deployment of immigration liaison officers in the Schengen area (EU) and non-EU countries (Recast of Regulation (EC) No 863/2007 on the European Border and Coast Guard and the European Union’s external border management system (EU-2020)
Joint joint deployment of immigrants and asylum seekers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU/1624 on the joint deployment and coordination of immigration officers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a network of liaison officers (i.e. immigration officers) to help the EU to manage its external borders.
The purpose of the network is to:
ensure that liaison officers work closely with the competent authorities in the EU countries;
provide information and statistics on migration flows;
support the return operations of the EU border and coast guard.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to liaison officers deployed by EU countries to the external borders of non-European Union countries. It does not apply to the United Kingdom (1) or the United States of America (2).
EU countries may decide to extend the scope of the regulation to include liaison officers from non-european countries.
In addition, liaison officers may be deployed by countries other than EU countries. They must have particular consideration for vulnerable persons and take into account the gender dimension of migration flows. They should act accordingly when carrying out their tasks.
Organisation
The Steering Board of the Network of Immigration Liaison officers is composed of representatives of the Member States and is responsible for:
monitoring and coordinating the deployment of the liaison officers;
coordinating the work of the networks;
planning the activities of the liaisons;
identifying deployment gaps and identifying possibilities for deployment;
organising and coordinating activities;
assisting in the preparation of the biennial activity report, including the overview referred to in the second subparagraph of Article 4(1), to be prepared by the Chair;
drawing up the list of deployments of the national liaison officers.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 14 September 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â  Â
 			
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the website of the European Commission's Directorate-General for Migration, Refugees and Citizenship (Eurojust).
KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: a liaison officer whose role is to assist the competent national authorities of an EU country in their cooperation with other countries in the field of immigration.
* Non-EU country: any country outside the EU with which the EU has a bilateral or multiannual agreement on the return of migrants.

Summary 14:
Security standards for identity cards and residence documents for EU citizens and their family members when exercising their right to freedom of movement
Security requirements for identity documents and residence permits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — security standards for the security of identity cards issued by EU countries to their nationals and to residence documents issued by Member States to EU citizens
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
This regulation strengthens the security standards applicable to identity cards* issued by the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
identity cards issued to EU nationals when exercising the right to free movement;
residence permits issued to non-EU citizens when exercising that right.
EU countries must ensure that:
the identity card or residence document is produced by law;
the document is kept in a highly secure manner and only until the date of collection of the document and, in any case, no longer than 90 days from the date it is issued;
there is a contact point for issuing the identity card and residence document.
The card and the residence document must comply with the minimum security standards set out in the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO) document 9303 and must be accompanied by a functional MRZ (Magnetic Recognition Zone).
National authorities must:
ensure that the security features on the card are in line with the requirements laid down in the ICAO document;
issue the card in a secure manner;
keep the document for at least 90 days;
no longer than 30 days from its date of issue;
inform the contact point of the identity number and the number of persons who have been issued the identity cards or residence documents;
use the contact points in accordance with EU or national law.
In addition, the regulation sets out a uniform format for residence permits for third-country nationals (OJ L 286, 1.11.2018, pp. 1-3).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation applies from 2 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the security and transparency of its internal borders. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1030/2002 laying down uniform rules on the issuing of residence permits and identity cards;
implementing Directive 2014/54/EU on the exchange of information and the protection of personal data.
For more information, see:
‘The Schengen area’ on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Identity card: an identification document issued by an EU country to its nationals or to its non-nationals.
* Residence permit: a document issued to a non-European country to allow its nationals to live and work legally in the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 752/2019 of 3 August 2019 supplementing Council Regulation (Euratom) 2018/746 of 25 October 2018 on the security requirements for the issuing and circulation of identity card (EC), residence permit and contact point documents (EC).
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 15:
Interoperability between EU information systems for security, border and migration management
Interoperation between the EU information system for security and border and immigration management
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/817 on interoperability between the European information systems (EES, VIS, ETIAS, Eurodac and ECRIS-TCN)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes a European information system (EIS) for the Schengen area.
The regulation aims to improve the interoperability of the EIS, VIS and ETIAS with the aim of improving the security of the EU’s external borders.
KEY POINTS
The EIS consists of:
a central repository for reporting and statistics;
a European search portal;
an ability component.
EIS is designed to be interoperable with:
the European border management system (BMS);
the national border management systems (NMSs); and
the EU border control system (EU-CIS).
The interoperability consists of the following components:
an EIS central repository;
the ability component;
e-LISA.
Each of these components is managed by the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurodac) and the ETIAS Central Unit. It is responsible for:
keeping the database up-to-date;
ensuring that the information it contains is accurate;
providing the relevant information to border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities;
collecting, processing and storing the data for the purposes of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences;
assisting border guards and customs authorities in their work.
In addition, the CIS central unit must:
ensure that the data it collects and stores are accurate and reliable;
make available to the relevant national authorities the information they need to prevent, detect or investigate terrorist offences; and
provide the competent national authorities with the necessary information to carry out their tasks.
EU-CRIS
The ETIAS central unit:
provides the relevant data to the European border control systems;
supports the development of the interoperable EIS;
is responsible for ensuring that the EES central repository and the ability component are interoperable;
cooperates with the national border and coast guard systems.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
They apply from 12 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve interoperability in the area of information systems. These include:
establishing a European Information System for Security and Border Management;
developing an interoperable European search engine;
implementing a common set of interoperability requirements for EIS.
For more information, see:
European Information System (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interoperability: the exchange of information between different information systems, such as the European Union Information System, the European Identification System and the European Central Identification System.
* Identifier: a unique identifier that is used to identify a person or a group of persons.

Summary 16:
Energy efficiency and greenhouse gas emissions saving in buildings: energy efficiency and renewable electricity (REFERENCES
Energy efficiency requirements for buildings
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/852 — energy efficiency requirements in buildings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the efficiency and environmental performance of buildings using renewable energy.
It recasts and repeals Directive 2012/27/EU on energy efficiency.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
new and existing buildings subject to the use of renewable electricity in buildings;
existing buildings subject in renewable electricity;
energy-only-installations;
cogeneration installations;
electricity-only installations.
Sustainability and emissions-saving criteria
The legislation applies to installations with a total rated thermal input of at least 1 kW (kilowatt-hour) and a total rating of at or above 1 MW.
Building installations must comply with the following:
energy efficiency requirements;
carbon capture and storage (CCS) requirements.
EU countries may apply the sustainability and emission-saving criteria to installations that have a lower total thermal input than those for installations with higher rated thermal inputs.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 5 years on the implementation of the directive. This report must include:
the number of installations covered by the directive;
the total energy efficiency* and CO2 emissions* saving* requirements; and
the greenhouse gas (GHGCO2) emissions* reductions.
the carbon emissions* reduction targets for buildings covered by this directive.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report on the results of the assessment carried out by EU countries on the application of the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world’s leading exporter of renewable energy and the largest market for renewable energy in the world. This is why it is important to ensure that buildings in the EU have the best possible environmental performance.
For more information, see:
Building energy efficiency (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Renewable electricity: electricity generated from renewable sources such as wind, solar and geothermal sources.
* CO2: the greenhouse gas emitted by the combustion of fossil fuels in the final energy commodity, i.e. the total amount of carbon dioxide (CO2).
* Energy efficiency: the amount of energy that can be used to heat, cool, transport, heat, cook, cook and cool a building.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU)* 2017/884 of 21 December 2017 on energy requirements for building installations subject to renewable electricity and repealing Directives 2012/28/EU and 2013/13/EU (recast) (OJ L 347, 28.12.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 17:
Radio spectrum: 5G: harmonised conditions for radio spectrum use and use-by-contracts (5G)
Radio spectrum for 5G
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/899 on radio spectrum
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to harmonise conditions for the use of radio spectrum in order to ensure optimal and efficient use of the radio spectrum to enable the development of 5G.
It recasts and repeals Directives 2002/58/EC and 2002/60/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
radio spectrum;
access to numbers and services;
information and communication technologies (ICTs);
mobile phones;
mobile broadband;
5G infrastructure;
infrastructure for interconnecting fixed-line and mobile broadband networks;
telecommunications infrastructure for mobile broadband.
The regulation applies to the following radio spectrum bands: the 3G, 4G, 5G and 4G bands, as well as the 5G band, which have specific qualities, in terms of coverage and data capacity, which allow them to be combined appropriately to meet 5G requirements.
National regulatory authorities
National authorities must:
apply conditions to individual rights of use for spectrum in accordance with Article 13(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU);
ensure that radio spectrum is allocated according to the requirements of the EU’s 5G legislation;
apply the conditions set out in Annex II to the rules on spectrum allocation;
4G and 5G to ensure that the spectrum allocated to 5G is used correctly and efficiently;
use the spectrum in a way that ensures the optimal and most effective use of spectrum.
Regulatory authorities must apply the conditions laid down in Annex III of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2018.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EU):
1.1.2018
-
OJ L 94 of 1.1 January 2018
RELATED ACTS
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 of 1 December 2018 laying down technical implementing measures for the harmonisation of conditions for use and allocation of spectrum in 5G (OJ C 202 of 31.12.2018).
Regulating Regulation (EC) No 676/2002/EC of the EC establishing a European Radio Spectrum Management Agency (ERMAA).
WHAT DO THE DIRECTIVES DO?
They set out rules on:
requirements for:
the use of frequencies;
the allocation of frequencies and the allocation of capacity;
conditions for the identification of transnational demand (i.e. the amount of spectrum needed to cover a given market);
the identification of pre-paid and post-paid services.
They also set out the conditions on the use, allocation and interconnection of mobile networks.
These rules are designed to ensure:
a high level of consumer protection;
a level playing field for mobile operators;
fair and transparent competition;
harmonised conditions on access to numbers, services and infrastructure, including in relation to premium rate services and other services subject to particular pricing conditions.
In addition, the directive sets out:
rules on the identification and interconnectivity of mobile network infrastructure, such as the availability of mobile phones, and the availability and use of mobile broadband, and on the provision of information and communication technology;
rules for the application of the 4G and 3G standards;
requests on the availability, availability, interconnection and availability of spectrum for mobile networks, including for mobile phones and for pre-pay services; and
rules concerning access to mobile networks and infrastructure.
EU countries must ensure that:
mobile operators have the right to use the frequencies allocated to them and to interconnect them in a manner that minimises interference with other mobile networks in their territory;
all mobile operators have access to the frequencies and capacity required to meet the requirements;
any mobile operator that has significant market power obligations for access or interconnection other than those listed in Articles 69 to 74 and Articles 76 and 80, it submit a request to the Commission for an exemption from these conditions. The Commission then assesses whether the exemption is appropriate.
If the Commission considers that a mobile operator does not meet these conditions, it must inform the national regulatory authority of its decision. The national authority must then inform the European Commission of its opinion and inform the Commission of the reasons for its decision, and, if necessary, issue an opinion on the validity of the exemption.
Amendments to the directive
The following amendments to the original directive have been incorporated into the original text.
Directives (EU), Euratom and Euratom have been recast in the light of technological progress and the need to modernise the regulation.



Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of data on CO2 emissions from light-duty vehicles (EU ETS)
Monitoring, reporting and collecting data on carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions from new-vehicle aerodynamic performance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 510/2011 — monitoring and reporting data on greenhouse gas emission allowances from new passenger cars and light commercial vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to monitor and report data on the greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions emitted by new vehicles.
It sets out the rules for the exchange of these data between the European Commission, the European Environment Agency (EEA) and manufacturers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation:
sets out the requirements for the EU ETS (the European Union’s system for greenhouse gas emissions trading) and for the non-ETS sectors;
sets emission performance standards for new cars, trucks and buses, as part of the integrated approach to reduce CO2 emission from light vehicles;
requires manufacturers to report to the Commission on the performance of their vehicles and to the EEA on their CO2 performance.
The data must be transmitted in accordance with Article 5(1) by the contact point to the manufacturer via electronic data transfer to the Business Data Rep managed by the Commission.
EU Member States must designate the competent authorities responsible for the reporting and monitoring of data.
Member States must ensure that the data collected are accurate, reliable and up-to-date. They must also ensure that they are made available to the public in a range format in order to take account of considerations of fair competition.
Manufacturers are responsible for verifying and correcting the final data they transmit.
They must also inform the Commission of any discrepancies in the data they send.
If a manufacturer fails to comply with the requirements of the regulation, it may be subject to fines. Those fines should be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
In addition, Member States are required to ensure that:
the data transmitted by the manufacturer and the Member States’ competent authorities are recorded in a publicly accessible database;
the Commission has the means to verify and, where necessary, correct the data it receives;
all Member States and manufacturers have the right to access the final database on behalf of the Commission;
any Member State that does not comply with this regulation may impose sanctions on the manufacturer or the Member State in which the vehicle is registered.
Report
The Member States have to send the Commission a report every 3 years on the number of vehicles registered in their territory and the total number of emissions they have recorded. The report must include the number and type of vehicle registered in each Member State, as well as the type and number of CO2 allowances issued. It must also include information on the uptake of new and advanced CO2 reducing technologies, and of alternative powertrains.
Reporting
The report must be sent to the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW), which is responsible for:
monitoring the data transmitted;
checking the accuracy and reliability of the data;
assisting the Commission with the verification procedure;
keeping a database of the information transmitted.

Summary 19:
Security of gas supply in the EU: emergency preparedness and solidarity mechanism (SPSM)
Security of the EU’s gas supply: emergency plans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 on the security of gas security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU-wide approach to gas security.
It sets out the responsibilities of the European Commission, the European Energy Community (EEC) Member States and the Member States’ national regulatory authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
gas supply and infrastructure disruption scenarios;
emergency preparedness measures;
security of the gas system;
measures to be taken by Member States in the event of a gas crisis.
EU countries’ responsibilities
Member States are responsible for:
establishing a gas security plan and an emergency plan;
implementing a preventive action plan and emergency plan.
Implementing the emergency plan
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up and implementing the emergency and preventive action plans. These must be in line with EU law and must include:
a description of the risks that could lead to a crisis;
the measures that Member States are required to take in case of an emergency;
an assessment of the impact of the emergency on the economy, the economy and the energy sector;
a list of emergency measures to be implemented.
National authorities must:
ensure that:
the competent authorities, the national regulatory authority and the national transmission system operators;
have the necessary technical, legal and financial arrangements in place;
inform the public of the measures they have taken;
identify the risk factors that could have a negative impact;
provide the public with all necessary information for the common and national risk assessments.
In addition, the Commission:
draw up a permanent reserve list for a monitoring task force consisting of industry experts and representatives of the Commission;
develops and implements the emergency response plan and the preventive actions;
draws up and implements emergency response plans;
ensures that the Commission has the power to issue delegated acts to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 652/2013.
The Commission must ensure that the measures are in place which are in conformity with the EU law, in particular with fundamental rights. These measures must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. The Commission must also ensure that: the measures taken are proportionate, dissuasive, effective and effective;the measures are effective and proportionate in the case of a natural gas crisis;the emergency response measures are coordinated with other Member States, including the European Network of Transmission System Operators (ENTSOG) and the European Gas Grid Management Group (EGG).
The emergency plan must be updated every 5 years. It must include a description of:
measures taken by the EU and its Member States for the prevention and management of a crisis, including:
preventive measures in the form of emergency plans and emergency response actions,
border measures,
measures for the management of natural gas infrastructure, and
measures that are required for the emergency management of the electricity sector.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
Energy security in the European Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Emergency preparedness: measures taken to ensure the safety, security and reliability of the internal gas market.
* Security of gas: measures that can be taken to protect the supply of gas to the EU.

Summary 20:
Rescaled labels for energy-related products
Rescued labels for Energy-related product
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) No 812/2013 supplementing Directive 2010/30/EU with regard to the energy labelling of water heaters, hot water storage tanks and packages of water heater and solar device
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  It aims to improve the visibility of energy-efficient products on the EU’s market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
water heaters;
hot-water storage tanks;
packages of solar devices;
water heater and package of solar device;
waste heaters.
It does not apply to the following products:
gas turbines;
gas-operated compressors;
electricity-operated boilers; and
gas generators.
EU countries may adopt delegated acts for each specific product group.
Labels
The energy label must contain:
the energy efficiency rating of the model;
the name of the supplier;
a description of the product and its characteristics.
The label must be clearly visible and clearly legible. It must also include a link to the product information database.
A dealer must indicate in the database when it no longer places on the market units of a model. The dealer must not display the rescaled labels before that date.
National authorities must ensure that dealers comply with the regulation.
If a dealer fails to comply, the dealer may be subject to a penalty.
Exemptions
The delegated regulation allows the Commission to adopt a delegated act for products which are not covered by the delegated regulation. The delegated act may be amended by delegated acts adopted pursuant to Article 10 of Directive 2010 or by Directive 96/60/EC, where the Commission adopts delegated acts pursuant to this regulation. It may also be repealed by delegated act.
In addition, the delegated act sets out a list of products for which an energy label could be established.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DELEGATION APPLY?
It has applied since 18 February 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 February 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Energy labelling’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data in the field of research
Protecting the personal data of the population
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the protection of fundamental rights and freedoms vis-à-vis the processing of personal data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that personal data are processed in a way that respects the fundamental rights of the data subjects.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the processing of data on the basis of a person’s consent;
the collection, storage, processing, storage and dissemination of data for the purposes of research;
processing of the results obtained from the processing.
It does not apply to personal data collected by the European Commission or the European Union Agency for Fundamental Rights (Euratom).
Processing
The purpose of the regulation is to protect the rights of persons who have the right to their personal data to be processed and to have their data processed in accordance with the law of the EU country concerned.
The processing must be lawful and fair. It must respect the rights and fundamental freedoms of the persons concerned, including:
their privacy and confidentiality;
their right to access and use their data; and
the rights of natural persons, such as the right of access to information and to be informed about their rights.
Data protection
The data must be collected and processed in line with the following principles:
personal data should be collected with the consent of the person concerned;
they should be kept for a minimum of 5 years;
data should not be used for any purpose other than the purpose for which they were collected;
there should be a clear distinction between personal data and non-personal data, i.e. data collected for the purpose of analysing, analysing and disseminating research results;
all personal data should only be processed if they are necessary for the proper functioning of the research programme.
Personal data must not be processed without prior consent from the data subject.
Collection and processing
The collection and processing should be carried out in a manner that is proportionate and fair to the data concerned. It should be based on the best interests of the subjects concerned and should not violate their rights or the confidentiality of their data.
Any processing must respect:
human rights and privacy;
human dignity;
personal security;
natural persons’ rights;
information security.
In addition, the regulation requires that:
data processing must comply with:
appropriate data protection policies and procedures;
appropriate security measures;
adequate data protection measures and procedures for processing;
privacy and security measures.
EU countries must ensure that the processing and storage of data does not violate the rights or privacy of the individuals concerned. This includes:
protecting the confidentiality and integrity of the information;
ensuring that the data are not used for purposes other than those for which it is necessary;
taking appropriate measures to prevent, investigate and investigate the misuse of the collected data;
establishing appropriate data protection procedures and procedures.
Implementing the regulation
The European Commission must establish a European Data Protection Agency (EDA). It is responsible for:
monitoring the application of this regulation;
providing the competent national authorities with information on the implementation of the regulations;
assisting them in their work;
developing and implementing data protection and data security policies;
conducting training and training activities;
implementing data protection training;
creating and maintaining a database of data subjects;
designating a designated representative to ensure compliance with the regulation.
Evaluation
The EDA must be reviewed every 5 years. The EDA is composed of representatives of EU countries and is chaired by the Commission. It is assisted by a supervisory authority, which must:
report to the EDA every 3 years on its implementation;
report annually to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the European Committee on the Protection of the Fundamental Rights of the People and the Committee on Civil Society;
make recommendations to the Commission on the adequacy of data protection;
draw up a report on the application and application of the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect personal data. These include:
Directive 2002/58/EC on data protection (see summary);
Council Regulation (EC) No 696/2003 on the processing, storing and dissemination in the fields of research and development of data (See summary); and
Directives 2002/54/EC and 2002/57/EC establishing the European Network of Data Protection Authorities.
For more information, see:
‘Data protection’ on the EEA website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Harmonomically-based indicator of price stability in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2646/98 — Harmonized Index of Consumer Prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Harmonized index of Consumer prices*, known as the HICP.
It aims to improve the comparability of EU countries’ national statistics with those of the United Nations (UN).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers the following categories of goods and services:
food and non-food products;
other services;
fees and service charges of brokers, investment counsellors and other services; and
other goods.
EU countries must ensure the quality of the harmonised indices provided.
National authorities must ensure that:
the harmonised data are produced in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex I of the regulation;
the data are collected and transmitted in a consistent and consistent manner.
The Commission must prepare a report on the suitability of the OOH price index for integration into the HCP coverage by 31 December 2018. Depending on the results of that report, the Commission should submit, within a reasonable time frame, a proposal for amending this regulation with regard to integrating the OH price index into the coverage.
Delegated acts
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts for the amendment of Annex I, in order to ensure comparability at international level. These delegated acts must be adopted by the Commission within 6 months of the adoption of the implementing acts.
Deadlines, exchange standards and revisions
The implementing acts must comply with the following deadlines and exchange standards:
1.1.2019: the date on which the Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council on its suitability for integration in the Harmonised index;
2.2.2023: the deadline for the Commission to submit a proposal to amend this regulation.

FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 1998.
BACKGROUND

For more information, see:
‘The Harmonized Indices’ on the European Commission’s website.

* Non-food product: a product which is not subject to taxes. It does not include:
alcohol;

Summary 23:
Anti-dumping investigation
Anti-Dumping Investigation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the application of the principle of dumping to the EU’s internal market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the investigation of dumping* in the internal market.
It lays down rules on the investigation and application of duties on imports of certain products from non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
all imports of goods originating from outside the EU;
all products originating in the EU, including:
processed agricultural products;
seeds, feed and feed additives;
plastics;
motor vehicles;
electrical equipment;
cable and radio equipment.
The investigation procedure
An EU country may initiate an investigation on the basis of:
evidence of dumping and of resultant injury to the Union industry;
the existence of a particular market situation for the product concerned within the meaning of the disclosure rights of the parties concerned.
An investigation may also be launched at the request of an EU country or any interested party.
EU countries must forward the request to the Commission forthwith. The Commission may decide to open an investigation if it considers that there is sufficient evidence of dumping or of damage to EU industry.
Importers and importers must inform the Commission of the reasons for their decision to launch an investigation.
In the absence of any complaint, a EU country is in possession of sufficient evidence to demonstrate that dumping is likely to have a detrimental effect on the EU industry, and must immediately communicate such evidence to the European Commission.
If the Commission considers that a complaint has been made, it must promptly communicate it to the other EU countries and any interested parties.
Procedures
The Commission may launch investigations on the grounds of the following:
the presence of dumping in the market;
excessive dumping margins;
a lack of conformity with EU rules on dumping margins.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The Regulation entered into force on 1 July 2004.
BACKGROUND
 
Anti dumping investigations
The European Commission has adopted a number of rules to combat dumping. These include:
rules on the procedure for initiating an investigation;
rules for the implementation of investigations;
requests for confidentiality.
For more information, see:
‘Dumping investigation’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 24:
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on subsidisation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the EU’s financial interests by:
ensuring fair competition in the agricultural sector;
protecting consumers’ rights;
promoting the sustainable development of the European Union (EU).
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2002 on subsidising agricultural products.
KEY POINTS
   
The regulation applies to all agricultural products, including:
grants of subsidies;
recipients of subsidies.
It does not apply to:
exports of agricultural products to non-EU countries;
suppliers’ claims for export performance;
the granting of a subsidy, without having been made a designated geographical region within the jurisdiction of the granting authority.
EU countries must ensure that:
the rules on the calculation of subsidies are based on the best available information;
there is no distortion of competition;
subsidies are not dependent on export performance.
The European Commission may, on a case-by-case basis, request EU countries to supply information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions. The Commission may also request information from the European Agricultural Committee (EAC).
The Commission may request the EAC to provide information necessary to monitor efficiently the application of measures.
In addition, the Commission may ask EU countries’ competent authorities to supply the information it needs.
to supply information it requires.
Information received pursuant to this regulation must be used only for the purpose for which it was requested.
Duties may be imposed by the Commission only for a 4-month period and may not be imposed for more than 4 months.
Imposition of duties may be subject to certain conditions, such as the following:
a duty must be imposed on imports originating in the EU;
a tariff must not exceed 3% of the value of the goods imported;
an import duty must not be more than 3 % of the total value of goods imported.
A duty may be suspended if the facts demonstrate that a subsidy has been granted.
An investigation must cover both subsidisation and injury, and they must be investigated simultaneously.
If the Commission considers that the subsidy has not been properly applied, it may launch an investigation into the case. The investigation must be open to all interested parties, including the EU countries and the European Commission.
Procedures for investigations
An EU country may submit a request for an investigation to the Commission. The request must be submitted within the time limits set out in the regulation, and must include the reasons, in terms of the EU interest, why the parties should be heard.
Reviews
An annual review of the regulation is conducted by a committee composed of representatives of the Commission and chaired by the Council. The committee is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation of the regulations;
assisting the Commission in implementing the regulation;
reviewing the effectiveness of the measures it has put in place;
examining the rules and procedures it has adopted;
checking that the Commission has applied the regulation correctly.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The EEC has been amended several times, most recently in 2013.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable development and food security’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Subsidies: a type of economic policy that aims to improve the quality of food and feed for consumers.
* Importers’: any person, company or organisation that receives a subsidy from the EU.

Entry into force - Date of expiry

investment priorities;
targeted measures to be put in place.

the Commission may suspend or terminate the agreement without prejudice to Article 14(3) of the Financial Regulation.
Financing not linked to costs should apply at the level of payments from the Commission to EU countries as beneficiaries, irrespective of the reimbursement in any form of financial contributions from Member States to final recipients.
