Summary 26:
EU-wide coordination of economic policies: recovery and resilience plans
EU–wide coordination on economic policies
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/241 — EU-wide cooperation and support plans
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the coordination of EU countries’ economic policies in the event of a severe economic crisis.
It sets out the general objective and specific objectives of the instrument.
KEY POINTS
The objective of this instrument is to:
ensure that EU countries are able to prepare and implement economic policies that take into account the needs of the population and the economy;
contribute to the recovery of the economy and to the economic and social cohesion of the European Union (EU);
provide technical support to EU countries in case of a crisis;
support the development and implementation of recovery plans and recovery strategies.
The objectives are:
to:
prevent and reduce the impact of the crisis on the social and health-related consequences for EU countries as well as to mitigate its social and economic fallout.
To achieve these objectives, the EU countries should:
prepare and implement recovery plans;
prepart recovery strategies;
develop and implement support measures;
cooperate with other relevant international organisations;
coordinate their economic policies.
Support measures
EU countries must:
make the necessary financial resources available to the European Commission;
organise calls for technical support;
make use of the communication technologies;
the Commission should organise additional dedicated calls in response to specific to specific emerging needs;
needs;
implement special measures.
Special measures
The Commission may adopt special measures for a limited period. These special measures may be adopted at national, regional or local level, where appropriate also at national and regional level.
Cooperation and support
The instrument provides for:
a coordination and support plan for each EU country;
special measures adopted pursuant to Article 12(7);
the implementation of support measures at national or regional level; and
the communication activities carried out by the Commission.
Monitoring of implementation
The EU country concerned must submit a report to the Commission every 3 years on the implementation of the instruments. The report must include:
the number and duration of the special measures adopted;
a summary of the measures adopted and the results of the monitoring and evaluation carried out;
an evaluation of the implementation and the impact on the economy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The crisis in the euro area has had a significant impact on EU countries and has led to a deterioration in the quality of living standards of many of them. It has also had a negative impact on social cohesion.
For more information, see:
‘Recovery and Resilience Plans’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Economic crisis: a crisis in which a country’s economy is in a state of crisis and there is a significant increase in unemployment.
* Recovery and resilience plan: an action plan designed to help the economy recover.

Summary 27:
Railway passenger rights: uniform rules for the carriage of passengers and their luggage (recast)
Railway passengers rights: common rules for passenger rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 — uniform rules on passenger rights and carriage of goods and passengers on the railway
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve rail passenger services within the European Union (EU).
It sets out uniform rules to ensure that passengers are entitled to fair and timely compensation for the loss or damage caused to them by the railway.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the carriage of persons and goods by rail;
the passenger’s right of access to the rail carriage;
access to the railway carriage and the right of carriage of their luggage.
It does not apply to the carriage by road of goods, goods or passengers.
Railways must:
ensure that:
passengers are informed of the conditions under which they can use the railway;
have the right to use the train;
are informed of any changes in the timetable;
make sure that the train is equipped with an adequate number of places for bicycles;
provide passengers with the necessary information about the train and the carriage conditions.
Passengers’ rights
In addition to the above, the regulation sets out rules on:
conditions under which railway undertakings are obliged to pay compensation for:
damage caused to passengers’ personal effects;
damage to their luggage;
loss or damage to their personal effects.
the amount of damages to the goods and personal effects that a passenger is entitled to receive;
measures to prevent or reduce the risk of damage to the personal effects of a passenger.
EU countries must ensure:
that:
railways have a system of complaint-handling procedures to deal with complaints;
there is a system for the exchange of information between the railway company and the passengers;
that passengers can lodge complaints with the competent national enforcement bodies.
Complaints must be handled in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
The railway company must ensure that it:
provides passengers with information on the conditions of use of the railway system;
ensures that passengers have access to a complaint procedure;
has the means to lodge a complaint with the relevant national enforcement body;
makes available to passengers all the information they need to make a complaint, including the number and type of items they wish to complain about;
supports the implementation of the rules and measures set out in the annex to the regulation;
is responsible for the collection, processing and dissemination of information on complaints.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The original regulation had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2008. It had to be amended by Regulation (EU) 2015/849 in order to incorporate the changes introduced in the new rules.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of its rail system. These include:
rules on the carriage and use of goods;
rules to improve passenger information;
requests for railway companies to provide passengers with more information about their rights and obligations.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 28:
EU external assistance for the Western Balkans and Turkey (IPA III) 2021-2027
EU external aid for the western Balkans and Turkish (IPAA III)
The European Union (EU) is providing financial assistance to the Western Balkan countries and Turkey for the period between 2021 and 2027.
ACT
Council Decision 2021/1059/EU of 7 September 2021 establishing a framework for planning and delivering external assistance under the IPA III programme.
SUMMARY
The IPA III is the third phase of the European Union’s external assistance programme. It aims to support the development of economic, social and territorial stability in the Western and Eastern Balkans (Western Balkans) and Turkey.
The programme is funded through the European Fund for Sustainable Development Plus (EFSD+) and the External Action Guarantee (EAG).
The beneficiaries of the programme are:
the Western Balkans (Serbia, Montenegro, Kosovo, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Croatia, Macedonia and Montenegro);
Turkey (Slovenia, Serbia, Kosovo and Macedonia);
the Republic of Macedonia (Sri Lanka);
Romania (Romania, Bulgaria, Greece, Hungary, Romania, Serbia and Turkey).
EU funding
The budget for the programme is €1.8 billion, which is divided into 3 tranches:
€1.5 billion for the first three budgetary commitments (1.3.2021, 1.4.2022 and 1.5.2027);
€2.2 billion from the EAG for the fourth tranche (2.1.2023 and 2.2.2025).
In addition, the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the World Bank (WB) will provide financial support.
In line with the principles of the United Nations Charter and international law, the beneficiaries of EU assistance must respect human rights’ rights and international humanitarian law. The European Commission will monitor this closely, including at local level.
Implementing measures and methods
EU actions under IPA III must be consistent with the principle of ‘do no harm’ and should comply with EU taxonomy to the extent possible, in particular to ensure the sustainability of investments.
EU assistance must be based on the following principles:
promotion of democracy and human rights;
respect for the rule of law;
equality of opportunity;
protection of minorities;
human rights and fundamental freedoms;
integration of women into society;
environmental protection;
sustainable development;
economic and social development.
Eligible beneficiaries
The following beneficiaries are eligible for EU funding:
The Western Balkans:
Serbia;
Bulgaria;
Greece;
Macedonian Republic;
Serbian Federation;
Yugoslavia; and
Turkey.

Summary 29:
EU external assistance instruments
EU external aid instruments
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2017/852 establishing a European External Action Guarantee
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It establishes a European Union external assistance instrument (EAGI).
The EAGI aims to contribute to a coordinated, holistic and structured approach to migration, maximising synergies and applying the necessary leverage.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The aim of the instrument is to:
contribute to the EU’s migration policy;
promote economic and social development in partner countries;
support the sustainable development of partner countries’ economies;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice on migration and border management;
provide a coherent framework for cooperation between rapid response actions and geographic programmes.
Programming
The instrument is funded by the European Union (EU).
It is financed by the EU budget, which is the main source of funding for overseas countries and territories.
Eligible countries
The European Commission must establish a checklist of the eligibility criteria as set out in Article 31, Article 35(1 and (2) and (3) of the IPA III Regulation.
The Commission must assess and select proposals to be supported by the EOGI against this checklist, on the basis of the information provided by the eligible counterparts.
Projects must comply with the following criteria:
they must:
be based on the criteria set out by the Commission;
have a minimum budget of €500,000;
at least €250,000 for each project;
be implemented within the EU;
contain at least one project in each EU country and territories;
not be funded by any other EU financial instrument;
meet the criteria laid down in Regulation (EEC) No 596/2013 on the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
be financed from the budget of the European Investment Bank (EIB).
Programmes
The programme must be based on:
programming documents for regional programmes;
programmes for geographic programmes; and
programme documents for specific projects.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Instrument entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
  The EAGA is one of a number of EU instruments designed to help developing countries. The other instruments are:
the European Investment Fund (EIF);
the Fund for Rural Development (FID); and
the EU Development Fund.
For more information, see:
External assistance instruments (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Implementing Decision 2009/973/EC of 27 June 2009 establishing a framework for the European External Assistance Instrument (EOGI) (OJ L 94, 1.7.2009, pp. 1-8)

Summary 30:
Whistleblower protection: EU rules on the protection of workers’ rights and the environment
WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It applies to workers who report breaches of EU law that are harmful to the public interest.
ACT
Directive 2014/24/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 November 2014 on the Protection of workers' rights and environmental protection in the field of public procurement.
SUMMARY
   
This directive aims to protect workers who have been victims of breaches of the EU’s public procurement rules.
It does not apply to:
employers’ organisations;
contractors’ bodies; or
the European Commission.
Scope
The directive applies to the following sectors:
the supply of products or the provision of services;
the transport of goods and services; and
the environmental protection of products and services.
EU countries may decide to exclude from the scope of the directive:
transport;
health and safety;
environmental protection;
marine transport;
information exchange;
public procurement;
social security.
Reporting
The report must be made available to the competent authorities in the EU country where it is received.
The competent authorities must:
ensure that the report is made available in a transparent, easily understandable and reliable manner;
make copies of the report;
notify the person who made the report and inform them of the reasons for their decision;
keep the report for at least 2 years;
use the information they have received;
2 years from the date of receipt;
report to the results of the investigation;
inform the European Commission of the decision-making process and the reasons that led to the decision to reject the report.
In addition, EU countries may exclude from protection the following-up of a report:
information that is already fully available in the public domain or unsubstantiated rumours and hearsay;
personal conflicts between the reporting person and the employer;
confidentiality of the information;
disclosure of the identity of the person concerned;
any other information that is confidential or hearsay.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management agency (EUROSUR)
European integrated Border Management Agency (EUBER)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 establishing the European Union Border and Coast Guard Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Agency for the Protection of the Union’s (EurosUR) external borders.
It sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the agency.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The agency is responsible for the protection of the external borders of the EU and the Schengen area. It is based in Luxembourg and has its seat in Luxembourg.
Its tasks include:
exchanging information with other EU countries or non-EU countries at external borders;
monitoring and analysing migration flows;
facilitating the exchange of information with the law enforcement authorities of the Member States, Europol or Eurojust;
operating the FADO system;
providing technical assistance to the Rapid Response Pool (RRP Pool) and the Rapid Reaction Mechanism (RPM);
assisting the competent bodies of EU countries in border management;
ensuring that EU countries’ competent bodies carry out forced-return monitoring activities.
EU countries contribute to the RRP Pool and the rapid reaction pool.
The RPM Pool consists of:
a pool of fundamental rights monitors from competent bodies from EU countries who carry out Forced-Return Monitoring activities;
a standing corps of technical crews that are part of the standing corps;
an operational staff deployed from the EU countries.
A rapid response pool consisting of operational staff from the European Defence Agency (EDA).
The Rapid Response Mechanism consists of a rapid response team composed of personnel from each EU country. The RPM is composed of a standing corps and a rapid reaction team.
RPM teams are:
based in Luxembourg;
cooperated with the competent national border management bodies of the participating EU countries;
maintained by the competent body of each participating EU country;
operated by the technical crews based in each participating country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION
The regulation applies from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Agency was set up following the creation of the European Integrated Border Management Network (EATMN) in 2014.
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Forced-return monitors: people who are forced to return to their country of origin.

Summary 32:
Information and communications technology (ICT) cybersecurity: European Network of Information Security Assurance Institutions (ENISA) (European cybersecurity certification body)
Information and communication technology: European network of information security Assurance Institutes (ENCI)*
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/1148 establishing a network of cybersecurity certification bodies for information and communication technologies (ICTs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes the network of national cybersecurity certification authorities (NICs) known as ENISA.
It sets up the network to:
ensure that cybersecurity certification schemes and certificates for ICT products, ICT services and ICT processes meet the requirements set out in the EU’s cybersecurity legislation;
provide information on cybersecurity incident response teams network (CSIRTs network) and the cybersecurity certification authority (ICCAs);
ensuring that cybersecurity incidents are quickly and effectively dealt with.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all products and services covered by the EU cybersecurity legislation, including:
products and services which are:
connected to the internet;
computers and mobile phones;
internet-based services;
information and communication networks;
mobile applications;
computer security incident response systems.
The legislation does not apply to: products, services and processes which are not connected to the Internet;
services that are not designed and built on the internet.
Ensuring interoperability between the EU and non-EU countries
EU countries must ensure that:
there is a high level of interoperability and interoperability of their cybersecurity certification systems;
there are interoperability with other EU countries’ cybersecurity legislation.
There is a clear distinction between cybersecurity* and cybersecurity* standards, such as those of the European Union (EU).
There are 2 types of cybersecurity standards:
Cybersecurity standards: cybersecurity standards for cybersecurity* products;
cybersecurity certification* for cybersecurity services.
ENISA is responsible for:
developing and maintaining its expertise on cybersecurity certification with a view to supporting the EU policy in that area;
advising EU countries and the private sector on cybersecurity;
assisting the European Commission and the EU countries with which the EU has concluded agreements on cybersecurity.
Role of ENISA
The organisation is managed by a management board and an advisory group. The Executive Director is appointed by the Commission and is independent of the management board. The board meets at least once every 3 months. The executive director is assisted by a committee. It is composed of:
seconded national experts and other staff;
members of the Management Board;
advisory group members;
third-party experts;
an advisory group comprising experts from the private and public sectors.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 establishing a European system of national income
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the transmission of data to the European Commission (Eurostat) on the European System of Gross National Income*.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out rules for:
the transmission of GNI aggregates and their components to Eurostat;
the verification of the quality of the GNI data and of the EU’s statistical system of accounts (ESA 2010);
the exchange of data between Eurostat and national statistical authorities of other EU countries.
It establishes a system of statistical confidentiality for the data transmitted by Eurostat to the statistical office of the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC).

carry out GNI information visits in order to verify the quality, reliability and exhaustiveness of the aggregates, their components and the statistical system;
keep an inventory of the collected data;
provide the ESSC with an inventory guide to ensure that it complies with ESA 2010;
assist the Commission in the preparation of delegated acts;
ensure that ESA 2010 is correctly implemented.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units
EU type of approval
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 168/2013 on the granting and placing on the European Union market of vehicles and systems, systems and components based on the same type as those of the United Kingdom
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the granting of EU type–approval* of vehicles*, systems and component* systems* based on a type approved by the EU’s single market, the EU.
It establishes special rules to ensure that EU type‑approval rules are applied to vehicles* and systems*, components*, separate technical* units* that have been previously approved in the UK.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles and systems* of the type covered by EU type‐approval;
separate technical units based on that type;
systems, components or separate technical unit based on other types and already placed on the EU market;
vehicle, systems or separate parts of a vehicle that have previously been approved by a single EU authority.
Manufacturers must apply to the European Commission for EU type approval.
The Commission decides on the basis of a proposal submitted by the manufacturer.
If the Commission accepts the proposal, the vehicle, system or component manufacturer must submit a type‐certificate to the competent authority of the EU country of origin. The type-certificate must contain the following information:
the type of vehicle;
the number of vehicles;
a description of the vehicle and the type of the system or components;
type of the component;
number of components; and
the total number of parts of the components.
Where the Commission considers that a vehicle or system, system, component or separate part does not comply with the requirements of the regulation, it may issue a type‑certificate that states that the vehicle or component does not conform with the regulation.
When the Commission decides that the type is no longer conforms with the regulations of the Member States, it must issue the type‐conditions for its withdrawal from the EU, including:
a type‐notification of invalidity;
recall, repair and maintenance information and in‐in-service conformity checks;
instructions for the maintenance of the vehicles and components concerned;
requests for information on the safety and environmental performance of the systems or components concerned.
In addition, the Commission must:
take over the responsibilities of the national authority that is to approve types previously approved for use in the EU;
make available on the market, registration, sale or entry into service of end-of series vehicles conforming to a type whose UK type‐authorisation has become invalid before the date on which EU type-approved legislation ceases to apply to and in the Member State where it was granted;
ensure that the manufacturer complies with the conditions laid down in the regulation;
apply for a type–certificate for the same vehicle or components that are already on the Union market.
Application for a Union type‐permanent type‐conformity mark
The manufacturer must apply for the type‑conditions set out in this regulation. The manufacturer must provide the Commission with:
information on the type–conditions of use, including the number and type of components, the type-number of parts and the number of separate parts, and the total number and number of components of the separate parts and separate parts;
information about the number, type, number and types of parts, components, separate parts or separate components, including a description of each component and of the parts and components, as well as of the different types of systems and separate units;
specifications for the safety, environmental performance and in-service compliance of the products and components and the information about the components and components. The Commission must also provide the manufacturer with information on:
safety and environmental compliance;
environmental performance;
compliance with the safety requirements;
safety requirements for the components, parts, separate components and subsystems;
technical requirements for systems, separate technicalunits;
conditions relating to the maintenance and in–service conformity check.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The Regulation applies from 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a single market. It is governed by the Treaty on European Union (TEU) and its annexes.
For more information, see:
‘EU type’ on Eurostat’.

Summary 35:
Europol: rules on the processing of personal data and the exchange of information with non-EU countries and international organisations (Decision 2009/371/JHA on the establishment of a European Police Office (Europol Convention)
Europol : rules on data processing and the sharing of information between the EU’s law enforcement agencies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 — rules on Europol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on how the European Union (EU) law enforcement agency Europol:
processes and exchanges personal data;
exchanges and exchanges information with other EU bodies, authorities of third countries, international organisations and non-governmental organisations (NGOs) to the extent necessary for the performance of its tasks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ national police offices and Europol itself.
It does not apply to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
It applies to:
national police offices in the EU countries;
international police organisations;
non-EU law enforcement bodies;
national judicial authorities;
law enforcement agencies and judicial authorities.
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that:
personal data are processed only for the purpose of preventing, investigating and prosecuting crime;
the processing of information is carried out in accordance with the lawfulness of the law;
data subjects have the right to have their personal data processed and stored in a secure and confidential manner;
to have access to all Europol premises and to all the data processed.
EU countries must ensure that personal data are stored and processed only in a way that is necessary for Europol to fulfil its tasks and that is in line with their national law.
Data protection rules
The European Union has a Data Protection Officer (DPO) responsible for protecting personal data. The EPO is appointed by the European Parliament and the Council of the EU and is assisted by the Management Board.
Duties of the EPO
The EPO:
is responsible for:
collecting, processing, storing, analysing and sharing information;
ensuring the confidentiality and security of the personal data it processes;
monitoring and ensuring proper data integrity and security;
assisting Europol in its work;
preparing and disseminating reports;
providing technical and organisational measures to protect personal data against accidental or unlawful destruction, accidental loss or unauthorised disclosure, alteration and access or any other form of processing.
Evaluation of data protection
EDPOs must:
ensure that the data they process are protected;
use the information they collect for the purposes of preventing and investigating crime, detecting and investigating serious crime, preventing and prosecuting serious crime and preventing crime.
They must ensure the protection of the confidentiality, security and integrity of the data that they process, including:
identifying the data subjects;
protecting personal data from accidental destruction or accidental loss;
notifying the identity of any person who has been the subject of a personal data query;
keeping a record of all personal data that Europol has processed and storing.
In addition, Europol must ensure:
the confidentiality and confidentiality of the information it has processed, stored and exchanged;
that personal data is protected against accidental destruction, accident or loss; and
the protection of data against alteration, deletion, combination or erasure.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
Decision 2010/852/EU has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The Europol Convention is based on Article K.3.3 of the Treaty on European Union, which establishes a European police office.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: personal data: any personal data, such as names, addresses, dates of birth, gender, place of residence, date of death, contact details and contact details of the person concerned.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2010/884/EU of 21 December 2010 establishing the rules of procedure for the establishment and functioning of Europol (OJ L 347, 21.12.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 37:
Resolution of credit institutions and credit institutions: financial stability and financial soundness
Resolving credit institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 — resolution and resolution tools
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that credit institutions, such as Credit Clearing Companies (CCPs), are able to be wound down and their assets, rights, obligations and liabilities transferred to another entity or, in the application of a resolution tool, from a bridge CCP to a purchaser.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all credit institutions in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to credit institutions operating in non-EU countries.
The resolution authority must ensure that:
the resolution authority:
recognises the CCP’s losses;
ensures a definitive valuation of the CCP in its books;
provides a decision to write back creditors’ claims or to increase the value of the consideration paid, in accordance with the terms of the resolution plan;
informs creditors of any decision to increase or write back the value or increase the amount of the settlement.
It must:
ensure that the resolution authority has the necessary financial resources and expertise to carry out its tasks;
take into account the needs of the creditors;
assess the recovery plans for the CCP and its assets and liabilities;
implement a recovery plan.
Resolution authorities must ensure the following:
have the necessary resources to:
collect and manage all the assets, liabilities and assets of a credit institution;
have sufficient to cover their costs;
make a sufficient number of claims and liabilities to cover the costs of their recovery plans;
be able to pay out a ‘no creditor worse off’ payment.
A resolution authority may, before 11 February 2021, apply to its competent authority for permission to avail itself of transitional arrangements.
Transitional arrangements may be granted to a CCP or a trading venue for a period of up to 2 years, or up to a maximum of 2 years.
Creditors must be compensated for any losses they suffer as a result of the failure of a CCP to comply with its recovery plans. They must also receive compensation from the resolution authorities. This compensation should be deducted from any entitlement to a no creditor better off payment. The resolution authority is responsible for:
paying all the claims and obligations of creditors, including:
recourse payments;
collecting and remunerating claims;
recovering assets;
keeping records of transactions;
making payments to creditors.
In addition, the regulation provides for the protection of counterparties in the event of partial transfers:
where a resolution authority transfers some but not all of the assets or rights of the credit institution under resolution to another institution or, where it transfers assets, obligations or liabilities of a CC under resolution, from another entity to another or from a purchaser;
where it exercises the powers referred to in point (g) of Article 49(1) of Directive 2014/59/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary);
where the CCP is subject to a suspension of the clearing obligation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 11 February 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) has adopted a resolution framework for credit institutions. It aims to improve the stability of the financial system and to ensure the sound functioning of credit markets in the EU.
For more information, see:
Credit institutions (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Credit institutions: credit institutions are credit institutions that provide services across national borders to a broad range of financial and other institutions across the EU, including banks, insurance companies, investment firms, investment management firms and other financial institutions.
* Trading venues: trading venues that provide trading services to the market in financial derivatives. They provide services in the form of trading venues. They may be regulated by the competent authorities of the EU Member States or by the European Central Bank (ECB).
* Rastructures: a structure or structure consisting of a centralised trading venue or a rastructure, which provides trading venues for the period specified by the CCP.

Summary 38:
European Year for the European Railways (2014-2020)
European year for the rail
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 642/2014 on the European Year for Railways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to promote rail as a sustainable, innovative, interconnected and intermodal, safe and affordable mode of transport, in particular by highlighting the role of rail:
as a game changer helping to achieve the EU’s climate neutrality objective by 2050;
as the pillar of an efficient logistic network, capable of guaranteeing essential services, even during unexpected crises.
KEY POINTS
The European Year 2014-2020 is the fourth railway year under the European Green Deal. It is the first year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the EU, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the EU and on providing the European Union Agency for Railway Safety (ERA) with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
The objectives of the year are to:
promote rail as:
a sustainable and innovative, innovative and interconnected transport option;
safe and affordable;
an efficient and cost-effective means of transport;
a high-speed, high-capacity and high-quality rail network capable of meeting the needs of the European economy.
To achieve these objectives, the EU countries must:
open the market for passenger services;
provide the European railway companies with the necessary financial resources;
reduce costs for railway companies;
ensure a high level of safety and environmental protection.
In addition, the European Commission will organise a number of events to promote the European rail year. These events will take place at EU and national level.
International cooperation
The Commission will, where necessary, cooperate with competent international organisations, while ensuring the visibility of the EU's participation. The Commission will also hold meetings with the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee, as well as with the International Transport Workers’ Federation (ITF) and the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe (UNECA).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is committed to achieving a true single European railway area by 2020. This is achieved by:
establishing a single European rail area;
developing a high speed rail network;
improving the efficiency of the rail sector;
strengthening cooperation between EU countries.
For more information, see:
European rail year (European Commission).

Summary 40:
Reused water: water quality class
Reclaimed water: reused water class
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the reuse of reclaimed water
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the management of the quality of water in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules for the management and monitoring of the re-use of water.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all water bodies in the EU. It does not apply to water bodies used for the abstraction of water intended for human consumption.
It applies to:
reclamation facilities;
recovery ponds and aquaculture ponds;
water treatment plants;
seawater treatment plants.
Risk management
EU countries must draw up a water reuse risk management plan (WRMP) for each of their water bodies.
The WRMP must include:
a description of the risks to the environment and human or animal health;
information and awareness-raising campaigns on the risks of water reuse;
measures to be taken to protect human and animal health and the environment;
preventing the contamination of water bodies;
monitoring and reporting on the results of the water reuse process.
Requirements for the drawing up of the WRP and the monitoring and reporting of the results
The plan must be drawn up by the water body operator or the authority responsible for the production and supply of the reclaimed water. The plan must contain:
the following information:
information on the risk to the water poses;
a list of sources and methods of water treatment;
the number of sources of water and methods used;
how the water is treated and treated;
methods of treatment and storage
the types of water used for water treatment.
If the plan does not contain all the information required, the plan may be amended by Regulation (EC) No 552/2014.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2012.

Summary 41:
EU blue card for non-EU nationals — mobility and residence
European blue card — mobility AND residence
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/801 on the granting of an EU blue card
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to facilitate the movement of third-country nationals (known as EU blue cards) between EU countries.
It sets out the conditions and procedures for granting and renewing these cards.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
third-country national who apply to reside in a Member State for the purpose of highly qualified employment within the meaning of Directive 2021/1883 of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary);
those who apply for an initial or renewal of a blue card in order to reside and work there under the EU Blue Card on the basis of a work contract or binding job offer;
those applying for residence in the second EU country for family members.
EU blue cards are valid for a period of 5 years. The duration of this period may be extended to 10 years if the holder is a resident of the EU country concerned.
The holder of a card may be granted residence in another EU country if:
the conditions for admission are fulfilled;
the person concerned is considered to pose a threat to public policy, public security or public health; or
the employer’s business was established or operates for the main purpose of facilitating the entry of third‑country nationals.
Member States must:
ensure that the person concerned:
has a valid residence permit;
has the necessary documents;
is not the subject to fraudulently acquired, falsified or tampered with;
provides proof that the documents presented are authentic;
does not comply with the conditions laid down in Article 5 of the directive.
An application must be submitted to a contact point in the Member State concerned within 30 days of the date of submission of the application. The contact point must be located in the same Member State as the contact point for the first Member State.
A Member State may reject an application for an EU card if it is found that:
a Member State considers that the application is not complied with or that the document presented has been falsified;
a third party has been involved in a fraud or falsified act;
there is reason to believe that the third party is involved in organised crime, terrorism or a serious threat to the public policy or security of another Member State;
it is considered that a third party poses a threat or poses a risk to public security, public health or public order.
In addition, Member States must ensure that the information provided in the application, such as the name, address, date of birth, nationality and place of residence of the applicant, is accurate and that it is accompanied by the necessary documentation. The information must be provided in a language easily understood by the contact points. Member States may also require the information to be accompanied by a photograph of the holder of the card and a copy of the relevant documents.
If the Member States consider that an application has not been complied with, they may refuse to grant the card if the applicant:
is an individual who poses a security risk,
has committed a serious offence,
is considered to be involved in fraud or has committed a crime,
the third party concerned is an individual involved in terrorism,
poses a threat of serious crime, or
has engaged in a serious breach of public policy.
Residence in a second Member State
The Member State must grant the holder a residence permit or residence permit for the duration of the 5-year period defined in this directive. The residence permit may be renewed for a maximum of 3 years.
Movement within the EU
An EU blue-card holder may move to a Member States’ territory for the purposes of:
working or studying;
studying;
being a self-employed person;
living or working in the country concerned;
exercising the right of mobility to other Member States;
visiting family members;
traveling to the country of origin of a third country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?

It has applied since 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU blue code was first introduced in 2004. It has been amended several times, most recently in 2016. The most recent amendment was adopted in 2018.
For more information, see:
‘The EU Blue Code’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) 2016 /801 of 27 June 2016 on the grant of an European blue card (OJ L 347, 28.6.2016, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive (Euratom) No 802/2014 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 42:
European Migration Fund
European migration fund
SUMMigration Fund
The European Migration Fund (EMF) is a European Union (EU) instrument designed to support EU countries’ efforts to tackle the migration crisis. Its main objective is to:
contribute to the sustainable development of the EU’s external borders;
provide financial support for the implementation of the common migration policy;
support the development of a comprehensive, pragmatic and resolute approach to migration.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/1060 of 14 March 2018 establishing the European Migration Facility.
RELATED ACTS
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 February 2013 establishing the common border management system for the EU.
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU establishing the Common Border Management System for the European Union.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
The EMF sets out the specific objectives of the EMF:
to:
ensure that EU countries have the capacity to manage the external borders effectively and efficiently;
to contribute to the prevention and control of irregular migration;
promote sustainable development in the field of migration; and
to support the integration of migrants and asylum seekers into the labour market.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the EMF has a budget of at least €2.5 billion for the period 2014-2020;
all EU countries are eligible for EMF funding;
at least €1 billion of that amount must come from the EU budget.
as well as from non-EU countries.
The EU budget must be used for the benefit of the Member State concerned.
Member States must report on the use and the distribution of EMF resources to the European Commission, as well as on the measures they have implemented.
Implementing decisions must be based on measurable indicators as a basis for evaluating the effects of the Fund on the ground. These indicators include:
number of people admitted through humanitarian admission;
the number of families placed in alternatives to detention;
total number of people transferred from one Member State to another.
In addition, Member States must:
report to the Commission on the number of persons and families who have been admitted to the EU;
report on the types of intervention they have carried out;
monitor and report the results of the actions they have taken;
assess the impact of their actions on the migration situation in the Member States;
draw up a report every 3 years on the progress made by Member States and on the results they have achieved.
Each year, the Commission must submit a report to the Council on the implementation and results of its EMF. The report must include a summary of the measures that have been implemented and the indicators that have not been implemented. It must also include a description of the results achieved and the measures still to be implemented. The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation;
reporting on the quality of the information it receives;
keeping a record of the data it has received;
identifying the number and types of beneficiaries of international protection;
checking that the measures are being implemented;
examining the number, types and categories of persons who have benefited from international protection.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 15 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
The migration crisis is one of the main challenges facing the EU and the EU is committed to addressing it. The EMF was established in 2003. It is the main instrument of the Common Migration Fund.
For more information, see:
European Migration Facility (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Resolution of 27 September 2017 on the continuation of the Migration Fund [Official Journal C 202 of 27.9.2017].
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Council Implementing Decisions 2014/884/EU and 2014/946/EU of 27 March 2014 on the common Border Management system for Europe (Official Journal L 347 of 28.3.2014).

Commission implementing Decision 2014-852 of 27 February 2014 establishing a Common Border Migration System for Europe, repealing Council Decision 2013/945/EU (Official journal L 347)

Summary 43:
Flexible working arrangements for mothers and fathers who take maternity and parental leave (Parental leave directive)
Facilitating flexible working arrangements between men and women who have a child and who have taken parental leave: Directive (EU) 2020/852
Facilitation of flexible working opportunities for mothers, fathers and other workers who have had a child
Families should be able to request flexible working* arrangements between them and their employers.
ACT
Council Directive (EEC) No 852/2010 of 2 August 2010 on the flexibility of working arrangements in the context of parental leave.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It aims to improve the quality of working conditions for women and men in the labour market by:
improving the flexibility and effectiveness of working hours;
promoting equal treatment of women, men and men;
encouraging the reintegration of mothers in labour market after they have taken a period of maternity and/or parental leave;
ensuring that women have access to flexible working conditions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all workers in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to:
part-time workers;
temporary workers; or
contractual workers.
The period of leave is linked to the age of the child.
It does not cover:
the duration of maternity or parental leave,
the number of weeks of leave or the duration of the leave, or
the length of leave, and
the nature of the flexible working arrangement.
Employers may postpone parental leave if they consider that it would seriously disrupt the good functioning of the employer’s’ organisation or would seriously affect the organisation of the organisation.
EU countries may establish the conditions under which an employer is allowed to postpone the granting parental leave for a reasonable period of time on the grounds that the taking of the parental leave at the time requested would seriously interfere with the organisation's good functioning.
Workers who exercise their right to take leave or to request flexibility working arrangements as provided for in this directive should enjoy protection from dismissal and any preparatory steps for a possible dismissal on grounds that they have applied for, or have taken, such leave or that they exercised their right of request such flexibility.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
employers do not dismiss workers who are entitled to take parental leave or request flexible work arrangements on grounds of their gender;
they do not discriminate between men or women based on the gender of the person concerned;
the employer is not obliged to inform the worker of the reasons for postponing parental leave in writing.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
This directive applies from 2 August 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 August 2024.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of this directive.
For more information, see:
‘Flexibility of working time’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) No 1888/2010
2.8.2010
-
OJ L 127, 2.8, 2010, pp. 1-8

Summary 44:
Fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests: definition of criminal offences
Fraud impacting the Union‘Misappropriation’ means the action of a public official who is directly or indirectly entrusted with the management of funds or assets to commit or disburse funds or appropriate or use assets contrary to the purpose for which they were intended in any way which damages the EU's financial interests.
ACT
Council Directive (EU) 2015/849 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 May 2015 on the prevention of the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or terrorist financing.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of fraud and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that fraud is a criminal offence when it is committed intentionally.
KEY POINTS
The definition of ‘fraud’ is based on the Convention drawn up on the basis of Article K.3.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on the protection of the EU Communities’ financial interests, including the Protocols thereto of 27 September 1996 (4), of 29 November 1996 and of 19 June 1997 (the ‘Convention’) (see summary).
The Council of Europe Convention on the Protection of the Union's Financial Interests (COPEC) is a set of rules designed to protect the EU countries' financial interests against fraud and corruption.
The COPEC Convention applies to the following types of fraud:
passive corruption and misappropriation;
misappropriation of funds and assets;
the abuse of public officials’ functions;
abuse of official positions.
Scope
The directive applies to all public officials covering all relevant officials, whether holding a formal office in the EU, in the Member States or in third countries.
It does not apply to:
public officials who are not in a formal position of authority;
non-EU countries’ nationals;
individuals who are resident in an EU country.
Penalties
EU countries must ensure that:
the offender is subject to the Staff Regulations of the Member State in which the offence was committed;
if the offender is a national of an EU Member State, the offender must be subject to an effective, proportionate, dissuasive and dissuasive financial penalty.
Sanctions
Member States must:
impose sanctions on the offender for the following offences:
fraud against the common VAT system;
defrauding the European Social Fund (ESF);
spreading false information;
using public funds for personal gain;
spamming of EU funds.
Exemptions from sanctions
The Directive does not cover:
any person who:
is not a Member State official;
does not hold a formal official position;
has not held a formal public office;
is a natural person.
References

Summary 45:
Interchange fees: EU rules for card-based payment transactions and internet and mobile payments based on cards (Regulation (EU) No 1093/2010)
INTERFERENCE FEFEFEFEFERENCES
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/35/EU on interchange fees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down uniform EU-wide rules on the application of interchange fees*.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following types of payment transactions:
card-based payments, i.e. transactions where a payment is made using a credit or debit card;
internet and mobile payment transactions.
It does not apply to:
banknotes;
cash transactions;
credit cards;
debit cards.
EU countries may define a lower per transaction interchange fee cap for domestic credit card transactions than those applicable in the EU countries. This cap must not exceed 3 % of the value of all card-related transactions made in that EU country. It should not prevent EU countries from maintaining or introducing lower caps or measures of equivalent object or effect through national legislation.
In addition, EU countries may:
limit the amount of the merchant service charges, interchange fees and scheme fees applicable with respect to each category and brand of payment cards, unless the payee subsequently makes a different request in writing;
set up a national authority to monitor the implementation of the regulation;
establish a European supervisory authority to ensure that the regulation’s rules are complied with;
ensure that the European Banking Authority (EBA) is independent of national authorities.
apply the rules laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
The EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the impact of EU interchange fees on the development of the electronic payments market in the European Union (EU). The report found that:
interchange fees have developed so as to allow consumers to benefit from efficient debit card markets in terms of card acceptance and card usage with lower interchange fees than the merchant indifference level;
in certain EU countries, they have developed to a level playing field.
For more information, see:
European interchange fees (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee charged by a payment card issuer to a merchant for the use of a payment instrument.
* Scheme fees: fees charged by payment card issuers to a payment service provider for the processing of payment instruments.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Regulation 2014/34/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 May 2014 on the approximation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to interchange fees applicable to the payment of credit and debit cards in the Member States and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 716/2009/EC (OJ L 331, 15.6.2014, pp. 1-3)
Successive amendments to Regulation (2014/35) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 47:
Statistics on passenger transport by inland waterways (NUTS)
Statistics on the passenger transport of goods on inland waterways
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 966/2012 — statistics on passenger traffic by inland waterways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 on statistics on inland waterway transport.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the total volume of goods transported annually by the EU’s inland waterways as national transport;
the number of vessels transporting goods;
passenger traffic;
traffic movements of vessels;
movements of vessels.
EU countries must supply the European Commission (Eurostat) with the following information and data as it may request:
Tonnes transported (Tonnes) and movements (Vessel-km)
Passenger traffic:
goods not in containers and empty containers;
transport of goods by road, rail, air, sea or inland waterways.
Movements of vehicles:
motor vehicles being moved for repair;
vessels not used for the carriage of goods such as fishery vessels, dredgers, floating workshops, houseboats and pleasure craft.
National authorities must:
provide the Commission with:
data on the number and type of vessels used for inland waterways;
information on the types of goods and movements of vehicles;
a description of the type of vessel and the number of containers used;
details of the types and number of vehicles used for each movement of goods.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to carry out the statistical actions for which those delegated acts provide, using, where appropriate, a cost-effectiveness analysis, including an assessment of the burden on respondents and of the production costs as referred to in Article 14(3)(c) of the regulation.
Delegated acts may be delegated by the Commission to EU countries or to the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA). The Commission must ensure that the delegated acts do not impose a significant additional burden on the Member States or on the respondents.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure laid down in Article 11(2) of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2013 on the transmission of data subject to statistical confidentiality to the Statistical Office of the European Communities (SOC).
The European Commission must submit a report to Eurostat every 2 years on the quality of the data transmitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Inland waterway: any waterway in the European Union (EU).
* Vessel: any vessel used solely for non-commercial purposes by port administrations and public authorities.
* Movements: movements of goods in and out of a vessel.

Council Regulation (Euratom) 2015/966 of 27 June 2015 on statistics of passenger transport on inland waters (Codified version) (OJ L 130, 29.6.2015, pp. 1-8)

Summary 48:
Statistics at EU level on rail transport
Statistics at Union level on railway transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 on EU-level statistics on rail traffic
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of statistics on railway traffic in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the carriage of goods and passengers by rail in the course of combined transport operations;
passenger train movements in the form of passenger train movements;
the number of passenger trains moving in and out of the EU.
EU countries must transmit the statistics to Eurostat, the EU’s statistical office, within 3 months of the end of the reference period.
Annexes to the regulation set out the variables and units of measurement to be used in the collection of the statistics. These are:
volume of goods transported;
number of passengers;
total train movements.
Data collection
The statistics to be collected are set out in Annexes I and II of the regulation. They cover:
total goods transported
total passengers
total passenger movements
total rail movements
frequency of train movements
the volume of passenger transport.
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the arrangements for the dissemination of the results.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU-wide statistics are required to ensure the proper functioning of the internal market in rail transport and to ensure that the EU has a coherent and reliable statistical system.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Combined transport: the transport of goods, passengers and freight by rail.
* Passenger train movements: the number of trains moving between two or more railway stations in one or more EU countries.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on EU level statistics on the rail transport system
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 49:
Suspension mechanism for the exemption from the visa requirement for non-EU nationals of non-Schengen countries in emergency situations (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the temporary suspension of the exemption of the Schengen visa requirement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a mechanism to temporarily suspend the application of an EU country’s visa requirement in the event of an emergency situation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to nationals of one or more EU countries whose nationals are subject to a visa requirement. It does not apply to the nationals of Switzerland, Austria, the United Kingdom (1) or the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to the holders of biometric passports issued by Moldova in line with standards of the International Civil Aviation Organisation (ICAO).
The exemption from visa requirement applies to Moldova, Austria and Switzerland.
It is suspended for a period of 18 months for all nationals of the third country concerned. The period of suspension may be extended by 6 months if the Commission submits a legislative proposal as referred to in point (h) to extend the period of the suspension.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to suspend the exemption. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure set out in Article 11 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) and must be published in the Official Journal of the European Union.
Annex II of the regulation contains a list of the countries which are included in the list of countries subject to an EU visa requirements.
In the Commission must notify the European Parliament, the name of the country in question and the date on which the exemption is suspended. The notification must contain a footnote indicating that the exemption has been suspended with regard to that third country and specifying the duration of that suspension. The Commission must publish the notification without delay by the Commission in its Official Journal.
A delegated act may be amended by inserting a footnote in the annex to the regulation. The amendment must be made by inserting next to the name on the annex.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Eurojust : operational and functional rules for Eurojust (JHJ)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the operational functions of Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the operational rules and procedures for the functioning of the Eurojust, a European Union (EU) body responsible for investigating and prosecuting serious crime.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that Eurojust is able to investigate and prosecute serious crime in the EU.
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ nationals who are nationals of an EU country. It does not apply to EU countries that are not members of the European Economic Area (EEA).

operate liaison magistrates;
take decisions on the transfer and processing of operational data.

monitoring and ensuring the application of the data protection provisions of this regulation;
ensuring the proper application of data protection rules;
assisting the European Judicial Committee on the Protection of Personal Data (ECPDP).
The European Commission:
assists Eurojust in its work;
is assisted by the European Criminal Police Office (ECP);
is responsible for the protection of the operational data transferred;
has the power to: issue or execute requests for judicial assistance;
approve or execute investigations;
issue investigative measures.
In urgent cases and in accordance with Article 19, the OCC may receive and transmit the requests referred to in the regulation if they have been issued by a third country which has also been transmitted by a competent national authority. The Commission and Eurojust must ensure that these requests comply with the relevant data protection requirements.

BACKGROUND

Summary 51:
Money laundering: criminal law
money laundering: legal system
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/849 on combating money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering by means of criminal law, enabling more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between EU countries’ authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to money laundering committed by individuals, companies or other legal entities.
It does not cover:
the proceeds of crime;
the effects of money laundering on the economy and the economy of the EU as a whole; or
the transfer of funds between the EU and non-EU countries.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that, in relation to the offences referred to in Article 3(1) and (5) and Article 4, the following circumstances are to be regarded as aggravating circumstances: the offence was committed within the framework of a criminal organisation within the meaning of Framework Decision 2008/841/JHA (see summary);
the offender is an obliged entity or an oblige entity, and has committed the offence in the exercise of their professional activities.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that the offence is punishable by effective, proportionate and dissuasive criminal penalties.
Penalties must be proportionate to the seriousness of the offence and must be dissuasive.
The offences covered by the directive must be punishable by at least one of the following the following aggravating circumstance:
a person or company has committed an offence that is an offence which is contrary to the general principles of the law of an EU country (e.g. For example, money laundering is a criminal offence if it is committed recklessly or by serious negligence;
an offence is committed by an individual or company which is liable to be liable to liability for the loss or damage caused by the offence;
a criminal organisation is liable for liability if its property was derived from criminal activity.
Exceptions to the above-mentioned aggravating conditions
The offence is also punishable by a maximum penalty of at least 1 year’s imprisonment. The maximum penalty may be reduced to a maximum of 3 years’ imprisonment if the offender is:
an individual, company or entity that is obliged to do so, or has committed such an offence for the purpose of preventing, detecting, investigating, prosecuting or prosecuting the offence, or punishing the offender for the purposes of preventing or investigating the offence.
Procedures for cooperation
In criminal proceedings regarding money laundering, the competent authorities of EU countries should assist each other in the widest possible way and ensure that information is exchanged in an effective and timely manner in accordance with national law and the existing EU legal framework.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 July 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Money laundering: the laundering of funds, goods, services, capital, services and intellectual property.
* Currency counterfeiting: the counterfeiting of the euro and other currencies against counterfeiting by criminal law.

Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Council Directive 2014/62/EU

Summary 53:
Sale of a vessel at less than normal value: injurious pricing
Sale sale of a vehicle at a price below normal value
This regulation codifies and repeals Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the sale of vessels at less-than-normal value.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 of 25 November 2011 on the enforcement of injurious prices of vessels.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules for the enforcement by the European Commission of any sale at a lower than normal price for a vessel sold to a non-market-economy (non-EU) country.
It sets out the procedure for the investigation and prosecution of such sales.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
shipbuilding contracts
contracts for the sale or lease of a ship to an exporting country;
ships for the use of the EU’s territorial waters;
seizures of vessels for use in international waters.
The Commission may decide to initiate an investigation if it considers that the sale is causing, or has caused, injury within the meaning of this regulation. It must be established that there is sufficient evidence to justify initiating proceedings. The Commission must examine the accuracy and adequacy of the evidence provided in the complaint, to determine whether there is a need to initiate proceedings.
If the Commission considers that there are sufficient grounds for initiating proceedings, it must do so within 45 days of the complaint being lodged or, or, in the case of initiation, no later than 6 months from the date on which the complaint was lodged.
the Commission must publish a notice in the Official Journal of the European Union (OJ).
The investigation may be terminated by the Commission acting according to the examination procedure referred to in Article 10(2) of the regulation.
Where a shipbuilder does not pay the charge within the applicable time limit, the shipbuilder must be informed of the reasons for its refusal.
In the event of non-compliance, the EU country concerned must notify the Commission of the reason for its non-cooperation.
EU countries must inform the Commission once it has determined that it is necessary to initiate such proceedings. If the Commission decides that the investigation is necessary, the Commission must provide information to the EU countries once it is satisfied that it has established a need.
Procedures for the prosecution of infringements
The European Commission may initiate proceedings against a company if:
it considers that:
the sale of the vessel is causing a loss;
it is causing injury; or
it has caused a loss which is disproportionate to the benefit from the operation of the ship.
An investigation must be launched within 6 months of the sale being known or should have been known, and must be concluded within 1 year of the date of initiation.
Investigations must be open to all interested parties, including the shipbuilding company and the exporting country concerned.
Information must be made available to interested parties as soon as possible.
Requests for confidentiality must not be arbitrarily rejected. In exceptional circumstances, such parties may indicate that such information is not capable of being summarised.
Implementing measures
If a company does not comply with the regulation, it may be subject to sanctions, including:
a reduction in the value of the contract;
a suspension of its export privileges;
the suspension of the right to use the vessel;
restrictions on the number of ships that can be used on the market;
suspension of the rights to use a vessel.
Expiry of the injurious price
In exceptional cases, a company may be required to pay a fine.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
 			
The EU has adopted a series of measures to improve the functioning of the internal market in the field of shipbuilding.
For more information, see:
Injurious pricing (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Injurious price: a price which is calculated to result in a profit which is not a reasonable one at the time of sale.
* Expiry: the price at which a contract is concluded for a particular product or service.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EC, Euratom):
1.1.2012
-
OJ L 145, 31.12.2011, pp. 1-8

Summary 54:
EU rules on the design, manufacture and testing of subsystems and safety components for cableways and lifts (Directive 2000/9/EC) (see summary)
European Union (EU) rules governing the design and manufacture of components and subsystems for cables and lifts for cableway installations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2010 — technical harmonisation and standards for subsystems, safety components and safety information relating to cableways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down EU rules on:
design, manufacture, testing and certification of subsystem* and safety* components for the carriage of goods and passengers on cableways in the EU;
authorisation and monitoring of installations;
EU declaration of conformity.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cableways and systems for the transport of goods, passengers and persons;
systems for the operation of cableways;
cables for the maintenance, repair or replacement of cables and systems.
It does not apply to: subsystems or safety components that are intended for use in the transport sector, such as:
trailers and escalators;
bicyclists;
rope lifts;
traffic control systems;
motor vehicles;
passenger lifts.
EU countries must ensure that:
the cableway installation complies with the requirements laid down in the regulation;
the manufacturer and importer have complied with the regulation’s requirements;
requests for the CE marking and conformity assessment;
safety analysis and safety report for planned installations are carried out by the competent authorities in the relevant EU countries;
manufacturers and importers have carried out the necessary safety checks.
Importers must carry out the required safety checks before placing a cableway on the market.
Manufacturers must:
operate an approved quality system;
ensure that their products are tested and certified according to the requirements set out in Annex II;
provide the competent national authorities with a copy of the CE conformity assessment report;
conduct a safety analysis and a safety report on their products;
assist the competent authority in carrying out the safety checks;
carry out a safety evaluation of their products before placing them on the EU market;
report to the relevant national authorities on the safety and health of the subsystem or safety component concerned.
The competent authority must inform the EU country concerned of the results of the safety assessment and the safety report, as well as of any corrective measures that may be necessary.
Notification of conformity
The EU country in which the cableway is located must notify the European Commission of its intention to issue a CE marking to a subsystem or a safety component. The CE marking must be affixed under its responsibility.
A notified body must submit a report to the Commission on the conformity assessment, notification or monitoring it has carried out. The report must contain:
a description of the components concerned;
an indication of their conformity status;
a list of the authorised authorities responsible for monitoring the safety of the equipment;
information on the risks to the health and safety of persons and property.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report on the implementation of the regulation in the Official Journal of the European Parliament and of the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2010/552/EC has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
The cableway sector is one of the most dynamic sectors in the European Union and is subject to a wide range of technical and organisational challenges. This regulation aims to ensure that it is possible to harmonise technical standards to improve the safety, health and environmental performance of the sector.
For more information, see:
Cableway safety (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cableway: a system of cables, systems or systems intended for the movement of goods or persons on a railway.
* Safety information: a description of a subsystem, safety component or system of equipment that is intended to be used on a rail or on a road. It must be in a language easily understood by users as determined by the Member State concerned, and that the manufacturer and the importer comply with the applicable EU rules.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2010 of 21 December 2010 laying down technical harmonisations and standards relating to subsystems*, safety components, safety information and technical documentation relating to the carriage and operation of cables (OJ L 131, 21.12.2010, pp. 1-8)
