Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for people with disabilities
Assessment and market surveillance of accessibility features for persons with disabilities in the internal market: Directive (EU) 2018/1972
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2018/72/EU on the assessment and marketing of products with accessibility features
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the accessibility of products, services and information on the European Union’s internal market.
It sets out minimum requirements for the assessment, marketing and certification of products to ensure that they comply with the accessibility requirements of the United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (UN CRPD).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services intended for use by persons with a disability;
electronic communications services and emergency services;
services for the provision of information and communication technologies;
information and communication services.
The scope of the directive is limited to products intended for the use of individuals with disabilities. It does not apply to microenterprises providing services within the scope of this directive.
Evaluation and conformity assessment
Manufacturers, importers and distributors must carry out an assessment of products before placing them on the market. The assessment must be carried out in accordance with the requirements laid down in Annex I.
Distributors and importers must ensure that:
the products they sell comply with all the requirements set out in Annex II;
the technical specifications of the products are clearly visible to all users;
they must indicate in the accessibility features in the packaging and in the instructions and on the products they make available to them;
all technical specifications must be clearly visible and easily understood by all users.
If a product is found to be in breach of these requirements, it must be withdrawn from the market and replaced by a new product that complies with these requirements.
Where a product does not comply, the European Commission must adopt a product to cover all the shortcomings in the technical specifications.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The UN CRPD has adopted a number of measures to improve accessibility in the EU. These include:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the accessibility and accessibility-related aspects of the European electronic communications sector (see summary);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 652/2004 and (EC), No 653/2005 on electronic communication services and (Euratom) Decisions No 654/2004, No 656/2005, No 752/2005 and No 753/2006 on electronic communications services, as amended by Regulation (EC).
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility’ on the EU Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Accessibility: the ability of a product or service to perform in an accessible manner.
* Electronic communications services: services that provide information, information or communication technology (e.g. e-mail, social media, etc.).
* Emergency services: emergency services that are designed to be used by users with disabilities, such as ambulances, emergency medical services, fire engines, ambulances and ambulances.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 56:
Importers of minerals and metals: due diligence and traceability of their supply chain and checks on the origin of conflict
Importing minerals and metal: due-diligence requirements
Imporcers of metals and minerals
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — due diligence requirements for the import and export of metals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s supply chain for metals and mineral products is subject to due diligence* requirements.
It sets out rules for importers of metals, minerals and minerals to ensure the proper functioning of the EU system while guaranteeing that the vast majority of metals imported into the EU are subject to its requirements. It also aims to prevent conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
mineral and metals imports from non-EU countries;
minerals and metals imported by EU importers from outside the EU;
metals imported by importers in the EU.
EU importers must:
identify and assess, in accordance with Annex II to the OECD Due Diligence Guidance and the specific recommendations set out in that Guidance, the risks in the supply chain based on available third-party audit reports concerning the smelters and refiners in that chain, and, by assessing, appropriate, the due diligence practices of those companies;
assess and assess the risks based on the information they have available to them;
ensure that they have carried out due diligence checks;
inform the European Commission of the results of these checks.
exchange information on shortcomings detected through the ex-post checks, as well as on infringements of the regulation.
The Commission must keep a list of importers’ annual import volumes of each mineral or metal subject to the regulation, and the volume thresholds listed in Annex I to this regulation, for at least 5 years. The Commission must also keep records of infringements.
Member States must notify the Commission of any infringements that they consider to be serious and must be reported to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
EU countries must establish the rules applicable to infringements, including:
the nature and duration of the infringements;
the penalties for infringements and the penalties for non-compliance;
a list of the mineral and metals covered by the regulation;
details of the importers and importers who have infringed the regulation and the sanctions applicable to them.
In addition, Member States must ensure that:
importers carry out periodic checks on their imports of metals;
they keep a register of the metals they import;
their records are kept for a minimum of 5 years;
there is a system for the exchange of information between the Commission and the Member States of the import volume of each metal and of the minerals covered by this regulation and for the annual import volume thresholds.
Each Member State must establish a national scheme for the management of its mineral and metal import quotas. The regulation sets out the rules for the operation of this scheme.
A national scheme must be notified to the Commission every 5 years of the number of tonnes of minerals or metals it imports and the total volume of minerals it imports. It must also notify the European Parliament and to the Council of the measures it has taken to combat conflict and to hold economic operators accountable under the relevant guidelines as established by the United Nations and OECD.
Annex II of the Regulation sets out:
rules for the implementation of this regulation by Member States’ national schemes;
rules on the notification and notification procedure;
exchanges of information;
requests for Member States to report infringements to the OLAF.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2011. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle conflict in the mining and metals sectors. These include:
a ban on the export of minerals from conflict-affected countries,
rules to combat the proliferation of conflict-related weapons of mass destruction (WMDs) and the use of chemical weapons;
measures to combat trafficking in WMDs.
For more information, see:
Mineral and metal imports (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Mineral: any mineral, metal or mineral-based product, whether it is mined, processed or used for industrial purposes.
* Metals: all metals, including copper, zinc, lead, nickel, cobalt, tin, copper oxide and zinc oxide, which are used in the manufacture of electrical and electronic equipment.

Summary 57:
Rules on the placing of goods into free zones and the establishment of customs records in the European Free Trade Area (EFTA) (Recast of Regulation (EC) No 596/2004)
Rules on:
the placing of products into free trade areas
the establishment of a customs procedure
the creation of a permanent storage declaration
the storage of goods.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
Regulation (EU) No 550/2004 — placing goods into a free trade area (FTA)
SUMMARY
This regulation sets out the rules for the placing into and establishment of free trade zones (FTAs) of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The FTAs are open to all goods, goods and means of transport entering or leaving the EU.
The regulation applies to:
goods placed into the FTAs by the customs authorities of the EU countries;
the goods placed in the FTA on the territory of a non-EU country;
all goods imported or exported from the FTA.
It does not apply to temporary admission with partial relief from import duty.
Authorisation
The customs authorities may grant authorisation to the holder of the authorisation if they consider that the conditions for granting it are fulfilled. The authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years. The holder of an authorisation must keep appropriate records in a form approved by the EU country’s customs authorities.
Customs authorities may carry out any customs controls they deem necessary to ensure that the goods comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation.
they may require that the customs controls are carried out in accordance with the rules laid down by the competent national customs authorities in the territory.
Importation of goods
The goods are subject to customs controls at the entry and exit points of the FTA, as well as at the place where they have been presented without the permission of the customs authority.
Entry and exit summary declarations
When goods are brought into the EU, they must be accompanied by an entry summary declaration. The declaration must be valid for at least 5 years and may be invalidated within 200 days of lodging.
If the goods are not accompanied by a declaration, the goods must be returned to the country where they were brought.
Exchange of information
The declaration must contain:
a description of the goods concerned;
a list of the persons responsible for receiving the goods;
information on the goods involved;
details of the means and methods of transport used;
an explanation of the reasons for the goods’ arrival and departure.
In addition, the declaration must include information on the origin, destination, destination and transit of goods, including:
how the goods were transported;
how they were stored;
where the goods have been transported; and
the conditions for their re-export.
Temporary storage declaration (Temporary Storage Declaration)
The temporary storage declaration may be issued by a customs authority to a person who has been granted authorisation by the European Commission. This authorisation may be transferred to another person who fulfils the conditions set out for the procedure concerned. It must be renewed at least every 3 years. It may be renewed for a further 5 years if the conditions are fulfilled by the holder.
An exit summary declaration
If goods for which an exit declaration has been lodged are not taken out of the VAT free zone, the authorities may invalidate that declaration in either of the following cases:
within 150 days after the lodging of the declaration;
within 200 days after lodging of a declaration.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGUMENTS APPLY?
   
Regulations (EU, EFTA) No 555/2004 and (EC, Euratom) No 652/2004 have applied since 1 July 2004.
Regulatory (EU), Euratom (EC and Euratom).
BACKGROUND
The FTA is one of the most important tools in the fight against counterfeiting. It is designed to reduce customs evasion and to improve the efficiency of the internal market.
For more information, see:
‘The EU Free Trade Agreement’ (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
                    б   ᴀ   Ā 
Commission Regulation (EU/2005) of 1 July 2005 laying down the rules on placing goods in a free-trade area (OJ L 183, 1.7.2005, pp. 1-2)

Summary 58:

EU rules in the Internal Market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2003 on the protection of intellectual Property rights in internal market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to protect intellectual property (IP) rights by:
ensuring that customs authorities have the necessary resources to detect infringements;
improving cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities;
establishing a central database to facilitate the exchange of information on infringements.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to goods of a non-commercial nature contained in travellers’ personal luggage.
It does not apply to:
goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries;
items imported into EU countries that are intended to be used for commercial purposes;
personal data and personal data contained in personal luggage;
travel documents.
Requests for access to the database
Customs authorities must receive applications for the right of access, rectification or destruction of goods.
The application must contain the following information:
the name and address of the holder of the intellectual property right;
the date and place of origin of the goods;
where the goods are imported from outside the EU;
a description of the nature and quantity of the infringing goods and their provenance.
If the applicant’s identity;
any other information requested.
the customs department considers that the application does not contain all the information required, it may refuse to accept the application.
An application must be accompanied by a recommendation for action.
Where the applicant does not provide the information requested, the competent customs department may decide to suspend the action of the customs authorities until the applicant has provided the necessary information.
In the event that the applicant fails to provide the requested information, the applicant may be informed of the decision and the reasons for the decision.
Implementation of the regulation
The competent customs authorities must:
notify the applicant within 10 working days of receiving the application;
ensure that the information provided in the application is:
relevant to the investigation and prosecution of the infringer;
notifies the applicant of the measures to be taken;
provide the applicant with information on the procedure to be followed in the event of a breach of the notification obligations;
make available to the applicant the information necessary to determine whether an infringed right has been infringed;
send the applicant a copy of a decision on the expiry of the period during which those authorities are to take action;
give the applicant an opportunity to appeal against the decision;
receive a written confirmation from the applicant that the decision has been taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATOR APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
 			
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve cooperation between the EU countries in the area of customs.
For more information, see:
‘The Internal Market’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 2016/1383 of 21 December 2016 on the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights in Internal Market (Implementation Regulation (EU-2013) (OJ L 347, 28.12.2016, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC-2013/33) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 59:
EU-wide customs cooperation programme (EU-2020)
EU-2019/2020: EU-2020 customs cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 952/2013 establishing a customs programme at EU level
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union-wide programme for cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities.
It aims to improve cooperation between the EU’s customs authorities in the field of customs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers cooperation with third countries that are not participating in the programme or with international organisations, as well as cooperation with non-EU countries.
Objectives
The aim of the programme is to:
support the development, deployment, maintenance and operation of adaptations of or extensions to the common components of the European electronic systems for customs cooperation;
provide technical and administrative assistance to the customs authorities of the participating countries;
develop and implement project-based structured collaboration;
promote the exchange of information and best practice in the fields of customs cooperation.
Cooperation
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. The programme is funded by the European Union (EU). It is implemented by the EU budget.
Programme objectives
The objectives of the Programme include:
improving the interoperability and interoperability of the EU electronic systems;
encouraging the exchange and exchange of best practices in customs cooperation between customs authorities;
supporting the development and use of the common electronic systems to enable customs authorities to exchange information, best practices, best practice and best practices;
enhancing customs cooperation with the non-performing countries and international organisations;
examining the application of the principles of the Schengenomenal system;
contributing to the harmonisation of customs procedures and procedures.
Funding
The EU budget for the programme covers the costs of the implementation of:
the development and deployment of the electronic systems,
the maintenance of the systems and procedures;
the implementation of project groups;
research and development activities;
technical and organisational assistance activities.
Exemptions
The Programme may be eligible for funding when it is of interest to the EU. It may also be eligible to support the activities of third countries, such as those involved in:
countering counterfeiting and fraud;
counterfeiting prevention;
monitoring and control of counterfeit goods;
implementing the harmonised system for the control of goods at the EU's external borders;
cooperating with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and repealing Regulation (Euratom) No 1074/1999 (OJ L 248, 18.9.2017, pp. 29-30).
Exemption
The Commission may exclude from the scope of this programme actions that are of particular interest to third countries or international organisations.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise customs cooperation in the EU in order to improve its efficiency and effectiveness.
For more information, see:
EU customs cooperation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs: the customs authority responsible for the inspection, control and inspection of goods.
ACT
Commission Decision No 951/2013/EU of 30 May 2013 establishing a Customs programme at Union level.

Summary 60:
Equal opportunities and access to the labour market, fair working conditions and inclusion: European Social Fund (ESF+ strand)
Equitable opportunities and opportunities: European social fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 on the European social framework and the social inclusion strand under shared management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the objectives of the social dimension of the European Pillar of Social Rights and Equal Opportunities, which is the cornerstone of the EU’s social policy.
It establishes a social inclusion policy strand under the European Structural and Investment Funds.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The social dimension is one of the three pillars of the Pillar of European Union (EU). The other two are equal opportunities and equal opportunities, which are based on the principle of equal opportunities.
The Social Pillar is structured in three categories: equal opportunities; fair work conditions; and social protection and inclusion.
EU countries are required to ensure that:
equal opportunities are achieved by:
participants in employment, including self-employment, six months after leaving;
people with an improved labour market situation 6 months after leave;
workers with a fair working condition;
persons with access to social protection.
In addition, EU countries are obliged to:
promote the socio-economic integration of third-country nationals;
encourage the integration of non-EU nationals into the EU society;
fight against material deprivation;
ensure that people with disabilities have access to adequate housing and adequate health care;
have access to education;
participate in the labour markets;
improve the quality of social housing and social housing;
increase access to affordable housing.
Specific objectives
To achieve the objectives set out in the Social Pillar, the Social Fund must:
provide financial support to the social partners and civil society organisations in the implementation of social inclusion policies;
support the creation of networks and networks of social partners;
facilitate the exchange of information, communication and publicity on social inclusion;
contribute to the development of innovative social policies and strategies;
provides technical assistance to social partners, civil society and other interested parties;
assist in the development and implementation of strategies at EU level relevant to the ESF+.
The ESF+ is managed by the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the rules on the use of the financial support from the European structural funds.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 28 April 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Pillar of Europe is a key element of the Union's social dimension. It aims to promote equal opportunities for all citizens and to improve the living standards of all people in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Equal Opportunities and Equal Work Conditions’ (European Commission).

Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps Programme (ESCP) 2018-2020 — activities for young Europeans
European Solidity Corps Programme for young people
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/817 — European Solidarity Card Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European solidarity programme for young European citizens.
It aims to encourage young people to express their solidarity in practice.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers the period from 1 January 2018 to 31 December 2020. It is open to the participation of organisations from the European Union (EU).
Objectives
The aim of the programme is to:
promote solidarity among young people in the EU;
provide them with opportunities to contribute to the development of the EU’s solidarity policies and actions;
encourage them to participate actively in the activities of the Union;
contribute to strengthening communities and civic participation.
Participating organisations
To become a participating organisation, organisations must meet the following criteria:
be based in an EU country or territory;
have at least 2 years’ experience in the field of solidarity;
be active in the areas covered by the programme;
participate in activities that respond to unmet societal needs and contribute to strengthening the civic participation of young people.
Organisations participating in the programme must:
have a budget of at least €500,000;
meet the requirements and objectives of the Programme;
operate in accordance with the principles of the European Pillar of Social Rights and the European Charter of Fundamental Rights, as set out in Article 3 of the Treaty on the EU's Fundamental Rights;
notify the European Parliament, the Council and the Council of Europe, the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions, and the Committee on the Functioning of the Council;
apply the principles and procedures laid down in Articles 1 and 2 of the Charter of European Social Rights.
Quality and support measures
EU countries and territories must ensure that the programme has a high level of quality and support. The level of support is based on objective criteria such as:
the quality and effectiveness of the activities;
the impact of the actions on young people;
their impact on society and on the economy.
The quality of the work programmes and the quality of support measures must be monitored and evaluated regularly.
In addition, EU countries must ensure, at national level, that the actions implemented under the programme are:
integrated into national programmes and work programmes;
coordinated with other EU programmes and projects;
transformed into a coherent, coherent and coherent work programme.
Financing
The European Union provides financial support to participating organisations based on a number of criteria, including:
a minimum budget of €500 000;
a maximum of €1.5 million for each participating organisation;
an annual work programme of €2 million for the period 2018-20;
support measures of €5 million.
National authorities are responsible for implementing the programme and ensuring that the projects are implemented in line with the programme objectives. They must also ensure that:
projects are implemented according to the principles laid down by the Pillar of social rights;
organisations have the necessary financial resources to carry out their work;
work programmes are designed to be flexible and adaptable to the needs of the participants;
projects have the capacity to scale-up and to be completed in a timely manner.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
  The European Solidity Programme is part of the broader European solidarity policy, which aims to strengthen the solidarity of young Europeans. The programme is one of a series of initiatives designed to promote solidarity and strengthen civic participation in Europe.
For more information, see:
European solidarity programme (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021 / 817 of 21 December 2021 on the European Solidary Corps Programme.

Summary 62:
Equal Opportunities Guarantee (EGF)
Equitable Opportunities Grant
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/1371 on equal Opportunities for Gender Equality
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the quality of life of women and men in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The European Gender Equality Grant (EGG) is the EU’s first social investment programme. It aims to reduce inequalities and promote equal opportunities between men and women in the labour market.
The EGF is funded by the EU budget. It is designed to:
promote equal opportunities for women in employment and self-employment;
encourage the creation of a sustainable and resource-efficient economy;
support the growth of the self-employed sector;
provide training and vocational training to women in education and training sectors.
Scope
The programme is open to all EU countries, regardless of whether they are Member States or not. It covers the period from 1 January 2017 to 31 December 2027.
Eligible beneficiaries
EU countries must ensure that:
the beneficiaries are:
employed;
employed in education or training;
self-employed.
Beneficiaries are eligible for the EGF if they:
have a job or training qualification;
have been unemployed for at least 6 months;
are unemployed for more than 6 months.
They are eligible if they have been unemployed or have been laid off during the reference period (1 January 2017-31 December 2018).
The beneficiaries must be:
working women;
working men or men with a qualification or vocational training experience;
women with a degree or training experience, and
employed women with a training qualification.
Applicants must have:
been unemployed or laid off;
been in employment or training for 6 months or more;
had a job, training or qualification within 6 months of being unemployed or having a qualification by 6 months after the end of the implementation period.
Participation
The EU country concerned must:
make contact with the beneficiaries and provide them with information on the programme;
ensure that the beneficiaries have access to the coordinated package of personalised services;
assist the beneficiaries in the preparation of their employment and training plans;
cooperate with other EU countries to ensure that the programme is implemented correctly;
make available to the beneficiaries information on their rights and obligations, including the conditions under which they can participate in the programme.
In addition, the European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation of the programme and ensuring that beneficiaries receive the information they need;
coordinating the programme with other relevant bodies and bodies in the field of employment, training and training; and
providing technical assistance to beneficiaries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
Gender Equality Grant is part of the European Social Fund (ESF). The EGF was first launched in 2009 and has been extended several times, most recently in 2016.
For more information, see:
Gender equality and equal opportunities (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
   
Directives (EU, Euratom) 2018/1321 and (EC) 2017 /1371 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States of the Union on Equal Opportunities For Gender Equality (EGF) (OJ L 131, 28.5.2018, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Directive (EU), Euratom have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 63:
Transparency register for the European Parliament and Council of the European Union (Euratom)
Transparency record for the Council and the EU institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852 on a mandatory transparency register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision aims to ensure the transparency of EU institutions’ policies, programmes and activities.
The regulation sets out the rules and procedures for the management of the transparency register.
KEY POINTS
The register is maintained by the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Internal Audiovisual Affairs (DIA).
It is open to applicants and registrants.
Applicants must:
present, if requested, supporting material demonstrating that the information relating to their registration continues to be accurate;
cooperate sincerely and constructively in accordance with the procedures set out in Annex III.
In the context of monitoring or of an investigation by the Secretariat, registrants must in particular:
provide evidence that they have observed the code of conduct;
make their views known in writing;
notify the secretariat if they believe they have been the subject of a serious breach of confidentiality.
If a registrant concerns by an investigation is not cooperating sincerely or constructively with the investigation, it may close the investigation and remove the registration concerned from the register. The registrant concerned may, after having given the registrant the possibility to make their views know in writing, decide to reopen the investigation.
An annual report on the functioning of the register is published by the DIA.

ensure transparency in EU policies and programmes;
promote dialogue between EU institutions and the public;
improve the quality of information available to the public.
For more information, see:
‘The Transparency Register’ on the Euratom website.

Recognition: the recognition by an institution that an applicant is a permanent representation.
Confidentiality: the right of an institution to keep confidential information.

Summary 64:
Budgetary cooperation between the European Union institutions
Budgets and budgetary cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on budgetary discipline between the Institutions on budgetary matters
Decision (EU) 2021/2027 on the conclusion of the Agreement on the implementation of budgetary discipline
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to implement budgetary discipline, to improve the functioning of the annual budgetary procedure and cooperation with the European Parliament and the Council.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s budget is the subject of a multiannual financial framework. It covers the period 2021-2027. The agreement establishes a roadmap for the introduction of new own resources that are sufficient to cover the repayment of a European Union Recovery Instrument established under Council Regulation (EURI) 2020/2094 (1) (the "EURI Regulation"). The annexes to this agreement form an integral part thereof.
In order to ensure full information as well as transparency and visibility as regards the loan component of the EU Recovery Instrument, the Commission will provide detailed information about loans provided to Member States under the EURI. The Commission will also provide the EU Parliament and Council with a financial forecast for the remainder of the financial year.
Member States will be required to provide the Commission with:
a draft budget for the current financial year;
a report on the progress made by the Member States in implementing the EU budget;
an annual report on their own resources; and
a financial forecast.
the Commission will publish its draft estimates for the following the draft budget.
Cooperation between the institutions
The institutions will:
meet on an ad hoc basis if one institution provides a reasoned request. They will jointly assess the matter with a view to finding common ground within 3 weeks of the request for a meeting.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The Agreement entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Commission is responsible for implementing budgetary discipline. This is achieved by:
establishing budgetary discipline;
ensuring sound financial management;
implementing the European Green Deal for Europe (see summary).
The EURI Regulation establishes a framework for own resources for the EU.
For more information, see:
‘The EU budget’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 65:
Economic and social support for COVID-19 crisis: financial instruments
Economic and Social support for the COVID–19 crisis
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2018 — financial instruments for the economic and social response to COVID‑19 crisis (OJ L 347, 31.12.2018, pp. 1-8)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a financial instrument for the EU’s economic and economic response to the COVIC-19 economic crisis.
KEY POINTS
The exceptional situation caused by COVID‐19, which is beyond the control of EU countries, has caused significant disturbances to economic activity which are reflected in a steep decline in gross domestic product and a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities.
In order to prevent further deterioration of the economy, employment and social cohesion and to boost a sustainable and resilient recovery of economic activity, the European Union (EU) is putting in place an exceptional and coordinated programme of measures.
The measures are designed to:
provide financial support to EU countries that have been particularly hard hit by the crisis;
support the recovery of the tourism sector;
contribute to the creation of jobs, growth and employment in rural areas in addressing the impact of COVID’19;
promote the development of sustainable and inclusive growth and jobs;
improve the quality of life in the labour market;
strengthen social cohesion;
increase the capacity of EU citizens;
inform the European Commission of the measures to be put in place.
Scope
The financial instruments cover:
economic support for EU countries experiencing a significant deterioration in their economic situation;
financial support for tourism and tourism sectors;
investment support for rural areas;
economic and investment support for agriculture and development;
social support for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
support for the sustainable development of rural areas.
Financing
The instruments are financed by:
the EU budget;
the European Regional Development Fund (EDF) and the European Investment Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EIB);
the contribution of the European Social Fund for Rural Development (ESFD) and its Member States’ contributions to the EU budget.
Funding
The instrument is financed up to an amount of EUR 750 million in 2018 prices on the basis of the empowerment provided for in Article 5 of the Own Resources Decision.
EU countries are required to provide the Commission with a report on the implementation of the instruments by 31 October 2022. This report must include information on the economic situation in their countries and the measures they have taken to cope with the crisis. The Commission must prepare a report for the European Parliament and the Council on the progress made by the Member States in implementing the instruments.
Implementation
The Commission is responsible for:
implementing the instruments;
monitoring their implementation;
providing technical and administrative assistance;
ensuring that the instruments are properly implemented;
maintaining and improving the quality and efficiency of the information and communication systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID crisis has had a major impact on the economy of the EU and on social cohesion in general.
For more information, see:
‘Economic and economic support’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* COVID: crisis in the context of the crisis in Cyprus (European External Action Service). COVID refers to the crisis caused by the collapse of the Cypriot financial union (COVID).
* Tourism sector: tourism is one of the most important economic sectors in the EU, which has suffered a significant decline in GDP and has been hit particularly hard due to the deterioration in consumer demand.

Summary 66:
Sanctions in the Stability and Growth Pact for countries whose currency is the euro (SGP)
Sanctions on countries with a currency other than the euro
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1466/97 — preventive and corrective measures in the euro area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system of sanctions for Member States whose currency, the euro, is not the euro.
It aims to ensure that Member States’ economic and monetary policies comply with the stability and growth pact.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all Member States. It does not apply to:
non-euro area Member States;
euro area countries that do not use the euro;
those that have adopted the euro as their national currency.
The Regulation applies to Member States that are not euro area countries.
Penalties
Member States must lodge with the European Commission an interest-bearing deposit amounting to 0.2 % of their gross domestic product (GDP) in the preceding year. The amount of the deposit must not exceed 0.5 % of the GDP of the Member State concerned. The Member State must inform the Commission of any non-interest-bearing deposits it has lodged. The Commission may, within 20 days of adoption of the Council’s decision, recommend that a Member State lodge a deposit. If the Council adopts a decision requiring a lodgement, the Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee on the basis of the report. The report must be sent to the Council within 10 days of the decision.
If the Commission decides to abrogate some or all of its decisions, it must return the deposit.
Fines
The Member State in question must pay a fine of at least 0.3 % of its GDP in the previous year. This fine may not exceed 1 % of GDP. The fine must be made up by:
the Member State that lodged the deposit;
the Commission;
a Member State which has not lodged a deposit; or
any other Member State with a deficit over the reference value.
In exceptional circumstances, the Council may decide to impose a fine on an EU country that intentionally or by serious negligence misrepresents deficit and debt data relevant for the application of Articles 121 or 126 TFEU and for the applying the excessive deficit procedure annexed to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
The Council may also impose fines on Member States which do not meet the medium-term budgetary objective (MRA). The fine may be:
0.2% of GDP;
1.5% of the Gross Domestic Product;
2.5%.
The fine may exceed 3% if the fine exceeds the amount of an interest deposit lodged with the Commission, or 3.5%, depending on the nature of the irregularity.
Report from the Commission
The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation of the SGP;
assisting the Member States in their implementation;
providing on-site inspections;
making recommendations and warnings.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The SGP entered into force on 1 July 1997.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The Stability and growth Pact (SGF) is the cornerstone of the EU’S economic and financial rules. It is designed to ensure the proper functioning of the single currency and the soundness of the monetary union.
For more information, see:
Sustainable growth and jobs (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Excessive deficit: an excessive deficit is a deficit that is greater than 2 % of gross domestic products (GDI).
* Fiscal deficit: a deficit which is larger than the GDI.
* Misrepresentation: a government deficit over a given budget year.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 2014/97 of 21 June 1997 laying down a system for the enforcement of the preventive and the corrective parts of the Stability & Growth Pact (OJ L 127, 21.5.1997, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1466 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 04.02.2016



Summary 67:
EU statistics — quality of data and metadata (2014-2020)
EU statistics – quality of information (2014 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 176/2008 on the quality of EU statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the production and dissemination of European Union (EU) statistics.
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of the EU’s statistical data.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must transmit to the European Commission (Eurostat) statistical data on the following topics:
housing arrangements,
type of living quarters and
occupancy status of conventional dwellings;
relationships between household members and households;
tenure status of households.
The regulation sets out technical specifications for the topics and breakdowns of the topics, as well as of their breakdowns, in accordance with the technical specifications set out in the regulation. These technical specifications must be adopted by the national and EU statistical authorities and transmitted to Eurostat in electronic form.
Each EU country must ensure that the data transmitted conform to the following technical specifications:
the technical specifications of the topic as required by this regulation as set out by the European Statistics Code of Practice adopted on 24 February 2005 by the Statistical Programme Committee, established by Council Decision 89/382/EEC, Euratom (4) and attached to the Recommendation of the Commission on the independence, integrity and accountability of the European and Community statistical authorities;
the breakdown of the data and the metadata.
the format of the metadata and the quality reports.
Eurostat:
transmits the validated data to the Commission (e.g. in electronic format;
assesses the quality;
publishes quality reports on the statistical data transmitted;
establishes a quality report system.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for producing and disseminating EU statistics. It is assisted by the statistical programme committee.
For more information, see:
‘Quality of European statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Non-derived topics: these include:
living arrangements (i.e. the current place; or place of usual residence one year prior to the census;
housing status: the number of occupants of a housing unit;
ownership status of a conventional dwelling;
units of housing;

Summary 70:
The EU’s own resources
The EU's own resources (OJ L 253 of 7 June 2007)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 609/2014 on the system of the European Communities’ European Communities' own resources and repealing Council Decision 2007/436/EC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The regulation establishes a system of own resources for the European Union (EU).
The purpose of the system is to provide the EU with the means to meet its financial needs.
It aims to ensure that the EU is able to finance its own budget and its own activities.
KEY POINTS
The system is based on the Gross National Income (GNI) keys.
The GNI keys are based on a uniform call rate of EUR 0,80 per kilogram.
There are 3 categories of own resource:
cash resources: the gross national income of the EU countries;
other own resources: revenue from the EU budget and other sources of revenue, such as taxes and other taxes;
investment resources: loans from the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Regional Development Fund (ERF) and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (ERDF);
administrative resources: grants and loans from EU countries.
In addition, the EU may call on the Member States to provisionally provide the difference between the overall assets and the cash resource requirements, in proportion (‘pro rata’) to the estimated budget revenue of each of them. This is done in accordance with the GNI key.
This is done on the basis of the average annual gross domestic product (GDP) of each Member State.
Borrowings: the EU borrows up to a maximum amount of EUR 390 million for expenditure and up to an average annual amount of € 1.5 billion for revenue.
Repayments: repayments are made on the principal of the loans. The amount of the loan must not exceed 7.5 % of the total amount of expenditure.
EU countries must repay the loans within 3 years of the end of the borrowing period. The repayments must cover the entire volume of funds borrowed under the empowerment of the Commission. The repayment period should not exceed 3 years from the date on which the borrowing of funds ceased.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 28 February 2001.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is the largest economy in the world. It has a budget of over €1.2 trillion.
For more information, see:
‘The European Community’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, No 608/2014 of 28 February 2014 on the System of European Community's European Communities's Own Resources and repealing Decisions 2007/434/EC, 2007/438/EC and 2007/437/EC (O J L 253, 7.6.2007, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 608/14 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 71:
Information on tyres: fuel efficiency, wet grip and external rolling noise
Information on tyre performance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/858 on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It sets out minimum requirements for the information to be provided on tyres.
It aims to improve the visibility of tyres on the market and to reduce energy consumption.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to tyres of the category C3 (C3) and C4 (C4) tyres. It does not apply to tyres designed for use in severe snow conditions.
EU countries must ensure that the information on the tyre label and on the pictograms for other parameters, such as rolling resistance and wet grip, correspond to the documentation provided by the supplier based on test results and calculations.
Manufacturers and importers must:
make the information available on their websites and on their products’ product database;
ensure that the label and pictograms on the product database are accurate;
assist the European Commission and national authorities in carrying out their tasks under this regulation and the relevant delegated acts, including enforcement of the regulation;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to carry out its tasks.
National authorities must check that the classes for rolling resistance, wet-grip and external-rolling noise displayed on the label, as well as the pictures of the tyre’s, correspond with the documentation they have received from the supplier.
In addition, they must check whether the classifications and classifications are consistent with the specifications set out in the regulation.
Labelling requirements must be accurate.
The information must include:
a description of the tyres’ characteristics;
the class of tyre;
an indication of whether the tyre is a tyre for use on snow-covered roads;
a pictogram for the class of wet grip;
information on the class and class classification of the external-roll-noise class.
If a supplier has not ensured the accuracy of the label or the pictogram, it may be subject to a penalty.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYORK?
It applies from 26 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Tyre label’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work website.
* KEY TERMS
* Tyre label: a label on a vehicle that describes the characteristics of a tyre and its characteristics, including the class, class characteristics and class characteristics, of its tyres and the class characteristics of its components. It must be printed on the upper part of the vehicle and include the following information:
the tyre type and class specifications;
type of tyre, class, classification and class of tyres;
class of tyres, class specifications and class classes of components;
classes of components, classes and components of components of the product;
number of components and classes of parts;
characteristics of the components and parts of the products;
types of parts and components, including:
internal dividers;
materials;
name of the type of tyre and type of components.

Summary 73:
Electricity market: rules on competition and market access for energy consumers (REFERENCES)
Electricity markets for energy users
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/943 on competition in the electricity market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve competition in electricity markets in the European Union (EU).
It recasts and repeals Directive 2004/36/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
the electricity market in the EU;
the transmission and distribution system operators;
energy consumers;
citizen energy communities.
It does not apply to the wholesale electricity market, which is regulated by the European Commission.
Regulatory authority
The EU’s energy market is governed by a single regulatory authority. This authority is independent of any market participants and comparison tool operators. It is responsible for:
establishing and maintaining a competitive market for electricity;
monitoring the market;
ensuring that market participants’ prices are fair and transparent;
providing market participants with information on electricity prices;
assisting market participants in the development and operation of market-based electricity price comparison tools.
Rules on market access and transparency
EU countries must ensure that:
electricity consumers are treated like active customers in accordance with point (e) of Article 15(2) of the directive;
they have the right to be directly informed of the offers and savings available on the competitive market, in particular relating to dynamic electricity price contracts;
market participants are provided with assistance to respond to and benefit from price-based offers.
they are guaranteed access to smart meters*;
there is no discrimination in terms of billing, billing or billing terms;
all consumers have access to the information they need to make informed decisions about their energy consumption.
The European Commission may decide to examine the case of a non-compliance of a regulatory authority with a decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It applies from 1 January 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Electricity market: a market where electricity is sold and traded on a regulated market.
* Distribution system operator: the body responsible for the operation of a transmission or distribution system.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 75:
Paris Agreement: implementation of the EU’s climate-neutrality objective and climate-resilient development goal (Paris Agreement)
Paris agreement: implementation and adaptation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/841 on the implementation and assessment of the Paris Agreement on climate change
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU-wide climate targets for the period 2030-2050.
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by at least 55% by 2030 compared to 1990 levels.
KEY POINTS
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change and to delivering on the agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
The Paris Agreement sets out a long-term temperature goal in point (a) of Article 2(1) of the UNFCCC, and aims to strengthen the global response to the threat of climate change by increasing the ability to adapt to the adverse impacts resulting from climate change, by making finance flows consistent with a pathway towards low greenhouse gas emissions and climate resilience.
To achieve this objective, the EU and its Member States must:
reduce GHG emissions and removals from land use, land use and forestry in the 2030 climate and energy framework;
ensure that they are reduced economy-wide and domestically by a minimum of 55% compared to the 1990 levels;
adaptate to climate change.
The Commission must review relevant EU legislation in order to enable the achievement of the target set out of this regulation.
By 30 June 2021, the Commission will review relevant Union legislation to enable it to achieve the target and the climate neutrality objective.
This review will include:
a review of the greenhouse gas budget for the 2030–2050 period;
the assessment of progress towards the EU's climate neutrality objective;
measures to adapt the economy and the economy to the climate change impacts;
implementation of the climate-recovery goal.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation applies from 30 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Climate Change Convention (UNFCCC) has been amended several times, most recently by Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020/852 on the inclusion of greenhouse gas emission reductions from land-use and land use-related activities in the EU climate framework.
For more information, see:
Paris Agreement (United Nations).
KEY TERMS
* Greenhouse gas emissions: greenhouse gases emitted by burning fossil fuels.
* Land-use emissions: emissions from the production, transport and storage of greenhouse gases (GHGs).
* Removals: emissions of GHGs emitted from land and land-based activities (i.e. from land, water and energy).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2018 of 30 May 2018 on the application of the provisions of the Protocol on the Implementation and Assessment of the Agreement on the Climate-Resilience of the Parties to the Convention on the Paris Climate Change and repealing Regulation (EC) No 529/2013/EU (OJ L 156, 19.5.2018, pp. 1–5)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU/842 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the objectives and actions of the EU4health programme, which aims to improve the health and well-being of people in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is designed to:
improve the quality of life of people living in the EU;

informative activities.
the general public of the specific objectives;
research and development programmes;
emergency support operations.

promoting the use of the internet and social media;
improving the availability of health information to individuals;
raising public awareness about the importance and importance of preventing and responding to disease;

developing and disseminating information and training programmes for health care professionals and health care workers;
establishing a network of experts and experts in the area of health and social care.
Financing

€1.1 billion from the EU budget for 2019-2023;

EU funds from the EPPO for the duration of the Programme.

In addition, the programme supports transnational projects for collaboration, networking, mobility and internationalisation, including through residency programmes, touring, events, exhibitions and festivals.
Projects
A project may be awarded a Seal of Excellence label as defined in point (45) of Article 2 of the Common Provisions Regulation for 2021–2027 under the Programme where it complies with the following cumulative conditions:
it has been assessed in a call for proposals under the programme;
it meets the minimum quality requirements of that call; and
it cannot be financed under that call for proposal due to budgetary constraints.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The ‘2014-2020 Programme’ is part of the broader ‘EU 2020-2020’ package of EU policies and programmes designed to promote culture and creativity.
For more information, see:
‘The EU 2020 programme’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 78:
European Institute of Innovation and Technology (EIT) 2021-2027 (SIA 2021-2020)
European institute of innovation and technology (Eit) 2021 - 2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 establishing the European Institute of Innovations and Technology
Sustainable Innovation and Entrepreneurship 2020-2021 — Strategic Agenda for the EIT
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE SIA?
The regulation establishes the strategic objectives and the priorities of the European institute of innovative and technological development (EIIT) for the seven-year period from 2021 to 2027.
The SIA sets out the priority fields and the strategy for the institute’s activities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EIT is open to countries (and regions in those countries) with modest and moderate innovation performance as classified in the European Innovation Scoreboard (EIS) to engage in knowledge triangle activities as part of a Knowledge Triangle (KIC) community. Through the RIS, the EIC has expanded its activities across Europe and offers opportunities for countries (or regions in countries) that have moderate and moderate EU innovation performance to participate in research and innovation activities in the knowledge triangle.
EIT activities include:
supporting the innovation and entrepreneurial capacity of higher education institutions (HEIs) and research institutes (RIs);
promoting the knowledge-based economy (KIs) of research and development (RDI);
creating a knowledge triangle ecosystem;
developing and disseminating innovative products and services;
agriculture;
research and innovation (IoD);
enhancing knowledge and entrepreneurship;
creativity and entrepreneurship.
Programmes
The aim to:
enhance the capacity of HEIs and RDI institutions;
support the research- and innovation-based economies of Europe;
encourage the development of innovative products, services and technologies;
promote the development and dissemination of knowledge and technology;
provide a platform for the exchange of best practices and best practices;
facilitate the sharing of best practice and best practice;
increase the visibility and visibility of innovation in Europe.
RIS activities
In addition, KICs must:
develop and implement innovative research, development, innovation and entrepreneurship strategies;
ensure that their research activities are open to the public, private and public sectors;
create and disseminate information and awareness-raising campaigns;
make use of the resources and expertise of the RIS;
organise and implement research projects, including research projects and research programmes, in line with the European Research and Innovation (R&I) Framework Programme (ERP) and the EU Innovation Strategy (RIS);
develop, implement and promote research and technological innovation (R & T) programmes and projects;
establish and implement knowledge-driven research and entrepreneurship programmes, including R & T programmes, and develop and implement R&T programmes, projects and projects in the areas covered by the R&I Framework Programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The European Institute for Innovation and Technological Development has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has established a European Innovation Institute since 2011. Its main objective is to contribute to the creation of a European innovation economy. It is based on the EIS, which is designed to improve the quality of life of people and improve the competitiveness of the economy.
For more information, see:
European Innovation Institute (European Commission).

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine products — description, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain aromatised wines and spirit drinks (Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011)
Aroma-based wine products: description and presentation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the definition, description*, presentation*, labelling*, protection and marketing of certain aromatic wine products*.
It aims to ensure that the products marketed in the EU are marketed correctly and that consumers can easily identify them.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
alcoholic wine products;
alcohol-based spirit drinks;
sparkling wine;
wine-based spirits.
It does not apply to spirits, sparkling wine or wine-based beer.
The regulations cover:
wine, sparkling wines and sparkling wines;
spirits;
whiskey;
vodka;
pomegranate juice;
bourbon;
coconut water;
white wine.
Labelling
The products must bear the following information:
the alcohol content;
the alcoholic strength by volume;
a description of the alcohol;
an indication of the wine’s alcohol content.
An alcoholic beverage not fulfilling the requirements laid down in this regulation must not be described, presented or marketed in a way that implies that it is an alcoholic beverage.
In addition to this information, the product must be accompanied by a description and a description of its alcohol content, which must be expressed as invert sugar.
the product must also include the alcohol content by volume and the alcoholic strength.
A description and description must include the alcoholic denomination and the sales denomination.
If a product does not fulfil the requirements of more than one sales denomination, the use of only one of those sales denominations is authorised, except where Annex II provides otherwise.
EU countries and the European Commission must notify each other of any information necessary for the application of this regulation and for complying with the international obligations concerning the aromatisation of wine products.
Protection
The European Commission may, by means of implementing acts, on its own initiative, decide to cancel the protection of existing geographical designations referred to in the regulation if they do not comply with point (3) of Article 2.
Existing geographical indications
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend or derogate from this regulation.

Summary 80:
Fishing quotas for certain species of fish in the EU’s outermost waters
Fisheries quotas for various species of fishes in the outermost seas
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on fishing quotas for fish stocks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU-wide fishing quotas* for certain fish stocks* in EU waters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing quotas:
for certain species* of fish (e.g. bluefin tuna, swordfish, molluscs and swordfish) in EU and international waters;
fisheries for certain types of crustaceans, such as swordfish;
fish caught in the Faroe Islands, Greenland, Iceland and Norway;
for some species of sharks caught in certain areas of the North Atlantic Ocean.
It does not apply to the following species:
whiting (Euphausia superba);
marine mussels (Eucalyptus, mussels, skates and skates);
dolphine mussels;
spotted mussels.
EU countries have exclusive access to the quota pool until 31 March 2020.
Specific rules apply to certain species and categories of fish, including:
bluefin tuna
swordfish
molluscan mussels
swordfishing for swordfish
marine pelagic mussels and scallops.
European seabass
European pelagic fish.
Fishing limits
The European Union (EU countries must:
limit the quantities of fish they can take in their respective waters, in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex II to the regulation;
not exceed the quotas set out in Annex III;
limit catches of certain species;
allow certain species to be caught only in the areas specified by SPRFMO.
The EU countries must ensure that fish stocks are not overfished, and that the quantities taken are not exceeded.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 May 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
The CCAMLR Convention (Convention on the Conservation of Atlantic Marine Living Resources (CCAMLR) is a multiannual agreement between the European Commission and the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea and the International Maritime Organization (CAMLR). It aims to conserve and restore the diversity of marine life in the Atlantic Ocean and to encourage sustainable fishing.
For more information, see:
‘Protecting the Atlantic’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fishing quota: the amount of fish that can be caught in each EU country's quota pool for a given year. It is divided into quotas for:
target species, i.e. fish stocks;
catch limit: the number of fish allowed in each quota pool;
total catch limit (TAC): the total quantity of fish permitted in each TAC.

SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 — emergency macro–economic assistance for the country of Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It provides for the European Union to provide financial assistance to the government of Ukraine for the period 2018-2022.
KEY POINTS
The decision authorises the EU to provide up to €3.5 billion in emergency macro‑financial assistance (EMFAs) to the country.
The EU’s EMFAs are subject to economic policy conditions, to be set out in a memorandum of understanding (MOU) with the Ukrainian authorities. The MOU sets out the economic policy and financial conditions to be followed by the European Commission. These include:
the establishment of a sustainable external financing situation for Ukraine;
continuing central bank independence and a flexible exchange rate;
safeguarding financial stability while recovering the costs from bank resolutions;
promoting governance and anti-corruption measures to preserve and deepen recent gains.

Summary 82:
Management, conservation and control measures applicable in the Convention area of the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT) area
Management and conservation measures applicable on the Mediterranean swordfish
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2107 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 November 2017 laying down management, conservation, control measures for the conservation, management and control of the Mediterranean Swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down measures to protect and conserve the Mediterranean Sea swordfish (Swordfish) species.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to fishing vessels 15 metres in length overall and below flying an EU country’s flag.
It does not apply to:
recreational fisheries;
fishing vessels of less than 15 metres;
recreational fishing vessels of more than 10 metres; and
fishermen engaged in commercial fishing activities.
EU countries must:
design and implement a scientific monitoring approach to collect the information on the activities of pelagic longline vessels of 15 metres or below flying their flag;
ensure that these vessels comply with the rules laid down in the regulation;
issue their national scientific observers with an official identification document.
Monitoring
The EU country concerned must design and implement:
a scientific monitoring system to collect information on:
the fishing activities of fishing vessels;
the number and type of fishing gear used;
catches of swordfish;
records of the number of catches;
of the number and types of vessels.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMIMPORTATION OF INFORMATION
Each EU country must send quarterly reports to the European Commission of all catches of Mediterranean sword fish made by authorised vessels flying its flag, unless such information is sent on a monthly basis. These reports must include:
categories of fish caught;
type and quantity of fish;
quantity of fish landed and transshipment;
number of vessels registered in the EU;
total number of vessels in the fishery;
frequency of catches and quantities of fish transhipment.
The Commission must ensure that:
all fishing vessels flying the EU flag are registered in accordance with the ICCAT rules;
all catches are recorded and reported to the Commission on a quarterly basis;
there is a limit on the total number of fish that can be caught in a given year. This limit is set out in Annex II of the regulation.
In addition, EU countries must ensure:
monitoring measures are in place to prevent, detect and control illegal fishing activities;
monitor and report on the fishing gear and equipment used; and, where appropriate, the number, type and quantity used.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
The convention entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
  IUU (International Union for Unlawful Trade in Tunas)
The European Union is party to the International Convention on the Conservation and Management of Tunas.
For more information, see:
‘The EU and the Swordfish’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Swordfish: a species of fish found in the Atlantic Ocean.
* Longline: a type of longline used to catch swordfish.

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework (MFF) for the period 2014-2020
Multiannial financial framework for the year 2014-20
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 establishing a multiannual Financial Framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) Multiannual Financing Framework.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s financial resources are used effectively and efficiently.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The MFF covers the period from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2023.
The framework covers:
the general budget of the EU;
the European cohesion policy envelopes (e.g. the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund, the Asylum and Migration Fund and the Internal Security Fund);
the common foreign and security policy (CFSP) and the common security and defence policy (CSDP) envelopes.
Specific instruments
The European Union Solidarity Fund (EU-SDF) provides financial assistance to EU countries in the event of a natural or man-made disaster. It may be mobilised only in relation to an amending or annual budget.
In exceptional cases and if the remaining financial resources available in the EU Solidarity fund in the year of occurrence of the disaster, as defined in the relevant basic act, are not sufficient to cover the amount of assistance considered necessary by the European Parliament and the Council, the Commission may propose that the difference be financed through the annual amounts available for the following year.
Emergency Aid Reserve
The Emergency Aid Reserve is intended to allow for a rapid response to specific aid requirements of third countries following events which are not covered by the MFF. It can be used in the case of:
natural or manmade disasters;
humanitarian emergencies;
international crises;
terrorism;
climate change;
natural disasters.
Contingency Margin
The regulation sets a contingency margin of 2 % of the total appropriations for payments required for the current and future financial years. This margin must not result in a call-in rate for own resources that exceeds the own resources ceiling set in accordance with Decision 2007/436/EC, Euratom.
Rights and obligations of EU countries
EU countries have the right to participate in the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The EIB has the following rights and obligations:
participation in the EIB;
recourse to the Contingency margin;
reimbursement of payments made by EU countries;
cooperation with the International Monetary Fund (IMF);
reciprocal access to the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
recovery of costs incurred under the ERDF.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the largest economy in the world and has the largest budget in terms of gross domestic product (GDP).
For more information, see:
‘The European Fund for Strategic Investments’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratox, Euronews, Euronext, Euromonitor, Eurobudget, Euromodal and Euratom Financial Regulation (OJ L 183, 1.1.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 596 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 84:
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFAF)
European Maritime & Fisheries Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 establishing the European Maritime and Fishery Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s Mediterranean and Fisheries Facility (EMF) and lays down the priorities of the EMF, its budget and the specific rules for providing EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the EMFAF.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EMF is responsible for:
fishing and aquaculture in the EU;
monitoring and evaluation of the EU fishing fleet;
investment in the fishing fleet.
EMF budget
The regulation establishes a budget of EUR 6.108 million for the period 2021-2027. This budget is divided between the EU Member States and the European Economic and Social Fund (EEF), the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the Fund for Agriculture and Rural Development (FARE). The EMF also supports research, innovation and investments on board EU fishing vessels.
Specific objectives
The main objectives of EMF are to:
promote the sustainable use of EU fishing resources;
protect the marine environment;
improve the quality of fish protein produced in the Union with high quality standards and available for consumers at affordable prices;
contribute to food security and nutrition;
encourage the sustainable development in the European fishing industry;
support regional and local development.
Funding
EU Member States must ensure that EMF projects are compatible with the conservation objectives of fish stocks and the sustainable fishing practices of the CFP (the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP).
Specific conditions for EMFisheries are governed by the following conditions:
the EMF must:
be based in an area where there is a high level of sustainable fishing;
be managed by EU countries;
not contribute to overcapacity or overfishing;
take into account regional and/or local challenges, as appropriate, and may identify intermediate bodies in accordance with Article 71(3) of Regulation (Euratom 71(2) of the EEF;
have a minimum budget of at least EUR 6 108 million.
Eligible projects
The following projects may be supported by EMF funding:
research and innovation projects;
fisheries-related research and development projects.
Investment in fishing fleet
The European Union Member States are responsible for ensuring that investments in fishing fleets are:
consistent with the objectives of the common fisheries policy (CPF);
contributing to the sustainable management of fishing stocks;
promoting sustainable fishing techniques;
providing information and communication to the public;
ensuring that investment in fishing vessels is compatible with CFP objectives;
developing sustainable fishing methods and techniques.
Other EMF activities
The Member States may support certain other EMF operations, such as:
scientific research and technological development;
research on sustainable fishing technologies;
scientific and technological innovation;
training of fishery personnel;
the establishment of research centres and research institutes;
exchange of best practices and best practices in the field of fisheries;
information, communication and publicity campaigns.
EU funding
Member States must also ensure that:
investments made by the Member States do not contribute to the over-exploitation of EU fish stocks, and that they do not result in over-fishing, over-harvesting or underfishing of fish products;
there is no risk that investments will contribute to an over-abundance of fish stock or to an increase in fishing capacity.
The Commission must ensure:
that EMF investments do not increase overcapacity;
that investments are not likely to contribute to or contribute to excessive fishing stocks.
In addition, Member States have to ensure that investments are in line with the principles of sustainable management and sustainable fishing.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a member of the Convention on the Functioning of the European Union (CFSP). The CFP is the cornerstone of the Lisbon strategy for the EU and the EU fisheries policy.
For more information, see:
‘Fishery and Aquaculture’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Overfishing: a phenomenon that occurs when fishing stocks are overfished or under-fished.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2011/2021 of 21 December 2011 establishing the Mediterranean and Faroe Islands Fund and laying down the general principles for the European Fisheries Fund.
