Summary 85:
Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish Species (CMSS)
Conservation and management of straddling fish stocks and highly migratory fish species
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 98/392/EC on the conclusion, on behalf of the European Union, of the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea and the Agreement for the Implementation of that Convention relating to the conservation and management on the conservation of species under the Convention
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The decision approves the EU’s participation in the UN Convention on International Trade in Tariffs and Trade in Fish (CITTF) and the agreement for the implementation of the CMSS.
The agreement aims to:
contribute to the long-term conservation and sustainable management of species of fish and to the sustainable use of their resources;
promote international cooperation in the field of fisheries;
ensure that the International Association for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (IATTC) is established.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The convention applies to fishing for:
whitetip sharks and bluefin tuna;
sea turtles;
marine mammals;
whales and porpoises;
fishing gear and equipment;
seabirds.
EU countries must:
carry on-board and, when sea turtle interactions occur, employ the necessary equipment (e.g. de-hookers, line cutters and scoop nets) for the prompt release of sea turtles incidentally caught;
when the majority of hooks fish at depths shallower than 100 metres, employ one of the following two mitigation measures: large circle hooks or the use only of finfish for bait;
report any interactions to the national authorities.
Fisheries must report annually by 15 June for the preceding year on their implementation of Article 16 and the Guidelines of the Food and Agriculture Organisation of the UN to Reduce Sea Turtle Mortality in Fishing Operations (2009) (18) for turtles, including information collected on interaction with turtles during fishing activities managed under the convention. The Commission must forward that information to the IATTC Secretariat by 30 June.
Member States must also:
report annually to the Commission on the number of discards and releases of whitetip shark, indicating the status (dead or alive), including those released under paragraph 2.3 of the convention;
record, inter alia, through the observer programmes, the number and status of sea turtle discards, as well as the status of any sea turtles accidentally caught.
They must also report to the European Commission on:
the number and type of fishing gear used;
the type and quantity of fish caught, and the type of gear used, in accordance with the Convention;
any interactions between vessels and turtles.
Monitoring and reporting
The Convention requires Member States:
to:
monitor and report the number, type, quantity and nature of catches and discards of sharks;
to report on interactions between fishing gear and sea turtles; and
to inform the International Maritime Rescue Coordination Centre (IMRCC) of any interactions that occur.
In addition, Member States must ensure that:
fisheries are equipped with the appropriate equipment to detect and respond to interactions between sea turtles and fishing gear;
all fishing gear, equipment and equipment used on board vessels must be in line with international standards;
ships and fishing equipment must be equipped with a satellite-based monitoring system to detect, report and report any interactions with sea turtles that occur;
if a vessel encounters a sea turtle, it must immediately notify the appropriate maritime rescue coordination centre, observer provider and the International Transport Workers’ Federation (ETF) to ensure that it is immediately notified and provided with a report on actions undertaken.
Each Member State must also take the necessary measures to inspect and verify the well sealed for the first time.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
 
    ‘The Decision and the Agreement’ entered into force on 1 July 1998.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a contracting party to the CITTF.
For more information, see:
International Convention on Tariff and Trade-Related Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CATTF) (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Whitefin tuna: the largest fish in the world and the most migratory.
* Bluefin tuna.

Summary 86:
Delegated acts: EU-wide dialogue between the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of EU legislation
Delegating acts: European Union dialogue between EU institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on Delegated Acts
Decision (EU) 2016/852 — conclusion of the agreement on delegated acts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that delegated or implementing acts are implemented within the limits of the EU’s legal instruments.
The decision concludes the agreement with the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The European Parliament (European Parliament) and Council (Council) have the power to adopt delegated acts. These acts enable the Commission to adopt EU legislation. The delegated acts are designed to:
improve the efficiency of the legislative process;
ensure that EU legislation is compatible with EU law;
provide the Commission with all the necessary expertise and information it needs to implement EU legislation;
contribute to the development of EU law.
Scope
The delegated acts may be adopted by the Commission or by the EU countries’ competent authorities. They may include:
basic acts;
implementing acts; and
legislative acts.

EU delegated and implementing acts (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Delegating act: a delegated act adopted by an EU country on behalf of another EU country.
* Implementing act: an act adopted under the urgency procedure.

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative procedure: rules on the registration of proposed citizens’ initiatives (CIP)
Citizen’s initiative procedure — rules on registering and publishing proposed citizens' initiatives
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the registering and publication of citizens' initiative procedures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes a procedure for the registration, publication and dissemination of proposals for legal acts of the European Union (EU) for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) and on the Council under Article 241 TFEU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to proposals for a legal act of the Union for the purposes of:
improving the functioning of the internal market in goods and services in the EU;
protecting consumers’ rights and fundamental freedoms;
promoting the protection of intellectual property rights;
ensuring a high level of protection of personal data.
It does not apply to:
proposals for the implementation of EU laws, regulations or administrative acts;
the implementation of treaties, regulations and administrative acts adopted by the EU countries;
reports to the European Commission;
actions adopted by EU countries.
Requirements for registration and publication
Citizens must submit a proposal to the Commission with a description of the objectives of the initiative and the contact details of the organisers.
The initiative must include:
a description of its aims and objectives;
an estimate of the resources required to carry out the initiative;
information on the sources of support and funding for the initiative.
the contact details and contact details for the organisers
the names, postal addresses and e-mail addresses of the contact persons.
Organisers must:
register the initiative with the Commission and publish it on their website;
provide, for the register and where appropriate on the website, regularly updated information on their sources of funding.
Registration of a proposal
The proposal must meet the following conditions:
it must be submitted by the organisers:
in writing, in no more than 500 characters;
in a language easily understood by the Commission
in the format set out in Annex II;
within 5 working days of receiving the proposal
inform the Commission of its intention to act on the initiative, in the form of a request inviting it to act;
give the Commission a copy of the proposal.
If the Commission considers that the initiative does not meet the above conditions, it may refuse to act. The Commission must inform the organisers of the reasons for its decision and inform them of the consequences of their decision.
In addition, the Commission must provide the organisers with information and informal advice on how to register and publish the initiative on its website. It must also provide information on the information contained in its online register.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It applies from 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
CITIZEN’S INITIATIVE PROCEDURE
The CIP is one of a number of legal instruments designed to facilitate the exchange of information between the EU and non-EU countries in order to improve the quality of life of citizens.
For more information, see:
‘Citizens' initiative procedure’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the Registration and Publication of Citizens’ Initiative procedures (OJ L 183, 1.1.2014, pp. 1-2)

Pesticides — plant protection products (Plant Protection Regulation)
Plants Protection Regulation — Plant Protection Regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 — EU statistics on the placing on the market and use of pesticides which are plant protection product
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the systematic production of Community statistics on placing on market and the use of certain pesticides.
It aims to reduce the risks associated with pesticide use.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
plant protection products;

plant growth regulators.
EU countries must report to the European Union (EU) Statistical Office (Eurostat) on:
the number of pesticides on sale and on the EU market;

pesticides used in the EU.
Reporting measures

a reporting measure in kilograms of substances.
The reporting measure must be expressed in kilograms.
Annex III of the regulation defines the area to be treated and the number of substances to be covered.
Each EU country must submit a quality report to Eurostat within 15 months of the end of the reference year.
A quality report must be sent to the Commission every 5 years on the quality of the data collected.

This report must include:
an estimate of the quantities of substances used;
a description of the methods used to collect the data;

Summary 89:
EU rules on plant protection products and active substances (Plant protection products)
European Union (EU) rules on the use of plants and plant protection substances (Active substances)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the application of Directive 91/414/EEC on the authorisation of active substances, safeners or synergists
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that active substances*, safeners*, synergists* and adjuvants* are authorised to be used to protect plants or plant products against threats to their health and the environment.
It sets out the conditions for their authorisation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
active substances* and synergists;
adjuvants*;
plant protection products;
plants and plant products intended for use on other crops.
Authorisation
Active substances
An active substance, safener or synergist must be approved if it complies with the following criteria:
it meets the essential requirements for the protection of plant health;
it has been approved on the basis of assessment of the scientific literature;
the competent authority of the EU country examining the application has carried out a programme of experiments or tests in advance or requires a permit for each experiment or test.
The European Commission must publish a list of approved active substances and safeners, synergists and adjuants in the Official Journal of the European Union (JEU).
The Commission must keep a register of approved substances.
Adjuvents must be listed in the following categories:
basic substances;
safer agents;
targets;
mixtures.
An adjuvant must not be included in the list.
There is no need to modify the nature or composition of an active substance or to modify its nature or its composition in order to make it more effective.
Approval criteria for basic substances
A basic substance must comply with the basic substances and synergist criteria.
A synergist may only be authorised if it meets the following conditions:
the active substance has been tested in accordance with the principles of good laboratory practice or of good experimental practice;
a synergist has been reviewed and certified as compliant with the rules of the JEU;
there is no reason to believe that it poses a serious danger to plant health or the environment;
an active substance does not pose a risk to the environment or to human health.
In addition, a synergist is only authorised if the competent authority considers that it is not likely to pose a serious risk to human, animal or plant health and that its use on another crop is not necessary to control that serious danger.
Examination for authorisation
An application must be submitted to the European Commission, which assesses the application and, if appropriate, issues a decision on its application. The Commission then forwards the application to the rapporteur, who then forwards it to the relevant EU country. The rapporteur must:
inform the Commission of the results and the costs of tests and studies on animals;
report to the Commission any claims for data protection made by the applicant;
draw up a list and exchange of information on applications for authorisations;
publish the results of the tests, studies and reports on the active substance and the synergist;
make available to the public any information it receives on the safety and effectiveness of the product;
ensure that the product is accompanied by a warning label and a product information sheet.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Plant Protection Products and Active Substances (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Active substance: a substance that is used to prevent, control or eradicate plant diseases or pests.
* Safener: a natural substance that can be used for the purpose of preventing, controlling or eradicating plant diseases, pests or diseases in plants.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of 21 December 2008 on the approval of active substance*, safener*, synergist*, adjuant*, plant protection product, plant protection material and active substance information (OJ L 183, 21.12.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom), Euratom have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 90:
Information Management Instrument (IMI)
Information management Instrument (MI)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — establishing Information Management Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the information management instrument (IMEI) and sets out the rules for its use.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the use of information management instruments (IMIs) by the European Union (EU). It covers:
personal data processing in IMI;
information exchange between IMI actors and users;
processing of special categories of data.
It does not apply to the exchange of personal data processed by IMI with its counterparts in a non-EU country.
Access rights of IMI users and users
IMI users have the right to:
use the IMI system on a need-to-know basis;
have access to their personal data;
ensure that personal data are processed in a way that does not compromise the confidentiality of their data; and
have the right of access, correction and deletion.
Right of access to personal data
Users are allowed to access their data processed in MI only on a ‘need to know’ basis and within the internal market area or areas for which they were granted access rights in accordance with Article 3 of Regulation (EC) No 45/2001 on mutual assistance in internal market areas (i.e. MI, unless a Union act provides for a role for the European Commission in such cooperation).
Right to correction or deletion
The rights of users are set out in Annex II of the regulation. These rights include:
the right to correct and delete personal data that has been wrongly processed;
right to access the information that has not been properly processed and to have it deleted;
access to the information processed by MI.
The right of users to correct, delete and delete the personal data they have processed.
Information exchange between actors
The European Commission is an IMI actor and is responsible for:
exchanging information with the other EU countries;
providing information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions;
developing and implementing IMI’s policies and procedures;
monitoring the use and functioning of the system.
Implementation
The Commission must:
implement IMI by means of a pilot project;
coordinate with the EU countries on the implementation of the rules and procedures for IMI.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that IMI is used only for the purpose of:
improving the efficiency and effectiveness of the EU's internal market;
raising initiatives, and gathering information on administrative cooperation or mutual assistance;
promoting information exchange between EU countries and the Commission;
supporting the development of information-sharing mechanisms between the Commission and EU countries in order to improve the efficiency of the information exchange system;
advising the Commission on the application of the relevant IMI rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
 
Information Management Instruments (IMi) is a tool that helps the EU to improve its internal market by:
collecting, analysing, processing and disseminating information on the EU economy;
improved the security of information;
facilitating the exchange and exchange of information between EU citizens;
enhancing the security and confidentiality of information.
For more information, see:
‘Information management instruments’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                  
Commission Implementing Regulation of 21 May 2011 establishing the Information Management Institute (Implementation Regulation) (OJ L 183, 28.5.2011, pp. 1-8)

Summary 91:
Fishing in the Mediterranean and Black Sea: GFCM Agreement
Fishing on the Mediterranean, Black Sea and the Black Sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the implementation of the General Fisheries Conservation Mechanism Agreement (GFCM)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fishing fleet registers and the EU fishing fleet register are kept up-to-date on the sustainable use of living marine resources.
It also aims to protect the marine environment and the marine biodiversity of the Mediterranean Sea and Black Seas.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to vessels of more than 15 metres in overall length flying an EU flag and registered in its territory that it authorises to fish in the Great Commission Mediterranean Maritime Maritime Maritime Management Area. It does not apply to:
vessels flying the flag of an EU Member State;
ships registered in an EU country other than that of the EU;
seagoing vessels;
fishing gear.
The agreement applies to the following areas, species and fishing gear:
the Mediterranean Sea;
the Black Sea.
Authorisations
Each Member State must:
ensure that authorisations to fish or transport fish and fishery products are compatible with the information obtained under point 1 and the duration of the authorisations and their application to areas;
verify that the official documentation on board is valid, if necessary, through appropriate contacts with the flag State or international records of vessels.
provide the Commission with a list of authorised vessels and the number and type of fishing gear;
check that the authorisation is valid and that the fishing gear is in line with the requirements laid down in the agreement;
provides the Commission a report on the carrying over of days lost in the previous year as referred to in paragraph 2.
Register of vessels
Each year, each Member State sends to the Commission, through the Commission's data-processing support, an updated list of the vessels of 15 metres or more flying its flag, registered in the country of registration and flying its territory and flying the Community fishing fleet number. The Commission then forwards this list to the European Commission, which then forwards it to the EU countries.
Port inspections
Port inspectors must verify that:
authorisations issued by the Member State to fish, transport or engage in fishing activities are valid;
where authorised vessels are present in an area, they must check that the information on board the vessel is compatible with points 1 and 2 of the regulation;
if authorised vessels fail to comply with the regulation, the Commission may issue an authorisation to withdraw it.
Fishery gear
The Commission must ensure that fishing gear inspections are carried out in accordance with the agreement. It must also send a report every 2 years on the results of inspections to the Member States and the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the General Fishery Conservation Management Agreement.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing’ on the EU Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fishing gear: any fishing gear used to catch fish and/or other species of fish.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Еォォォ
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the enforcement of the general fisheries conservation management agreement (GFMC) (OJ L 347, 21.12.2013, pp. 1-3)

Summary 92:
European patent with unitary effect
European patent on the market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2012 on the granting of European patents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Patent Office (EPO) as a European patent organisation.
It sets out the rules for granting European patents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
patents that are granted by the EPO on the basis of a single application, i.e. a patent that has the same effect in all EU countries, irrespective of the country in which the patent was granted;
pending patents that have the effect of a ‘unitary effect’.
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that patents granted by EU countries have the same legal effect throughout the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to patents which have been granted by a single EU country.
EU countries may grant a patent to a patent proprietor if they consider that they have the right to prevent any third party from committing acts against the patent.
Licensing of patents
A patent may be licensed in respect of the whole or part of the territories of the participating EU countries. It may also be licensed to a non-EU country if it is granted in the territory of the EU country where the patent is licensed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Patent Organisation
The EPO was established by the Convention on the Grant of European Patents of 5 October 1973, as revised on 17 December 1991 and on 29 November 2000 (hereinafter ‘EPC’).
The EPC was established in order to ensure the proper functioning of the European patent, consistency of case-law and hence legal certainty, and cost-effectiveness for patent proprietors.
For more information, see:
European Patent Organisation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent which is granted by an EU country to a single person or company.
* Unitary patent: the patent granted to a person or a company which has the exclusive right to patent a product or service in all the EU countries in which it has been granted.

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

The recommendation concerns the application for a Unified Product Patent. The recommendation is based on the experience gained in the case of a patent conferred on an object of property (Object of Property) which is a product which is not a product. The aim of the decision is to improve the legal certainty of the patent and to ensure its proper functioning.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

It is composed of:
representatives from the public administrations and bodies of the Member States dealing with intellectual property;
representative from the private sectors;
participation of consumers and of small and medium-sized enterprises;
members of the internet intermediaries.
The European Commission:
draws up the work programme and the management report;
publishes the notices that are necessary for the fulfilment of all the tasks entrusted to the Office by this regulation;
cooperates with the Office on its activities;
is assisted by the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).

Summary 94:
International trade agreements — rules for exercising the EU’s rights under international trade agreements (trade in services, intellectual property rights and intellectual property protection)
International trade deals – rules for the exercise of the EU's rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2014 — rules on exercising the Union's rights under International trade agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on how the European Union (EU) can exercise its rights to trade in goods and services with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
international trade agreements between the EU and any country which is a party to an international trade agreement;
trade agreements between EU countries which are not parties to an EU trade agreement.
It does not apply to agreements concluded under the World Trade Organization (WTO) or the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade in Services (GATT).
EU countries must ensure that:
the measures adopted under the regulation do not infringe on the rights of the exporting country;
they do not affect the rights or obligations of the importing country.
The measures may include:
a safeguard measure or an extension of a safeguard measure;
the imposition of a mandatory price penalty on terms established in Article XXVIII of the GATT;
a level of nullification or impairment of the benefits accruing to the EU under the agreement, i.e.
the suspension, modification or repeal of measures adopted by the EU.
Implementing measures
The Commission must adopt implementing acts suspending, amending or repealing measures suspending, modifying or repealing implementing acts adopted under this regulation within 3 years of the adoption of an implementing act. The Commission must report its findings to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of the implementing acts.
In the case of measures in the field of public procurement, the Commission must consult the public authorities in the EU countries before adopting implementing acts, in particular the European Commission.
Suspension, modification and repeal
An implementing act may be suspended, modified or repealed by the Commission if it:
does not:
violates the rights conferred on the EU by the agreement; or
is contrary to the interests of the contracting parties;
is not compatible with the principles of the WTO;
may infringe the rights and obligations of a contracting party.
An EU country may suspend, modify or repeal the implementing act if it considers that the measures are contrary to its economic interests or that they are not consistent with the objectives of the agreement. It must inform the Commission and the other contracting parties of the reasons for its decision.
A Commission committee must adopt immediately applicable implementing acts:
in the case where the contracting party is a natural or legal person, the origin of that legal person is deemed to be the origin or legal entity which own or control it. This origin of the legal person must be determined in accordance with the rules laid down in the annex to the regulation;
in cases where it is a legal person who is not engaged in substantive business operations such that it has a direct and effective link with the economy of the Member State in which it is established;
where it is not a natural person, it is considered to be an origin of a legal entity that own or controls it.
Information
Information received pursuant to this regulation must be used only for the purpose for which it was requested. It may not be used for any other purpose other than the purposes for which the information is requested.
Disputes between contracting parties must be settled by means of a negotiated settlement.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on 18 July 2014. It repeals Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/2013.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
International Trade Agreements (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC, EC) No 550/2014 of 8 May 2014 on the exercise by the Union of its rights under the International Trade agreements (Trade in Services, Intellectual Property Rights and Intellectual Property Protection) (OJ L 127, 18.5.2014, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, EC), Euratom have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 95:
Safeguard measures for the import of bananas from Central America
Safefarmers’ measures for bananas from the Central American countries
The European Union (EU) has adopted a series of measures designed to protect the EU’s banana sector from the risks posed by the illegal trade in bananas originating in Central America.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2019 of 31 December 2019 establishing a system of safeguard measures for imports of bananas originating from the countries of the Central America region.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a system for implementing safeguard measures to protect EU countries’ banana sector against the risk posed by illegal imports originating in the region of Central America (known as the ‘Banana Triangle’).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The measures apply to:
bananas originating in Costa Rica, El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, Nicaragua, Panama, Nicaragua and Venezuela, as well as to products originating in those countries;
grains originating in Colombia, Ecuador, Peru, Venezuela and Nicaragua;
seeds originating in Ecuador, Paraguay, Peru and Venezuela.
The regulation applies to all imports originating from these countries, except those originating in Panama.
It does not apply to the following countries:
the United States of America;
the European Economic Area (EEA);
the Republic of Colombia;
Yucatán;
Venezuela;
Guatemala;
Zambia;
Somalia.
Import statistics
The Commission must monitor the evolution of import statistics of bananas imported from the regions of the Caribbean and the Pacific Ocean.
If the import statistics show that there has been a surge in imports, the Commission may adopt a safeguard measure. The measures may be temporary or permanent, depending on the volume of imports.
A safeguard measure may not be applied beyond the expiry of the transitional period, except with the consent of the country concerned.
In the event that there is a surge of imports concentrated in one or several Member States, or outermost regions, or if there is sufficient prima facie evidence that the conditions for initiation are met, it may be initiated in the event of a surge. The Commission may also initiate a proceeding to suspend the preferential customs duty applied to products of the same origin during that same year for a period of time not exceeding 3 months, and not going beyond the end of the calendar year or determine that such suspension is not appropriate.
Exemptions from safeguard measures
No safeguard measure shall be applied to imports of a product originating in a country that is not a party to the Agreement on the Free movement of Goods between the EU and its Member States (see summary).
In addition, no safeguard measure will apply to imports originating, exported or intended to be exported from a country other than the one covered by the agreement.
Importers must inform the Commission of the volume and origin of the products they intend to import from the affected country.
EU countries must notify the Commission if they consider that the volume or origin of a particular product is likely to exceed the threshold set by the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It applies from 31 December 2020.
BACKGROUND
  The EU has adopted measures to safeguard the banana sector in the context of the agreement on the free movement of goods between the European Union and the countries in the Caribbean.
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).

Summary 96:
Preservation of bank accounts in cross-border civil matters (Regulation (EC) No 596/2013)
Preservation in cross -border civil matter (Regulating (EC)) No 597/2013
The European Union (EU) has adopted a regulation to ensure that bank accounts held by or with central banks are preserved.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a procedure for a creditor to request that a bank account or accounts be preserved in order to recover debts from a debtor.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
bank accounts held in an EU country other than:
the EU country of the court seised of the application for the preservation order pursuant to Article 6;
the country in which the creditor is domiciled.
It does not apply to bank accounts that are held by the European Central Bank (ECB) when acting in their capacity as monetary authorities.
Creditors may request that the bank account be preserved if they consider that there is an urgent need for such account information because there is a risk that, without it, the subsequent enforcement of the creditor’s claim against the debtor is likely to be jeopardised and that this could consequently lead to a substantial deterioration of the debtor's financial situation.
The creditor must provide the creditor with all the relevant information available to him about the debtor and the bank or accounts to be preserved. The creditor may also provide security in lieu of preservation.
In addition, the creditor must inform the debtor of the existence of a need for the order. The debtor must be informed about the creditor's application and be heard prior to the issue of the order or notified of the Order prior to its implementation.
A creditor may refuse to issue the order if it considers that the creditor has not presented sufficient evidence to support its claim.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    ‘Preservation Order’ has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  “Cross-border Civil Matters” is part of a series of measures designed to improve judicial cooperation in civil matters.
For more information, see:
Cross-Border Civil Matters (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bank account: any account or account that is held by a central bank.
* Creditor: any person who is a creditor of a debtor and who has a claim against that debtor for the same amount of money.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Directive 2013/17/EU
1.1.2013
-
OJ L 127 of 1.1., 2013, pp. 1-3
RELATED ACTS
Regulation 2013/18/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2013 on the protection of personal data held by central banks and amending Council Regulation (Euratom) No 498/2013 on the preservation of accounts in the event of a change in the monetary policy of the Member State concerned (Official Journal L 96 of 31.12.2013, p. 1)

Summary 97:
EU border surveillance: operational rules for border surveillance and search and rescue operations
European border surveillance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 656/2014 on the operational rules of border surveillance for the implementation, application and development of the Schengen acquis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules on border surveillance to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
border surveillance in the territory of the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to border surveillance in non-EU countries.
The purpose of the regulation is to prevent illegal border crossings.
It applies to the following areas:
crossing the external borders of the EU;
the transit of persons, goods and services between EU countries;
cross-border smuggling;
trafficking in human beings;
transport of goods, services and persons.
Operational rules
EU countries must:
ensure that border surveillance is proportionate to the objectives pursued, non-discriminatory and should fully respect human dignity, fundamental rights and the rights of refugees and asylum seekers, including the principle of non-refoulement;
not discriminate between national authorities;
cooperate with each other in order to prevent, detect and respond to illegal border crossing;
take appropriate measures to prevent or respond to cross-crossing incidents;
use the information provided by the participating country’s national border surveillance units;
assist in the prevention, detection and response units.
Coordination and coordination of surveillance operations
Each EU country must establish a border surveillance unit. This unit is responsible for:
detecting and responding to illegal crossings;
carrying out searches and rescues;
operating in the event of interception on the high seas;
ensuring the safe return of persons and goods to their country of origin;
coordinating and coordinating searches and rescue situations.
In addition, the EU country in which the surveillance operation is taking place must establish an operational plan for the operation. The plan must contain details of the operation, including:
the number of personnel involved;
its duration and duration;
a description of the objectives and objectives of the surveillance operations;
details of the intended actions or measures authorised by the flag country;
measures taken in the course of a surveillance operation that are proportionate and proportionate.
National coordination centres
The national coordination centres of the participating EU countries must coordinate with the European Asylum Support Office (EASO) and the International Coordination Centre (ICC) of the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Border surveillance (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2014/656/EU of 28 June 2014 on the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 on the establishment of a European Union border surveillance system (OJ L 132, 29.5.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 608/2014 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 98:
EU migration and asylum programmes: financial rules
EU migration, asylum and protection programmes: Financial rules
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012 — financial rules for the implementation of national programmes
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ national programmes are financially sound.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the financing of these programmes.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
national programmes for the management of the external borders of the EU;
EU actions, emergency assistance and technical assistance for migrants and asylum seekers;
the protection of EU citizens’ rights and the protection of the environment.
EU countries are responsible for:
implementing national programmes;
monitoring and evaluating them;
ensuring that they are in line with the EU’s general principles of subsidiarity;
providing information to potential beneficiaries about funding opportunities;
publicising to EU citizens the role and achievements of the specific regulations.
Evaluation
The European Commission is responsible for the monitoring and evaluation of the national programmes. It must:
monitor and evaluate the programmes’ effectiveness;
identify and correct any irregularities;
report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the European Commission on the results and impact of the programmes; and
report annually to the Commission on its implementation.
Responsibilities of EU countries
Each EU country must ensure that beneficiaries receive the full amount due promptly. They must also:
ensure that beneficiaries do not have to pay any late payments;
make sure that beneficiaries are not overbilled;
take the necessary measures to prevent, detect and correct irregularities and recover amounts unduly paid together with any interest on late payments.
In addition, EU countries should ensure that:
all beneficiaries are provided with information on and access to the programmes in their country;
all payments are made in the euro.
The Commission may:
make financial corrections if it considers that a national programme has breached EU or national law;
notify the responsible authorities of any breaches;
use the financial correction mechanism;
inform the Commission of the nature and gravity of the breach and the amount of financial correction.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 June 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is one of the most dynamic economies in the world. It has the largest external borders in Europe and faces a high level of migration and protection needs. It is therefore essential to have a coherent approach to tackling these challenges.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Migration and asylum: the EU's external borders policy. It aims to protect EU citizens' rights and respect for international and human rights law, including as regards actions implemented in third countries, showing solidarity amongst all EU countries and demonstrating the need to respect national responsibilities in the process of ensuring a clear definition of tasks.

Summary 99:
European Asylum and Migration Fund
European asylum and migration Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 establishing the European Asylum and Migratory Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up the EU’s first permanent migration and asylum fund.
It aims to provide financial support to EU countries in order to strengthen and develop the common European asylum system, including its external dimension, and to support legal migration to the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The fund covers the period 2008-2013. It is funded by the general budget of the European Union (EU).
The EU countries are responsible for:
allocating the funds;
providing specific assistance to the European Migration and Integration Office (EMI) and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (BERCAA);
supporting the implementation of EU readmission agreements;
organising joint return operations, including joint actions with non-EU countries.
Specific actions
EU countries must ensure that:
the EMI and the BACAA work together to:
ensure that EU countries respect the principle of non-refoulement;
promote the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
facilitate the exchange of information and experience between EU countries on migration and border management;
encourage EU countries to cooperate in the field of migration;
cooperate in the fight against trafficking in human beings.
The EMIs and BACAsylum-seekers are eligible for financial support under the EU countries’ national asylum systems.
National contact points
The European Union countries must establish national contact points (NCPs) to provide information and assistance on migration issues. The NCPs must:
coordinate with each other and with the relevant international organisations;
assist in the coordination of national migration policies and programmes;
support the development and implementation of the common EU asylum system;
develop and strengthen all aspects of the Common European Asylum System, including the external dimension;
contribute to the development of the internal dimension of the EU migration system.
Efficiency of measures and quality of spending
The objectives of the fund are evaluated using common indicators as set out in Annex IV to the regulation and programme-specific indicators included in national programmes.
Financing
The Fund is financed by the European Commission and the Member States. It may be used for the following specific actions:
reintegration operations;
joint return operations; and
legal migration.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Asylum Fund was established in 2008 as part of the General Programme ‘Solidarity and Management of Migration Flows’ (OJ L 144, 6.6.2007, pp. 45-48).
For more information, see:
European Asylum Fund (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Migrant: a term used in the context of the general programme ‘solidarity and management of migration flows’.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member State
Official Journal
Council Decision No 575/2007/EC
1.1.2015
-
OJ C 202, 1.1 of 1.2.2015.
RELATED ACTS
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 496/2014 and (EU), Euratom No 513/2014 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Union on the establishment of a European Migration Fund and on the European External Action Service and on cooperation between the European Agency for Fundamental Rights and the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
Council Regulation (EC) No 652/2013 of 27 June 2013 on the creation of a new European Migration Facility and on coordination between the EU Member States’ external action centres and on a European Asylum Facility for Migration and the Union External Action Services.

Summary 100:
European Union Fund for the fight against transnational crime
European Union fund for the combatting crime
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 establishing a European Union Fund to combat crime
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a multiannual financial instrument to support EU countries’ efforts to combat transnational organised crime.
It aims to provide financial assistance to EU countries in order to protect people and goods from increasingly transnational threats and to support the work carried out by Member States’ competent authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to matters that are covered by the EU’s action in the field of freedom, security and justice (2014-2020) and to the European Union (EU), the European Economic Area (EEA) and the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).
It does not apply to matters related to the EU budget.
EU countries are encouraged to use a part of the global resources available for the national programmes to address the strategic EU priorities set out in Annex I to this regulation. These are:
the fight against organised crime and transnational terrorism;
the prevention and control of organised crime;
countering the financing of illicit drugs and their transport;
protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the instrument. It is assisted by the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
Funding
The instrument is funded by EU countries and the European Commission. It has a budget of €2.5 billion.
Funds are allocated to:
€1 billion from the general budget;
€500 million from the European Investment Bank for Europe (EIB);
€250 million from Horizon 2020.
In addition, the EU countries may use a portion of the resources available from the EIB for:
emergency assistance;
research and development;
cooperation;
training;
exchange of information and best practices;
technical assistance.
Programmes
The EU countries must, within the objectives referred to in Article 3 of this regulation, pursue in particular the strategic Union priorities listed in the annex to the regulation, taking account of the outcome of the policy dialogue and the results of the European Council on the EU's foreign and security policy, as well as of the Council of Europe's annual policy dialogue on security and security.
National programmes
The national programmes must:
contribute to the prevention, control and enforcement of transnational crimes;
promote cooperation, coordination, contingency planning and the exchange and dissemination of best practices among public and private actors;
support the work of the competent authorities of EU countries;
provide technical assistance to Member States and the Commission;
ensure that Member States implement their national programmes in line with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Union is a member of the Schengen area.
For more information, see:
‘The fight against crime’ on the European External Action Service's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transnational crime: the illegal trade in goods, services, capital, capital movements and capital movements of persons, goods and capital.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 496/2014 of 21 December 2014 establishing a Fund for combating crime (OJ L 31, 1.1.2015, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments and corrections to Regulation (EJ L31, 1) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing: EU rules on non-EU countries’ access to fishing stocks
Non-EU rules on Non-EU fishing: European Union policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the EU’s implementation of measures to combat non-Sustainable fishing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules on the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the Member States’ implementation of the EU fisheries policy.
It aims to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a framework for cooperation between the EU countries to prevent and deter non-sustainably fishing*.
It does not apply to:
fishing in the Baltic Sea;
the Baltic Sea and the Black Sea; or
the Black Sea.
The European Commission has the power to identify non-compliance with the rules of the regulation and to adopt measures in respect of such countries. These measures must be effective and proportionate, and must be enforced by competent authorities.
In addition, the Commission must:
identify a country that is allowing or is likely to allow non sustainable fishing;
take measures to prohibit, restrict, restrict or prohibit fishing in the area in question;
ensure that the measures are enforced by the competent authorities of the country concerned;
establish an appropriate system for their enforcement.
Cooperation may be established in the framework of regional fisheries management organisations (RFMOIs) or, where RFMOs have no competence for the stock in question, by means of ad hoc arrangements among the countries having an interest in the fishery.
This cooperation may take place in the context of an RFMO or, for example, through the creation of an ad hoc arrangement.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules on fishing, including:
the EU fisheries strategy (see summary);
the rules on sustainable fishing (see overview);
rules on the management of stocks of common interest.
For more information, see:
EU fisheries policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-Union fishing: fishing that is not sustainable.
* Sustainable fishing: non-fishing that takes place in areas where there is a high risk of overfishing.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the exercise of implementing powers by the Commission pursuant to Article 4 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) (OJ L 347, 28.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Council Regulation (Euratom) no 550/13 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between the EU and non-EU countries (until 9 January 2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the signing and conclusion of bilateral investment agreements with third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral investment* agreements with countries outside the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to agreements concluded between an EU country and a third country. It does not apply to bilateral agreements concluded by EU countries with the United Kingdom (1) or the United States of America (2).
EU countries must notify the European Commission of their intention to sign or conclude a bilateral investment agreement with a non EU country. The notification must include:
a copy of the agreement;
a description of the reasons for the decision;
the terms and conditions for concluding the agreement.
The Commission may request further information from the EU country concerned.
If the Commission finds that the agreement satisfies the requirements of Article 9(1) and (2) of the TFEU, it authorises the maintenance or entry into force of such an agreement under EU law. The agreement may be maintained in force, or enter into force, until a bilateral agreement between the Union and the same third country enters into force.
In the case of a dispute, the Commission must:
seek the agreement of the country concerned before activating any relevant mechanisms for dispute settlement against a country included in the agreement and must, where requested by the Commission, activate such mechanisms.
Authorisation to sign and conclude an investment agreement
An EU country must request the Commission’s authorisation to open formal negotiations. This authorisation must be granted in accordance with the advisory procedure referred to in Article 16(2). Where additional information is needed to take a decision, the 90-day period must run from the date of receipt of the additional information. The Commission may, where appropriate, consult the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and the Committee for Investment Agreements.
Where the Commission considers that a bilateral Investment agreement notified pursuant to paragraph 1 of this regulation fulfils its requirements, it may authorise the maintenance, entry or renewal of such agreement under Union law.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 9 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy. This means that it has the power to adopt and implement bilateral agreements.
For more information, see:
Investment agreements (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: an international agreement between a country and another EU country on the subject of investment.
* Non-EU country: a country outside the EU with which the EU has an economic or trade relationship.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  � 
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of 9 December 2011 establishing the conditions for the signing, on behalf of the Union, and conclusion, of bilateral investments agreements between Member States and third countries (OJ L 127, 9.1.2011, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 181/2010 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 103:
Guaranteed market access for the least-developed countries
Guarantee market access to the EU’s common commercial policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/93 — GUaranteed preferential market access (GSP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a general framework for the European Union (EU) to apply the Generalised system of preferential trade preferences for the countries of the least developed world.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a list of eligible countries for the GSP.
The list includes countries for which preferences may have been temporarily withdrawn or suspended.
Beneficiary countries
The GSP applies to:
non-EU countries that have not been identified by the United Nations (UN) as a least developed country during the first available review of the category of least-develop countries;
the EU countries that are not identified as least developed countries by the UN.
It does not apply to countries which are not members of the World Trade Organization (WTO) or the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD).
Beneficiaries
EU countries are eligible for GSP preferences on the basis of the following criteria:
their gross domestic product (GDP) per capita;
gross domestic product per capita.
GDP per capita per inhabitant;
their GDP per capita as a percentage of the population.
A country is eligible if it meets the criteria set out in the annex to the regulation.
its GDP per head of population
its population is at least developed (i.e.
Its GDP per inhabit less than 1.5 % of the EU.

Bulgaria
Seychelles

The SAFTA is an area of cooperation between the EU and the EU countries which have ratified the following international agreements:

FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing act has applied since 1 January 1994.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to establish the general framework of the Generalized System of Preferential Trade Arrangements.
For more information, see:
General framework of trade preferences (European Commission).
KEY TERMS

* Common customs tariff specific duties: a tariff which is reduced by 30 % on products listed in Annex V as sensitive products.

Summary 104:
Exporting firearms: UN Firearms Protocol
Export firearms
SUMMARY OF:
United Nations Firearms Protocol (UN Firearms Protocol)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE PROTOCOL AND OF THE REGULATION?
The United Nations (UN) Firearms Protocol aims to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms, their parts and components and ammunition.
It aims to:
promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among the signatory countries in order to prevent and combat the illicit manufacture and trafficking of firearms;
combat the illicit trade in firearms and their components;
contribute to the prevention, control and eradication of the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The protocol applies to the import and export of:
firearms;
parts and components* and ammunition* intended for use in a firearm;
firearm accessories;
machineguns;
ammunition.
Exports
An export authorisation must be granted by the competent authorities of an EU country to a non-EU country. The authorisation may be revoked if there are grounds for suspicion that the reasons substantiated by hunters or sport shooters are not in conformity with the relevant considerations and the obligations laid down in the protocol.
The authorisation is valid for a period not exceeding 10 days. It may be renewed at any time by the customs authorities of the EU country.
An exporter must:
ensure that the goods are accompanied by an export contract has been concluded or that the exporter has the power to determine the sending of the items out of the item;
provide proof of the goods;
the exporter is responsible for ensuring that the items comply with the requirements of the export contract;
notify the customs authority of the importing country of the reason for the suspension or revocation of the authorisation.
If an exporter fails to provide proof of receipt of the import authorisation, it must inform the competent authority of that country of its decision to suspend the export or, if necessary, otherwise prevent the export.
EU countries must ensure that:
the consignments of firearms and ammunition are accompanied and accompanied by a certificate of authenticity;
there is no risk of counterfeiting or falsification of the certificates of authenticity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE PROTOPOL APPLY?
It has applied since 3 July 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
The UN Firearms protocol, which is part of the United Nations Convention on the Suppliers’ Rights of Man and of Animals, entered into force on 3 July 2004.
For more information, see:
‘Unlawful trade in arms’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 929/2005 of 3 July2005 on the conclusion, on behalf of the European Union, and provisional application of the Protocol on the International Trade in Arms and Ammunition (OJ L 127, 3.7.2005, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 929 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

See consolidated version.

Summary 105:
EUROSUR: situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the EU countries
EurosURASUR
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 establishing the framework for cooperation in the field of border surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EUROSUR framework, which aims to improve the situational awareness, reaction and intelligence capabilities of national authorities and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurasur) at the EU’s external borders for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
national coordination centres;
national situational pictures;
communications network;
a European situational picture;
common pre-frontier intelligence picture.
It does not apply to any legal or administrative measure in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the Agency.
National coordination centres
The national coordination centres of neighbouring EU countries must share with each other, directly and in near-real-time, the situational picture of neighbouring external border sections relating to the following:
incidents and other significant events;
tactical risk analysis reports as contained in the analysis layer.
The communication network contains information in the form of a communication network in accordance with Article 11d of Regulation (EC) No 2007/2004.
EU countries must ensure that the communication network is interoperable and can be used for the exchange of information.
EURASA
The Agency may refuse a request for a request from a national coordination centre for technical, financial or operational reasons.
Agency
The agency may use on its own initiative the surveillance tools referred to in paragraph 2 for collecting information which is relevant for the common pre-border intelligence picture and the common surveillance tools.
In addition, the Agency may use surveillance tools for the following purposes:
collecting, analysing and sharing information;
monitoring and analysing movements of migrants;
reacting to impact levels;
providing information to the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and other relevant bodies.
Cooperation with neighbouring countries
The EU countries may cooperate with the Agency in the following areas:
cooperation with non-EU countries;
cooperating with the United Kingdom, Ireland and the United States of America;
coordination with the European Union Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (OLAF).
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
Eursur is part of the ‘Schengen acquis’, the common legal instrument for the prevention, detection and control of illegal immigration.
For more information, see:
‘The EU's external borders’ on the European External Borders Agency's website.

Summary 106:
Eurodac: access to Eurodac for the prevention, detection and investigation of terrorist offences and serious criminal offences (Recast Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
EurodAC: access and exchange of data for the purpose of preventing, detecting and investigating terrorist offences
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2014 on access and sharing of data between national authorities of the Member States of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions and procedures for access by national authorities to Eurodatacontrol (Eurodatac) and Europol’s (Europol) automated fingerprinting identification systems.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to national authorities in the EU that are designated by the European Commission to:
request EurodatAControl data;
compare Eurodatatic data with other national fingerprinting systems;
check that Eurodatasheets are interoperable;
assist in the exchange of information between national security and law enforcement authorities.
The competent national authority in each Member State must designate a national authority that is authorised to request Eurodatatics data. This authority is responsible for:
collecting Eurodatadata;
checking the data; and
checking that the data are compatible with the national systems of other Member States.
National authorities must keep a list of the designated authorities and the national access points that they designate.
Eurodatabases must be secure and take place electronically.
Each Member State has to set up and maintain a central system to store and exchange data.
identify the data it receives and transmit it to the national authority of the country of origin.
When a Member State receives a request from another Member State, it must:
check the data against the national database;
ensure that the requested data is compatible with national databases in the Member State of origin;
make a copy of the data and send it to that country;
use the data to establish the identity of the subject of the request.
In addition, the national authorities must ensure that the information they receive is:
secure;
validated;
authentic;
transmitted in a secure format;
inform the Central System as soon as possible and no later than after 72 hours of the erasure of data, for the reason specified in Article 2(c) of this regulation;
notify all Member States in the event of a hit with data which they transmitted relating to data relating to the data referred to in Article 9(1) or 14(1).
The Central System must automatically transmit the hit or the negative result of the comparison to the Member state of origin, which must inform the country in which the data came from.
Member States must also ensure that:
the national authority which received the request for Eurodatascrypt data has the necessary technical infrastructure to process the request;
the requested data are stored and transmitted securely;
all communication between the designated authority, the verifying authorities, the National Access Points and the central system is secure and takes place electronically;
information exchanged between the competent national authorities and national security authorities is encrypted;
national security authorities must cooperate to this end;
National supervisory authorities must: ensure that national data exchange systems are secure and that national security information systems are secured;monitor and evaluate the application of the regulation;ensure the security of Eurodatastore data;monitor, evaluate and monitor the security and integrity of national data systems;monitor the implementation of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
Eurodata was established under Decision 2008/615/JHA (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘Eurodatatics’ on Europol's website.

Summary 107:
Protected designations of origin and protected geographical indications and traditional specialities
Protected names of origin* and protected names of geographical indications*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 — protected names and protected designations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that protected names* and geographical indications (designations* of origin) are used fairly and in order to prevent practices liable to mislead consumers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to products originating in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to:
products originating in non-EU countries;
traditional specialities*;
products produced in mountain areas.
It applies to all products originating from the territory of the EU.
Protection of origin
The protected name and geographical indication must be registered in the EU country of origin. It must be accompanied by:
the name and address of the applicant group and of the authorities or, if available, the place of production;
proof that the product is protected in that country;
a description of the characteristics of the product;
evidence of the production methods used;
the product’s provenance;
information on the quality of the products.
The European Commission may adopt implementing acts laying down rules on the use of the protected name or geographical indication. These must be adopted by the Commission in accordance with the rules laid down in the annex to the regulation. The Commission may also adopt delegated acts concerning the establishment of the methods of production and other criteria relevant for the application of the protection of the name or the protected geographical indication or the traditional speciality.
Exemptions
The Commission may exclude certain products from the scope of the regulation if they do not meet the conditions laid down by this regulation. For example, the Commission may decide to exclude products originating outside the EU from its scope.
In addition, the regulation does not cover certain categories of products, such as:
food and feed;
medicinal products;
animal feed and feed additives;
live animals;
seeds and fodder;
means of transport;
surgical products; and
products intended for human consumption.
EU countries must ensure that the protected names or geographical indications do not become generic.
Cancellation of protected names
An application for the cancellation of a protected name of origin or protected designation must be submitted to the national authorities of the country concerned, in compliance with minimum common provisions, including a national opposition procedure. The application must include at least:
a copy of the application and the name of the person or group responsible for its protection;
an explanation of the reasons for the request for cancellation;
procedures for the withdrawal of the designation or designation;
documents relating to the application for cancellation.
If an application is rejected, the applicant must provide proof that the protection is not justified by the following reasons:
it does not comply with the requirements laid down under the regulation;
it is contrary to public policy;
there is no reason to believe that it is protected by the protected designation or designation.
An EU country may withdraw a protected designation or designation if it considers that it does not correspond to the public policy objectives of the European Commission or the EU as a whole.
National authorities may refuse to accept an application if they consider that it:
does not conform with the general principles of EU law;
is contrary to the principles of public policy or to the general interests of the Union; or
is likely to mislead the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?

It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the quality and traceability of food and feed.
For more information, see:
European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
KEY TERMS
* Protected name: a registered name or designation of origin, a geographical indication, a traditional name or a traditional designation, or a speciality, which is used to describe a product originating in an EU country.
* Traditionally speciality: a product which is known as a product of a particular geographical origin or a specific geographical area.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU) No 550/2006 of 21 December 2006 on protected names (OJ L 131, 24.12.2006, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, No 550) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 108:
EU rules on oil tanker age and design requirements (MARPOL 73/78)
EU rules in respect of oil tankage
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 — rules on the age and type of oil tanker
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that oil tankers entering into ports and offshore terminals or anchoring in an area under the jurisdiction of EU countries and oil tanker flying the flag of EU States comply with Regulation 20 of Annex I to MARPOL 73/1978 as revised in 2004 by Resolution MEPC 117(52) adopted by the IMO’s Marine Environment Protection Committee (MEPC) in order to reduce the risk of accidental oil pollution in European waters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
double-hull or equivalent design requirements by single-Hull oil tank ships, irrespective of their flag, which enter or leave a port or offshore terminal or anchor in areas under EU countries’ jurisdiction;
any oil tanker above 15 years of age, regardless of its flag, that enters or leaves ports or offshore terminals.
It does not apply to oil tanker carrying heavy grade oil, unless such tanker is a double-HULL oil tanker.
Exemptions for Category 2 and Category 3 crude oil tanker:
the regulation allows for an exception to the phasing-out of Category 2* and category 3 oil tanker rules, under certain circumstances, beyond the time limit of their phasing out.
EU countries may decide to allow Category 2 or Category 3 tanker rules to continue to operate for a limited number of years.
The EU countries may also decide to extend the duration of the exemption for Category 1 and Category 2 crude oil ships.
National authorities must:
ensure that:
all oil tanker vessels flying their flag are registered in the EU country in which they are registered;
have a certificate of registration issued by the International Maritime Organization (IMO);
operate under the authority of an EU country;
apply the conditions laid down in Annex II of the regulation;
carry heavy oil;
notify the European Commission of any changes to the conditions of operation of a tanker;
check that a tanker complies with the condition assessment scheme referred to in Annex III.
Compliance with the requirements
A single- or double- hull oil tanker of less than 15 years’ age must comply with the following requirements:
it must be designed and built in accordance with the MARPOL requirements;
it is not allowed to enter, leave, anchor or tranship oil terminals under its jurisdiction, on the basis of Regulation 20.5 of Annex II;
its crew must be at least 18 years old;
the vessel must be equipped with the necessary equipment and equipment.
In addition, it must have the following equipment:
a fuel tank;
a propulsion system;
an emergency escape system; and
a fire extinguisher.
No oil tanker is allowed to carry heavy oil unless it is a heavy-duty oil tanker, unless it meets the requirements of Regulation 19.2 of Annex III of MARPOL.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 June 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) is responsible for the management of the maritime environment and for the implementation of maritime law in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Oil tanker age’ on the EMSA’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 May 2013 on the application of Regulation (EC) No 20/2013 on the date and type-approval of the age of oiltankage requirements (recast) (OJ L 347, 21.5.2013, pp. 1-8)

Financial supervision: European supervisory authority (ESA) (European Banking Authority)
Financial supervision : European banking authority (EBA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 establishing the European Banking Authority
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA), the European Union’s (EU) single supervisory body.
It aims to ensure that the European financial system is fair and balanced.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESCA is responsible for supervising all financial institutions in the EU, including banks, insurance companies and investment funds. It has the following tasks:
supervising the prudential supervision of financial institutions;
providing guidance and recommendations for financial institutions to take account of the systemic risk posed by them;
ensuring that financial institutions comply with EU law;
assisting national supervisory authorities in preparing equivalence decisions relating to supervisory regimes in non-EU countries in accordance with the acts referred to in Article 1(2) of TFEU (see summary).
The ESMA has a Board of Supervisors. It is chaired by a Chairperson appointed by the Commission. The Commission appoints the Chairperson on the basis of merit, skills, knowledge of the financial sector and experience relevant to financial supervision and regulation. The Chairperson is assisted by a Joint Committee on Financial Conglomerates. The Joint Committee may establish further Sub-Committees.
The Commission may decide to admit observers.
