Summary 110:
Equal treatment of EU workers in the territory of another EU country: employment and employment and equal treatment
Equality of treatment of European Union (EU) workers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2011 — equal treatment of workers in EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system of equal treatment for workers from other EU countries who wish to work in the EU.
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2004.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to permanent, seasonal and frontier workers, as well as those who pursue their activities for the purpose of providing services.
Eligibility for employment
Nationals of EU countries have the right to take up available employment in any EU country with the same priority as nationals of that country. This right does not depend on medical, vocational or other criteria which are discriminatory on grounds of nationality by comparison with those applied to nationals of the other EU country who wish the same activity.
The right to employment does not apply to:
nationals of one EU State for a post in another EU State;
national workers who are employed in a non-EU country for the purposes of providing a service;
non-EU workers working for a service that is provided by an EU country.
Employment and equality of treatment
National workers enjoy the same rights and benefits as national workers in matters of housing, including ownership of the housing he needs.
They enjoy all the same social and tax advantages that national workers enjoy, including:
access to the same health and pension schemes;
the same working conditions as nationals;
equal access to housing and working conditions;
equivalence in terms of working hours and pay;
health and safety;
equality of treatment with regard to working conditions, including working hours, pay, working conditions and working time;
protection of workers’ rights, including protection against discrimination;
social and tax benefits, including equal treatment as nationals.
Rights and benefits of employment
A worker who is a national of a Member State and who is employed in another Member State is entitled to the following rights:
full employment;
full and equal access to health, safety and protection;
compulsory paid leave;
time off;
reciprocal access to paid holidays and leave.
EU countries must regularly send to the specialist services of other Member States and to the European Coordination Office the following information:
the number of vacancies which could be filled by nationals of other EU States;
details of vacancies addressed to third countries;
information, by region and by branch of activity, on applicants who have declared themselves actually willing to accept employment in another country. Such information must be brought up to date regularly.
An Advisory Committee
The Advisory Committee is composed of representatives of trade unions and employers’ associations. It meets at least twice a year and is chaired by the European Commission. The term of office of the members and their alternates is 2 years. It may be amended by the Council. The committee may adopt opinions on issues of particular interest to workers from the other countries. It is assisted by expert advisers.
References

Summary 111:
Ozone-depleting potential: Protocol on the protection of the ozone (Protocol on the Protection of the Earth’s Ozone)
Ozone - Depleting Potential
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2003 recasting the Montreal Protocol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It recasts and repeals the Protocol of the Hague Convention on the Prohibition of Pollution from the Emissions of Pollutants (Ozone Protocol).
It aims to ensure that the Protocol is fully implemented.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The protocol applies to all substances that have a potential to deplete the ozone layer. It does not apply to substances that are used as feedstock or process agents.
It does not cover substances used as process agents, such as paints, solvents, paints, paints and varnishes.
The annexes to the regulation set out the conditions for the placing on the market of controlled substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses, and processes in which controlled substances may be used. These conditions are laid down in Annexes I and II of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
the substances concerned are destroyed using approved destruction methods;
the quantities and quantities of the substances concerned must be kept under control;
manufacturers, agents, distributors and importers must keep records of their products and equipment;
they must keep a register of the quantities, quantities and types of substances they have placed on sale.
they may not place on the substances on sale in non-refillable containers, except for laboratory or analytical uses.
the quantity and type of substances must not exceed the limits set out in Annex III of the protocol.
In addition, the annexes set out:
a list of the chemicals that are subject to the protocol;
annexes to Annexes IV and V set out conditions for placing on sale the substances;
a description of the methods of destruction and the quantities of substances to be destroyed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
The Montreal Protocol is the cornerstone of the global effort to protect the environment against the adverse effects of ozone depletion. It aims to protect human health and the environment by:
reducing the ozone-degradation potential of certain substances, in particular those that have been shown to have a significant effect on human health;
protecting the environment and human health by reducing the ozone depletion potential of some substances.
For more information, see:
The Ozone Protocol (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Controlled substances: substances which have a potentially harmful effect on the environment.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2003 of 21 December 2003 recasting and recasting Council Regulation (EC).

Summary 112:
European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment (EURE) (codified version)
European Union agency for the protection of the environment (Eureka)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 on the European Union’s Environmental Protection Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) Environment Protection Agency.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the EUREKA is to:
ensure that the EU has a high level of environmental protection;
provide the European Commission with the necessary information on the environmental situation in the EU;
assist the Member States in the implementation of their national environmental policies and programmes;
support the development of new environmental measurement methods and instruments.
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the agency.
EUREKA:
is responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating environmental information;
monitoring and analysing environmental phenomena;
developing and sharing information on environmental problems;
coordinating the activities of the national environmental information networks;
ensuring that national authorities have access to the information it collects and disseminates;
carrying out research and development activities in the areas covered by its mandate.
It:
cooperates with other EU bodies and programmes, notably the Joint Research Centre, the Statistical Office of the European Communities (Eurostat) and the European Environment Agency (EEA);
provides technical assistance to Member States on environmental issues;
is assisted by the European Monitoring Centre (EMC).
is funded by the EU budget.
is independent of the Commission.
Its budget is adopted by the Management Board, which is composed of representatives of Member States and chaired by the Director-General for the Environment.
Budget
The Eureka budget is based on the revenue and expenditure of the EU. It covers the period from 1 January to 31 December of the following year:
the general budget;
the programme for the period 1 January-31 December of that year.
Each year, the management board adopts a draft budget. The draft budget must be approved by the Commission and the Council of Europe. It must contain the following information:
a description of the objectives of the programme;
a list of the priorities for the year;
an estimate of the costs of implementing the programme.
In addition, the budget must include:
details of the sources of revenue, expenditure and revenue-raising measures to be taken;
measures to be made by Member States to ensure that the budget is in balance;
cost-benefit analysis of the expenditure;
procedures to be adopted to ensure the sustainability of the budget; and
the number of employees.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
 
  The EUREka has been operating since 2001.
For more information, see:
The European Union's Environment Agency
The EU's EureKA (European Commission).

Summary 113:
Organisations’ environmental performance assessment (EMAS) — European environmental performance management system (EASM) (European Commission)
Organisations' environmental performance measurement system (EEMS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the European Environmental Performance Management System (EPSM)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for the management of the environmental performance of organisations.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the organisation’s management, audit and environmental performance monitoring system, known as EMAS.
It aims to improve the quality of environmental performance in the EU by:
encouraging organisations to participate in EMAS;
providing them with information and assistance on how to improve their environmental performance;
ensuring that organisations’ internal environmental audits are carried out;
establishing an audit programme and audit frequency;
assessing the organisation's environmental performance.
EMAS
It is based on the principle of ‘environmental performance management’. It is designed to:
ensure that organisations have a clear, understandable and unambiguous approach to environmental management;
give an accurate appraisal of the organisation;
allow for a year-on-year comparison to assess the development of the environment performance of the company;
enable for comparison with sector, national or regional benchmarks as appropriate;
make it possible to compare with regulatory requirements as appropriate.
The European Commission is assisted by the Forum of Competent Bodies.
the European Environment Action Programme (EAP) and the Committee of the European Parliament and of the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE APPLY?
The EMAS regulation applies from 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The EASM is part of the ‘six pillars’ of the EU environmental action programme.
For more information, see:
‘Ensuring environmental performance’ on the EAP website.
* KEY TERMS
* Environmental performance management: the way an organisation manages its environment and its resources.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004/EC of 21 December 2004 establishing the European Environment Performance Management Systems (EESM).

Summary 114:
Protection of spirit drinks and the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks (Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the protection, description, presentation and labelling of spirits and their geographical indications
Protection, description or labelling: spirit drinks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition, description and presentation, presentation, labelling, and protection of the geographical indications (GIs) of certain spirits and spirit drinks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks, regardless of their origin. It does not apply to:
alcoholic spirits;
liqueurs and liqueurs of agricultural origin;
rum-verschnitt;
natural spirits.
It does not cover:
liquor or liqueur-based spirit drinks;
wine and spirit-based wine;
coconut spirit;
pomegranate spirit.
Labelling
A spirit drink must bear the following information:
the name of the product;
the alcoholic strength;
a description of its composition;
an indication of its origin.
This information must be accompanied by a description of the production methods used and by-products formed in fermentation.
In addition, the product may be given special organoleptic characteristics, other than a predominant flavour, such as a fruity or fruity flavour, which are derived from the raw materials used.
The product must be labelled in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex II of the regulation.
Rules on labelling
Labels
The label must contain:
a list of the spirit drink’s alcoholic strength by volume,
the maximum alcoholic strength
the number of litres of the drink
the quantity of alcohol per litre
the volume of alcohol in each volume
the geographical indication
the description of each spirit drink
an explanation of the origin of the alcohol in the product.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of rules on the regulation to ensure that spirit drinks comply with the requirements of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU). These include:
rules on the labelling and description of spirits
rules for the description of spirit products
rules concerning the description, description of their presentation and label
rules regarding the protection for geographical indications.
For more information, see:
‘Spirit Drinks’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Spirit drink: a spirit drink obtained by maceration of unfermented fruits and berries listed in category 16(a)(ii) or vegetables, nuts, or other plant materials such as herbs or rose petals in ethyl alcohol, followed by distillation at less than 86% vol.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Regulation(EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States concerning the regulation relating to the regulation on the definition and presentation of spirits (OJ L 31, 1.2.2008, pp. 1-2)

Summary 115:
Civil aviation security: common rules for safeguarding passengers and cargo and mail
Civil aviation safety: common standards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — common rules on civil aviation security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out common rules to ensure the safety and security of passengers, cargo, baggage and mail on board aircraft.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all flights within the European Union (EU). It does not apply to flights to non-EU countries.
EU countries must draw up, apply and maintain an airport security programme.
Airport operators must:
ensure that security measures are applied to aircraft, passengers and cabin baggage;
apply alternative security controls to ensure that the security standards applied to the aircraft are not compromised;
monitor and monitor the implementation of the security measures;
have a national quality control programme;
provide the European Commission with a list of the measures they have implemented.
The Commission must publish measures that have a direct impact on passengers.
Penalties must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive for infringements of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
It applies from 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the security of its aviation sector. These include:
rules on:
airport security;
passenger screening;
aircraft security checks
air traffic management
airports security.
For more information, see:
European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
KEY TERMS
* Passenger screening: an inspection of those parts of the interior of the aircraft to which passengers may have had access, in order to detect prohibited articles and unlawful interferences with the aircraft;
Cargo security check: a check of the hold of an aircraft to check that there are no weapons, explosives or other prohibited items on board.

Summary 116:
Freezing funds and economic resources of those responsible for serious human rights violations or abuses: human rights abuses or bodies responsible for providing support to or otherwise involved in such violations
Freezing of funds and other economic resources for those involved in serious human-rights violations and abuses
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the freezing of funds, economic resources and the prohibition to make funds available to, or to any natural or legal person, entity or body responsible for, providing support for, or other abuses or abuses, as well as those associated with the natural and legal entities and bodies covered by this regulation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the EU’s financial and economic interests by:
further restricting access to the EU budget;
restricting the movement of funds or economic resources;
banning the transfer of funds to or from the EU;
imposing sanctions on persons or bodies involved in or associated with these activities.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
natural or legal persons, entities or bodies acting through or on behalf of one of the bodies referred to in point (i.e. any person or body acting in accordance with the law of an EU Member State);
any natural, legal or commercial person or business doing business in the territory of the Member State;
any person or legal entity acting in respect of any business done in whole or in part within the Union.
The Member State concerned must inform the other Member States and the Commission of any authorisation granted under the regulation within 2 weeks of the authorisation. The Member States must also inform the Commission and the other EU countries of any changes in the addresses of their websites listed in Annex II.
Member States must notify the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council of the European Union and the European Economic and Social Committee of the EU on any changes to the addresses listed in the annexes to the regulation.
In addition, the Member States are required to inform the European Commission of:
the nature and nature of the frozen funds;
the value of frozen funds and information on authorisations granted by the competent authorities.
Any Member State may freeze the funds of an individual or group of persons or entities if it considers that they have committed or are likely to commit serious violations of human rights or abuses.
If a Member State considers that an individual, group or business has committed or is suspected of committing serious violations, it must immediately freeze all funds and any economic resources belonging to that person or group.
A Member State must also notify the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) of any other Member State that has granted an authorisation under this regulation. The OLAF must inform OLAF of any additional information received directly by the Commission.
Annex III of the regulation contains a list of persons and entities covered by the regulation, including:
individuals, entities, bodies and bodies acting on their own behalf;
legal persons, bodies or businesses acting through, on behalf or on the behalf of any of these bodies;
natural, legal, commercial or commercial persons or businesses, including any natural, commercial persons and businesses, which is incorporated or constituted under the law or rules of an Member State.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND

EU military mission in Libya

Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 on a European Union military operation on the Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to provide military assistance to the United Nations (UN) Mission in Libya (UNMIL) and the International Criminal Court (ICC) in order to prevent the illicit export of arms and related materiel from Libya.
KEY POINTS

Summary 118:
Fraudulent documents: European image archiving system (FADO) (European Union Agency for the Operational Management of Large-Scale IT Systems in the Area of Freedom, Security and Justice)
Document fraud can undermine the internal security of the European Union (EU) and undermine the Schengen acquis. This is why the European Image Archiving System, known as FADO, was set up to facilitate the exchange of information on authentic documents and on known methods of falsification between Member State authorities.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1726 of 14 November 2018 on the establishment of a European Image archiving System for Authentic Documents.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes an EU-wide system for the storage and management of authentic and false official documents.
It sets up a system to store and exchange information on documents that have been forged, counterfeited or falsified.
KEY POINTS
The system is designed to:
ensure that authentic documents are kept for at least 5 years;
provide the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (the ‘Agency’) with the information on the documents referred to in the first subparagraph of the regulation;
protect the security and integrity of the EU’s external borders;
facilitate the sharing of information between Member States and the EU Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation.
The Agency is responsible for the operation of the system and the management of the information it holds. It has the responsibility for:
operating the system;
ensuring that the system is up-to-date;
collecting, storing and exchanging information on official documents;
monitoring and analysing the data it holds;
assisting the European Commission in the implementation of delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Image Archive System (FAS) is a large-scale IT system set up by the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission. It is the first of its kind in the world.
For more information, see:
‘Facing the threat of fraud’ on the FAS website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Regulation (EU), Euratom of the Parliament and of the Council of 11 November 2018 establishing a European image archive system for authentic documents (European Image Archive System) (OJ L 333, 9.12.2018, pp. 1-3)
Successive amendments and corrections to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 119:
Access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions: justification and proportionality of rules
Access to and pursuit of regulated professions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2005/36/EC on the regulation of the professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the quality of life of EU citizens by:
improving access to and access to regulated professions;
ensuring that the quality and safety of these professions is guaranteed;
protecting the health of the public.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the following professions:
physicians;
medical professionals;
social workers;
veterinary workers.
It does not apply to:
administrative professions.
The scope of the directive is limited to the professions listed in the directive’s Annex II.
Access
EU countries must ensure that the regulation does not restrict access to or access to the regulated professions. It must be based on the following criteria:
the importance of the objectives pursued;
the risks to the public interest; and
the expected gains.
Proportionality
The regulation must be proportionate to the importance of these objectives.
In addition, EU countries must take account of the nature of the risks related to these objectives, in particular the risks to service recipients, including consumers, to professionals or to third parties.
Exemptions
The European Union (EU) Member States may exclude certain professions from the scope of this directive if they consider that they do not meet the objectives of the regulation.
Regulation of certain professions may be justified by the need to protect the public health or the safety of the health or safety of third parties or the public, or for the protection of the environment or the environment.
If the regulation is not proportionate, it should not be applied.
Member States must also ensure that:
there is no restriction to access to, access to professions which are not regulated by the regulation;
there are no restrictions on access to a profession which is not regulated.
There should be no restriction on the access of professionals to professions that are regulated by Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the establishment of a regulated profession.

Summary 120:
Fishery management of demersal stocks and by-catch stocks (Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013)
Fishing of demerals
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/35/EU on the conservation of fishery resources and the protection of marine ecosystems through technical measures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fisheries and marine ecosystems are protected against overfishing, illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing and destructive fishing practices, in order to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce MSY as determined by their biological characteristics.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
fishery resources, i.e. the stocks of fish, crustaceans, mussels, clams, anchovies, swordfish, porpoises, porbeagle species, muskellunge species, molluscs, muskrat species, porcine species and porpoise species.
It does not apply to the following:
by-catch (by-fishing)
fishing of fish caught by non-EU countries’ fishing fleets;
bycatch of marine mammals (e.g. sharks, skates and skates).
By-catch of certain species, such as swordfish and mussels.
Fisheries and marine mammals that are taken in mixed fisheries.
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts in the following areas:
management of by-caught fish stocks;
the management of fish stocks that are caught in mixed fishing;
monitoring of marine biodiversity;
management and monitoring of marine life and ecosystems;
reciprocal management of marine resources.
Specific conservation measures
The Commission may adopt specific conservation measures:
when scientific advice indicates that remedial action is required for the conservation or re-establishment of any of the stocks referred to in Article 1(4) of this regulation, or when the spawning stock biomass and the abundance of Norway lobster stocks are below MSY B;
when the biomass and abundance of the species for which are covered by the regulation for a given year are below the maximum sustainable yield (MSY B), the Commission may take the necessary measures to restore the stocks to MSY levels.
Where the Commission takes into account a significant impact of recreational fisheries in the framework of the fishing opportunities for a certain stock, it may set a TAC for commercial catches which takes account of the volume of recreational catches and/or to adopt other measures restricting recreational fisheries such as bag limits and closure periods.
Regional cooperation
The EU and its Member States are required to cooperate in the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Summit on Sustainable Development held in New York in 2015 called on the EU and Member States to effectively regulate harvesting, to end illegal fishing, illegal fishing and unreported fishing and to implement science-based management plans.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing for sustainable fishing’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* By-catch: the catch of fish that is not intended for commercial use.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1410/2014 of 21 December 2014 on the Conservation and Protection of Fishery Resources and the Protection of Marine Ecosystems through Technical Measures (Codification Regulation) (OJ L 304, 28.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2014/34/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 122:
Control of cash entering and leaving the European Union (EU) — rules on the declaration of cash and the exchange of information between EU countries and non-EU countries
Control of money and the exchanging of information among EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2005 on controls of cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that cash is declared to the competent authorities of the EU country through which it is entering or leaving the EU and make it available to them for control.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to cash:
cash originating in the EU;
cash intended for the financing of criminal activities;
money intended for terrorist financing.
It does not apply to cash intended for:
the financing of terrorism;
the funding of organised crime;
drug trafficking;
human trafficking; or
money laundering.
The competent authorities must:
make a declaration of the origin, destination, economic provenance and intended use of the cash;
make it available for control;
keep a copy of the declaration for at least 3 years;
ensure that the declaration is delivered to the authorities in the country of origin;
temporary detention of cash by competent authorities.
Exchange of information with third countries
EU countries may exchange information between a competent authority of a Member State or the Commission and the authorities of a third country provided that there are appropriate safeguards.
In addition to the list of information to be exchanged, the authorities may also exchange information on:
on the origin and destination of cash; and
the identity of the sender or the recipient.
the nature and purpose of cash.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDITION APPLY?
The original regulation entered into force on 1 July 2005. It has been amended several times, most recently in 2017.
BACKGROUND
The introduction of a new EU law on the control of cash is part of a package of measures designed to protect the EU’s financial interests.
For more information, see:
‘Controls of cash’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 123:
Energy and climate change: EU energy and climate progress report
Energy, climate change and climate report
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 517/2014 on the EU’s long-term strategy for energy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the reporting and reporting of energy, climate and climate data to the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy security;
the internal energy market;
energy efficiency; decarbonisation;
research, innovation and competitiveness.
EU countries must:
report to the Commission every 2 years on the progress they have made in achieving their objectives;
report annually on the policies and measures adopted or intended to be adopted and implemented to achieve these objectives.
The data must cover all fuel and energy placed on the market in each EU country and be available to the public by 30 September every year.
Each EU country must ensure that:
the Commission publishes an annual report on the implementation of its national energy strategy;
it publishes an integrated national energy progress report every 3 years;
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the measures it has adopted to achieve its objectives and the progress it has made;
provide the Commission with a report on its annual progress report;
publish an annual energy efficiency report.
In addition, EU countries must provide the Commission and the European Commission with:
a report on energy efficiency and climate action plan for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2030;
a national report on climate and energy policy;
an annual energy report on progress made by EU countries;
reports on the results of the national climate action plans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 30 September 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU is committed to achieving a low-carbon energy economy by 2050.
For more information, see:
‘The Energy Union’ (European Commission).

* CO2 emissions: total concentration of GHGs in the atmosphere.
* GHG: the total amount of greenhouse gases (GHGs) emitted into the atmosphere by burning, burning, producing, transporting, storing or using energy.

Summary 124:
EU rules on substances listed in Annex III of the Convention on Substances of Pollution from Chemicals (COPD) (Recast) (OJ L 334, 17.12.2016)
EU rules covering substances of Pollutants listed in Appendix III of The Hague Convention
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — Listing of substances of pollutants (Annex III)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the listing and placing on the market and use of certain substances of pollution from chemicals in the European Union (EU).
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
any substance listed in the annex to the convention;
any substances that are present in mixtures or articles in the EU’s internal market;
waste consisting of, containing or contaminated by any substance listed.
It does not apply to wastes and lead-containing leaching residues of the non-ferrous metal production.
Listing
Annex I lists the substances to which the EU must apply its obligations under the convention.
The list includes:
the substances to be listed;
the quantities to be released by the EU countries;
measures to identify, characterise and minimise, with a view to eliminating where feasible as soon as possible, the total releases of these substances;
information to the European Commission, the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW) and the European Monitoring Centre (EMHCWaste Management).
EU countries must keep inventories of substances in their inventories, as well in advance of their introduction on the EU market.
National authorities must ensure that the inventories are kept up-to-date and that they contain information on:
total releases of substances listed by EU countries,
the total releases from substances in Annex I and II;
total annual releases from Annex III as recorded in inventories drawn up in accordance with their national implementation plans.
In addition, EU countries must:
make available to the Commission and the other EU countries all relevant data on total releases;
ensure that national authorities keep a record of total releases and total annual waste from substances listed. They must also ensure that these data are accessible to the public.
Each EU country must also:
keep a register of the substances in its inventories;
keep an inventory of waste and waste-contaminants;
establish a committee to monitor the implementation of the regulation;
draw up, implement and update national action plans;
provide the Commission with information on the substances listed, including the quantities of waste, which are to be disposed of and the quantities that may be released.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation applies from 16 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on Pollutant Substances is the cornerstone of the EU strategy to reduce greenhouse gas emissions from industrial activities. It aims to protect human health, the environment and the economy.
For more information, see:
The Hague Convention (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Waste: any substance that can be contaminated by, contains or is likely to be contaminated with a substance listed on the annex.
* Contaminants: substances that have the potential to contaminate, contain or are likely to become contaminated with, or may be present in, waste.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2017 of 16 July 2017 on the Listing and Placing on the Market and Use of Persistent Release of Chemicals from Chemists’ Inventories of Substances, Annex III (Recasting Regulation)

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products: EU rules on their disposal and recycling
Single-used plastic products; reducing litter
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/13/EU on the use of plastic
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce plastic waste in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules on the placing on the market and recycling of single-use products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the following products:
wet wipes, i.e. pre-wetted personal care and domestic wipes;
balloons, except balloons for industrial or other professional uses and applications that are not distributed to consumers;
lightweight plastic carrier bags as defined in point 1c of Article 3 of Directive 94/62/EC;
sanitary towels (pads), tampons and tampon applicators.
It does not apply to:
toy products with filters containing plastic.
EU countries must ensure that the packaging of these products is clearly visible and clearly labelled.
Labelling
Labels must be clearly visible on the packaging and on the product itself. They must also bear the following information:
the product’s name and address;
the number of times it has been used;
a description of the purpose for which the product is intended;
how it is intended to be disposed of.
The labelling must be visible on all packaging and the product must bear the number of trips or rotations by being refilled or re-used for the same purpose as the product.
the quantity of plastic used.
a clear description of how the product will be recycled and reused.
in addition, the labelling and packaging must indicate that it will be reused or recycled.
In addition, EU countries must set up separate collection systems for waste fishing gear containing plastic, as well as for waste plastic collected and report to the European Commission on the collection rates for this waste.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The plastic waste directive is part of a package of measures designed to tackle plastic pollution in the EU. The other measures include:
measures to reduce litter from post-consumption waste of tobacco products with filter containing plastic;
measures designed to reduce the amount of plastic waste generated from the manufacture, packaging, transport and disposal of food and beverage containers.
For more information, see:
Plastic waste: EU action plan (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Single-use product: a product which is intended for use only once or for a short period of time before being disposed of and which is not designed for re-use or recycling.
* Medium-density plastic (MDP): a plastic material that can be reused, recycled or reused multiple times.
ACT

Summary 126:
EU trade mark registration
European Union (EU) trade mark registry
European trade mark registrar
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the registration of EU trade marks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system for the registration and authorisation of trade marks in the EU.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 139/2003.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to applications for registration of the EU trade mark. It does not apply to:
international applications;
conversion applications; or
designation of goods and services under the Madrid Protocol.
The registration of a trade mark must be renewed at the request of the proprietor of the trade mark or any other person expressly authorised by him, provided that the fees have been paid.
An application for registration must contain the following information:
the name and address of the applicant;
the goods or services concerned;
a description of the products or services intended for use;
an explanation of the origin of the mark;
any other textual elements forming part of the international application.
A registration application must be filed in the language indicated when the application was filed. It must be accompanied by:
a declaration of the identity of the person or body responsible for registering the mark,
a copy of the registration application and the declaration of its validity;
documents relating to the registration;
information concerning the goods or service;
procedures of the name and place of use of the trademark.
If an application is refused, the applicant must provide the reasons for the refusal, including the goods and service concerned.
the applicant’s name, address and postal address.
Application procedure
The applicant must submit a request for registration to the European Office for the Registration of Trade Marks (OOR).
The OOR may refuse to register an application if it considers that the applicant has not fulfilled the conditions laid down in the application or that the application does not comply with the conditions set out in the annex to the regulation.
Requests for registration are made in the following languages:
English
French
German
Italian
Spanish
German.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
From 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘EU trade marks’ on the OOR’ website.

Summary 127:
Accessible format copies for the benefit of the blind, visually impaired and otherwise print-disabled
Accessible form copies for beneficiaries of the Marrakesh Treaty
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the making and dissemination of accessible format copies of certain works and other protected subject matter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the availability of accessible formats for beneficiary copies in all of the Member States of the EU and the circulation of such copies within the internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to copyright and related rights for:
the making and distribution of copies, in accessible formats, of certain protected subjects;
the importation of, and access to, these copies from a non-EU country, by authorised entities established in an EU country, for non-commercial purposes; and
the exchange of these copies between authorised entities.
It does not apply to:
copyright, copyright-related rights, or related rights;
rights relating to the reproduction, reproduction, distribution or reproduction of works or other protected subjects, including:
books, films, musicals, plays, musical compositions and other works of literary, artistic or visual interest;
works of literary or artistic merit;
books and musical compositions, plays or musical compositions or works of non-musical interest.
Authorised entities
An authorised entity may distribute, communicate or make available to beneficiary or to an organisation that is responsible for its primary activities, institutional obligations or as part of its public-interest missions.
as well as to an authorisation.
The authorised entity must:
make available to beneficiaries copies of protected subjects and works in accessible format;
provide them with the necessary information, such as their names, addresses and contact details;
ensure that these copies are made available to the beneficiary or the organisation responsible for their primary activities;
use the information provided by the authorised entity for the purpose of its activities or for its institutional obligations;
keep copies of the accessible format for the purposes of its own use;
make copies available to other authorised entities for their own use.
EU countries must ensure that authorised entities do not use the personal data of beneficiary persons for commercial purposes.
Exceptions to the above rules
The EU countries may:
use exceptions to certain rights that are harmonised by EU law;
exchange of the information contained in these exceptions;
notify the European Commission of any exceptions to these rules;
allow the Commission to review these exceptions in the light of technological progress;
introduce rules on the protection of personal data and on the free movement of such data.

Summary 128:
Coordinated action to combat fraud and abuse of credit agreements for consumers: Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 (see summary)
Cooperative action to curb fraud and abusive use of credit agencies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 184/2011 — coordinated action for the prevention, control and enforcement of consumer fraud
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve consumer protection in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules on the coordination of actions between EU countries’ consumer protection authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all consumer credit agreements (i.e. credit agreements) with a European dimension. It does not apply to credit agreements with a non-EU dimension.
EU countries must notify the European Commission if they have a reasonable suspicion that a widespread infringement with a Union dimension has occurred.
The Commission must notify EU countries, through the competent authorities and single liaison offices concerned by that infringement, stating the grounds which justify a possible coordinated action in the notification.
If the Commission has reason to believe that an infringement has occurred, it should notify the relevant authorities of other EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries.
Cooperation
The competent authorities of the EU countries must:
share information about the suspected infringement covered by the regulation;
cooperate with each other and with the Commission on coordinated actions;
exchange information on suspected infringements;
coordinate actions with the European Anti-Fraud Office (EAFO).
Coordination of coordinated actions
The coordinated actions are coordinated through the EAFO is responsible for:
monitoring the alerts issued by competent authorities;
designating a coordinator;
providing information to the Commission;
sharing information with the other countries on the coordinated actions.
In addition, the coordinated action must include:
a description of the infringement;
the reasons for the notification;
a list of the companies involved in the infringement.
Information exchange between the authorities and the Commission
the names and contact details of the coordinators.
A coordinated action may be launched by the competent authority or the Commission if:
there is reason to suspect that the infringement is widespread;
there are grounds for a coordinated action involving more than one EU country;
it is likely to have a significant impact on the economy of the country concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the protection of consumers in the EU, including:
Directive 2008/48/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 April 2008 on credit agreements and repealing Council Directive 87/102/EEC (OJ L 133, 22.5.2008, pp. 1-5);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 2006/2004 and (EC), No 18/2009 on consumer protection and Regulation (EEC) 2015/849 on consumer information and on consumer rights.
For more information, see:
‘Cooperation’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Coordinated action: coordinated action: an action to prevent, control or enforce a consumer fraud or abuse of a credit agreement. It includes:
alerting the authorities of another EU country to a suspected infringement,
coordinating actions,
exchanging information between authorities, and
coordination of actions with other countries and bodies.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EG) No 185/2011 of 21 December 2011 on coordinated action on consumer fraud and abusing credit agencies for consumers (recast version) (See summary).

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices: European Union (EU) safety and health rules for in vitro diagnostics
In vitro diagnostic medical devices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/745 on in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the safety and quality of in vitro medical devices (i.e. medical devices designed to diagnose, monitor and treat diseases).
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the European Union’s general rules on the labelling and labelling of medical devices.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
class A and class B devices;
class D devices; and
class C devices. It does not apply to devices designed for use in the laboratory.
Manufacturers must draw up and keep up-to-date the technical documentation for those devices. This must include:
a description of the device and its characteristics;
a product description;
an indication of the purpose and purpose of the product;
the description of its purpose and the purpose of its use.
Labelling
Class A devices must bear the European Medicines Agency (EMA) UDI (United Nations Industrial Device Identifier).
Class B devices must be labelled with the UDI and must bear their own UDI.
Class C devices must carry a UDI number.
The UDI must appear on the packaging of class A devices. For class B and C devices, it must be printed on the label. For single use class A and B devices packaged and labelled individually, the label must be written in the same language as the label, except where it is written in a language other than ‘in vitro diagnostic’, ‘single use’ or ‘multi-use’.
For class D devices, a label must contain the following information:
the name of the manufacturer and the name and address of the notified body responsible for monitoring the device,
the number of samples taken and the reasons for and the nature of the modifications made to the device.
Where a class D device has been modified, the EU country in which the modification was carried out must inform the European Commission, the European Agency for Medicines Safety and Health and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) of the reason for the modification, the nature and duration of the modification and the results of the evaluation.
EU countries must notify the Commission of any modifications that have been made to a class A or class B device and, where applicable, the EFSA of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and modernise the regulation in order to ensure that it is consistent with EU law. These include the following:
—
the introduction of EU-wide harmonised labelling requirements for class A diagnostic devices (see Annex II);
the harmonisation of labelling for class B diagnostic devices and class C diagnostic devices, and
the establishment of a European labelling system for class D diagnostic devices for single-use devices (See Annexes III and IV).
The Commission has also adopted a series of measures designed to ensure the uniform application of the regulation.
In addition, the regulation sets out the rules for the monitoring and monitoring of the quality and safety of class C and class D medical devices, as well as the monitoring of their performance.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 130:
EU rules on appliances and fittings — conformity assessment and safety information (EC)
EU rules governing appliances and fitting (EC).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the harmonisation of technical harmonisation and standards for the sale and putting into service of electrical appliances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the technical requirements relating to the design, manufacturing, provision, assembly, use and maintenance of electrical and electronic appliances.
It sets out rules on the labelling and safety of these products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
electrical appliances* and electrical fittings* designed and manufactured in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to the following products:
cooking appliances;
circuit-circuit televisions;
electromagnetic equipment;
radio equipment; and
electro-medical equipment.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the appliance bears the CE conformity marking;
it is accompanied by a label with the manufacturer’s name, registered trade name or registered trade mark;
the label bears the type, batch or serial number of the appliance or other element allowing its identification;
its packaging appliance contains the necessary information for adjustment, operation and maintenance;
instructions and documentation must be provided in a language easily understood by consumers and other end-users;
there is a single point at which the manufacturer can be contacted.
Importers must ensure the appliance is accompanied and its data plate bears the same information as the label.
in addition to the CE marking, the label must bear the following the following information:
name of the manufacturer;
type of electrical supply used, where applicable;
number of electrical connections;
frequency of power supply;
power supply pressure;
volume of electrical current;
pressure of the electrical supply.
Labelling
The label must contain:
manufacturers’ name and trade name;
name and trade mark.
In addition, it must bear:
an EU declaration of conformity (CE marking);
the type of electrical connection used, the nominal supply pressure for the appliance and the nominal electrical current for the fittings;
an indication of the type of fittings as part of the finished appliance, as appropriate;
a description of the operation of the appliances and their fittings.
Instructions
The manufacturer must:
make available on the market and putting in service the appliance in accordance with Annex I of the regulation;
provide the consumer with the information and documentation necessary to ensure correct and safe installation;
ensure that the appliance has been tested and certified to ensure that it complies with the requirements of this regulation.
Information and documentation
The product must be accompanied by:
a label in the language of the EU country concerned, in line with Annex II;
information in the format set out in Annex III, in order to facilitate the exchange of information between the manufacturer and the end-user;
implementing measures to ensure the safety of the product, including:
identification and documentation;
recalling the appliance if it is found to pose a risk to health and safety;
identifying and recalling it if there is a risk of a fire or explosion.
EU countries must ensure:
importers and distributors ensure that the label and the label are clearly visible on the product;
they ensure that they provide the appliance with the required information in the form of a label or label;
all necessary information is written in an easy-to-read language that can be understood and understood by the consumer.
The information must include:
contact details for the manufacturer, including the name of the importer and the postal address where the manufacturer may be contacted;
where the manufacturer is located, the address must indicate the number of distributors authorised to carry out business activities;
authorisations and authorisations must be issued by the national authority responsible for the safety and health of the products concerned.
Examination and conformity assessment
The European Commission must carry out an examination and testing of every appliance or fitting to check that it meets the essential safety requirements. It must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on the Functioning of the European Atomic Energy Community on the basis of the results of these tests.
If the Commission considers that an appliance does not conform to the essential requirements, it may issue a decision to recall it or to recall the product.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It applies from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is based on the ‘new approach’ principles, as laid down in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical harmonisations and standards.
For more information, see:
Electromagnetic devices (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
 



Summary 131:
Cross-border cash-in-transit (CIT) transport
Cross-Border cash transport
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 96/71/EC on the cross-border transport of euro cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the security of the transport of cash in euro-denominated euro banknotes and euro coins.
It establishes minimum protection rules for the transport and security staff of euro notes, euro coins and euro notes by road.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
cross-border transactions involving cash in transit;
cash in transit between national central banks;
categories of cash;
euro banknotes;
currency notes.
Requirements for CIT companies
CIT companies must:
have a valid CIT licence issued by the national central bank of the EU country in whose territory they are established;
be able to show the inspection authorities the original or a certified copy of a valid cross-banknote-carrying licence;
have security staff with bulletproof vests during the transport;
ensure that their vehicles are equipped with a global navigation system (GNSS);
have an end-to-end IBNS (Interbank Identification Number System (IBNS) that can be programmed in a secure location.
The CIT security staff must be:
certified by the competent national authority of the participating EU country;
equipped with the necessary equipment to carry out security checks on CIT vehicles;
inform the national authorities of their specific training requirements;
carry out the necessary for the security staff;
wear the bulletproof Vests referred to in point (c);
wear them where required by the law of the Member State where they are situated.
EU countries must ensure that:
the CIT company’s CIT vehicle is equipped with the GNSS;
the security staff have the appropriate training and appropriate training;
there is a minimum rate of pay, including overtime rates, for the CIT drivers, security staff and CIT cash centre staff.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
It applies from 1 January 1996.
BACKGROUND
   
The euro area is one of the most dynamic economies in the world. It is the largest economy in Europe and has a high rate of economic growth.
For more information, see:
‘Cash in transit’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* CIT: a company that carries out cash transport within the euro area. The term ‘citizen’ is used to refer to any person, organisation or business that is established in an EU country and has the right to transport cash.

Summary 133:
Issuing of euro coins: circulation and collector coins
Issuance of euros: circulation coins
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the issuance of circulation coins, collector coins and 2-euro commemorative coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the issuing and circulation of euro-denominated coins.
It aims to ensure that euro coins are legal tender throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to coins issued by the European Central Bank (ECB).
EU countries may issue two types of euro coin: circulating circulation coins* (circulation coins) and euro collector coins (collector coins) which are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
The circulation coins must comply with the denominations and technical specifications laid down in the regulation.
Collector coins may be put on the market at or above face value. They must not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
They must be clearly and easily recognisable on the coin and must bear the identity of the issuing EU country.
There are limits set per year and per issuing Member State for the number of issues of such coins. The total number of collector coins must not exceed 0.1 % of the cumulated total net number of 2 euro coins put into circulation by all Member States whose currency was the euro up to the beginning of the year preceding the 1-year preceding the issue of the 2-coin.
Number of euro commemorative coin
The number of 1-euro coins issued must be limited to no more than 2,000,000.
1-euro collector coins may not exceed the following limits:
0.1.1% of the cumulative total net net number issued by Member States for each individual issue;
0,1.5% for each issue for each Member State.
2.
Commission Regulation (EU) 2019/852 amends the regulation to allow for the possibility to issue 1-cent and 2.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 1998.
BACKGROUND
The euro has been legal tender in the EU since 1 January 1999.
For more information, see:
‘Issuing euro coins’ on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Circulation coins: coins denominated in euro and cent and complying with the regulations laid down by the Council.
* Collector coins: euro coins intended for collection.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/952 of 24 June 2018 on the issue and circulation limits of euro collector coin and 2 euro commemoration coins and repealing Council Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 852/98 (OJ C 273, 16.9.2018, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 1052/2018 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 134:
European Social Fund
European social fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 establishing the European Social Fund (ESF)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the mission and scope of the European social fund (ESSF).
It sets out the thematic objectives for the ESF.
KEY POINTS
The ESF’s objective is to promote social inclusion and combating poverty, in particular with regard to disadvantaged people such as the Roma. The ESF contributes to the creation of a sustainable, inclusive, inclusive and inclusive society.
The objectives are:
promoting social inclusion, combating poverty and any discrimination;
contributing to promoting equal opportunities;
encouraging the development of a knowledge-based economy;
improving the quality of education and training;
increasing employability;
supporting research and innovation;
strengthening human capital development.
Objectives
The main objectives are to:
support social inclusion;
combat poverty;
promote equal opportunities for disadvantaged people;
fight racism and xenophobia;
ensure the protection of the rights of women and minorities.
Specific investment priorities
EU countries may set up specific investment priorities for specific thematic priorities, such as:
the creation of an integrated urban development strategy;
the promotion of sustainable urban development through strategies setting out integrated actions to tackle the economic, environmental and social challenges affecting the urban areas identified by the EU countries on the basis of the principles laid down in their respective Partnership Agreements.
the development of an inclusive, sustainable and inclusive economy.
support for young people.
Supporting young people
The European education initiative (YEI) may support actions to combat early school leaving. The YEI may also contribute to cultural and creative skills.
Promoting equal opportunities
The aim of the programme is to ensure that young people have equal opportunities and that they have access to equal opportunities, irrespective of their gender, race, ethnic origin, religion, language, age, sexual orientation, disability, age of birth, gender identity or sexual orientation.
In addition, the programme aims to promote equal opportunities by:
providing young people with access to quality education;
raising awareness and understanding of gender equality;
developing a culture of equal opportunities.
Eligibility of expenditure
The EU countries may finance the following activities:
research and innovation (R&I);
education;
employment;
training;
health and safety at work;
creative skills;
science and technology;
environmental protection;
social inclusion.
Financing
The allocation of ESF resources in each EU country must be calculated taking into account the co-financing rate of the overall ESF allocation together with the special allocation for the Youth Employment Initiative (YEI).
The priority axis of the priority axis is fixed at 20 % of the total ESF budget.
EU Member States and the European Commission must regularly evaluate the effectiveness, efficiency and impact of the investment priorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The European Society for the Prevention of Poverty and the Eradication of Racial Discrimination’ on the European Council's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EC) No 1301/2013 of 20 December 2013 setting up the European System of Social Funds (ESSF) (OJ L 127, 31.12.2013, pp. 1-3)

Summary 135:
European statistics on population and vital events
European statistics in the field of statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the development, production and dissemination of European statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and exchange of data on the EU’s population, vital events and population statistics.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on:
the total population at national level at the reference time, in accordance with Article 2(c), within 8 months of the end of the reference year;
the number of births, deaths, marriages, divorces, deaths and births of children under the age of 18, as well as the number of deaths and deaths of infants under 18.
The Commission must transmit the data and metadata required under this regulation to Eurostat within 8 weeks of the beginning of the first reference year.
Eurostat must ensure the quality of the data transmitted and ensure that the data are transmitted in a language easily understood by the end-users.
National authorities must ensure that they transmit to the Commission:
data on the total population of the EU, including the total number of people under 18, and the total birth, death, marriage, death and death of children of the population;
information on the number and age of persons under 18 and the birth of children;
statistical data on births and deaths, deaths of children, marriages and deaths;
national statistics on births, marriage and death rates;
international statistics on vital events.
Data collection and transmission
The data transmitted must be:
scientifically-based and well-documented statistical estimation methods, where appropriate;
based on the best available information available;
accurate, reliable and comparable data on birth and death statistics;
data for births, death rates and deaths by sex;
total birth rate and death rate by age;
number of births by sex, age, sex, socioeconomic status and geographic dimensions;
percentage of births in the EU;
death rate by sex and birth rate by country of birth;
countries’ birth rates by age and sex by gender;
population by birth rate;
frequency and reference time
The first reference date for population data is in 2013 and the last reference date is in 2027. The reference period for vital events data is the calendar year in which the events occurred.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide the Commission with:
a description of the events covered by the data;
a list of the countries and regions where the events took place;
an estimate of the number, birth rate, death rate and birth rates of children in each EU country;
identification of the regions and countries covered by vital events;
methods used for the transmission of data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to improve the common legal framework for developing, producing and disseminating European statistics. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the European statistical system (see summary);
Directive 2014/23/EU on the statistical system for the European Union (European Statistical System).
This regulation establishes the European Statistical System (ESS).
KEY TERMS
* Total population: the population of all EU countries.
* Vital events: events that have a direct or indirect impact on the population.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of 21 December 2011 on the collection and dissemination, transmission, transmission of European statistical information on the general population and on population statistics (OJ L 183, 21.12.2011, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 181/11 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Pre- treatment of textile fibre

Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on pre-treatment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a uniform EU-wide system for pre-treating certain textile products.
It aims to ensure that the labelling and labelling of these products is accurate and to ensure the quality of the products. It also aims to protect consumers and the environment.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all textile products, including:
textiles, including clothing, footwear, clothing accessories, tableware, tablecloths, tablecloth covers, table cloths and tableware accessories;
covers for mobile telephones and portable media players with a surface of not more than 160 cm2;
towards the end-of-life of textile products;

This regulation does not apply to:
wearwear;
accessories for children’s mobile phones;
tablecloths;
clothing;
wear accessories for children;
wool;
cotton;
fibres used in clothing.
Labelling
Textile products must bear the following information on their packaging:
the fibre composition;
the number of fibres in the product;
a description of the fibre.
The textiles must be labelled in accordance with the technical specifications laid down in the regulation.
the quantity and type of the product.
Textiles must bear a technical description of their fibre composition.
Fibresin accordance with Regulation (EU) No 10/2014 on the labels of textile product packaging.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Textile fibres’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Pre-treatment: the process by which a natural non-fibrous substance is removed from a textile product in order to remove its natural constituents. This process is known as ‘pre-treatment’. It is the process used to remove the natural constituents of the textile product. The term ‘fibre’ is used to describe the fibres that have been treated with a pre-chemical agent. It does not refer to the fibre itself, but to the components that are present in the finished product, such as the colour, the texture, the colouring or the texture of the surface of the material.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   Â     ‘PRIIP’ refers to a type of investment product designed to provide specific investment solutions tailored to the needs of retail investors, which can be complex and difficult to understand.
It aims to ensure that retail investors have access to the information they need in order to make informed investment decisions.

products marketed to retail investors in the European Union (EU);

KEY TERMS

Summary 139:
Resolving insolvency proceedings in the European Union (EU) — Resolution mechanism for the establishment of the Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM)
Resolving solvencies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 on the resolution of insolvencies in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European System of Resolution Mechanisms (SRSM).
It aims to ensure that resolution proceedings are carried out by national authorities and bodies.
It sets up a European system of resolution for the European banking sector.
KEY POINTS
The resolution mechanism consists of a Board of the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA).
The SRSM is composed of representatives of the national resolution authorities of the EU countries and the European Banking Authority (EBA). It is chaired by the European Commission and composed of at least one representative from each EU country.
The European Commission appoints the Chairperson and Vice-Chairperson of the SRA. The Chairperson is appointed by the Commission and is assisted by the EBA. The SRA’s budget is set out in the regulation.
Resolution procedure
The national authorities must submit to the Board all the information necessary to draw up and implement the resolution plans, as obtained by them in accordance with Article 11 and Article 13(1) of Directive 2014/59/EU, without prejudice to Chapter 5 of the same directive. The resolution authorities must ensure that the resolution plan meets the objectives of the resolution scheme. The plan must include a business reorganisation plan, which will restore the entity in question to financial soundness and long-term viability, as well as a valuation of the assets and liabilities of the entity.
In addition, the resolution authority must submit a report to the board every 3 years on the progress made in the resolution process. The report must include information on:
the assets, liabilities and assets of the institution;
the financial position of the company and its subsidiaries;
investment and lending prospects;
financial position of shareholders and creditors;
capital adequacy of the bank and its assets;
costs related to the resolution procedure.
A valuation is carried out as soon as possible after the resolution action or actions have been taken. The valuation must be carried out to assess whether the resolution proceedings would have received better treatment if the institution under resolution had entered into normal insolventcy proceedings. The board must also assess whether there is any damage to the natural or legal interests of shareholders or creditors caused by the resolution.
If the board considers that the institution in question does not meet the resolution objectives, it must take the necessary resolution action to carry out the resolution measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING REGULATED DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2002. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2003.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Solvency II: the process by which an insolvent bank is brought into bankruptcy proceedings.
* Resolution mechanism: the system in which a bank is placed under resolution proceedings. It aims to:
restore its financial health;
reorganise its business;
ensure that its assets are sound and its liabilities are repaid;
provide the board with the information it needs to make its decisions.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1024/2001 of 21 December 2001 on the establishment, on behalf of the Union, of a system of resolutions in the field of banking institutions (OJ L 304, 23.12.2001, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, EEA) No 1080/2001 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
