Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions: rules for aircraft noise
Noise - restrictions for aircraft emissions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 561/2013 on noise-related air traffic management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the European Union (EU)’s ‘balanced approach to noise’.
It aims to improve the soundness of air traffic in the EU by:
reducing aircraft noise at source;
improving noise abatement operational procedures;
not applying operating restrictions as a first resort, but only after consideration of the other measures of the balanced approach.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to aircraft engaged in civil aviation. It does not apply to:
aircraft engaged in military operations;
military aircraft;
passenger aircraft; or
passengers flying between airports.
EU countries must ensure that:
noise performance information is available to the European Commission and the public;
airport capacity and airport capacity planning and management are taken into account when drawing up noise performance information;
the number of aircraft flying in and out of an airport is assessed;
measures are taken to reduce aircraft noise.
Airport capacity, airport capacity and noise
The Commission must notify the relevant authority if it finds that the process followed for the introduction of noise-based restrictions does not meet the requirements.
The European Commission must inform the European Parliament, the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA) and the Commission if it considers that the regulation does not comply with the requirements of the regulation.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the Commission:
identifies the airport concerned;
draws up a list of airports with a high level of noise;
ensures the Commission has access to the information it needs to assess the impact of noise on safety, security and competition;
assesses the cost-effectiveness of the proposed measures;
provides the Commission with a cost-benefit analysis of the measures to be taken;
informs the Commission of the decision-making process and the reasons for selecting the preferred option.
Where the Commission considers that a combination of available measures is the most cost-effective measure or combination of measures, it must inform EU countries, the Commission and interested parties of the choice of option. The Commission must also notify the competent authorities of any organisation which could be affected by the decision.
Implementing the regulation
The competent authorities must give the Commission, the Member States, the EASA and the interested parties at least 6 months’ advance notice before introducing an operating restriction, ending at least 2 months prior to the determination of the slot coordination parameters for the relevant scheduling period.
Before introducing an operational restriction, the authorities must ensure the following information is provided:
the location of the airport;
a description of the noise level at source and the duration of the operating restriction;
noisy performance information.
If an airport has a high noise level, this information must be provided to the Commission by the competent authority.
A number of factors must be taken into consideration in the selection of the option to impose the operating restrictions. These include the following:
capacity and the number of flights to and from the airport, including the number and frequency of aircraft movements;
capacity of the airspace;
any effects on the European aviation network.
An airport must also be notified of any changes in the number, frequency or duration of flights.
When a decision is taken to impose a noise restriction, it should be accompanied by a report on the impact on the environment and the safety of aviation operations, including third-party risks. The report must include:
a summary of the results of the assessment of the impact, including an estimate of the expected noise benefit of the envisaged measures, now and in the future;
effects on the safety and environmental impact of the planned measures, including impacts on third parties;
impact on the EU aviation network;
an assessment of any adverse effects on air traffic safety, including on the capacity of the airports concerned, as well as on the effects on competition and on airport capacity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 			
The ‘Balanced Approach to Noise’ is part of the ‘EU Aviation’ package of measures designed to protect the environment, air traffic and safety.
For more information, see:
‘Noise’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Aircraft noise: the noise emitted by an aircraft at source.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the implementation by Member States of the Balanced Approach to Air Traffic Management in the field of noise operating restrictions (EEC) (OJ L



Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings: rules for the administration of cross-border proceedings (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
Cross-Border insolvencies: rules on the application of the regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 184/2011 — rules on cross-bankruptcy proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the administration and transparency of cross border insolvence proceedings in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the debtor’s assets and affairs;
the assets and assets of the debtor;
creditors’ rights.
It does not apply to assets and liabilities of a company that has ceased to exist or that has been wound up.
Rules for the insolvent debtor
The rules apply to the debtor and to the parties involved in the proceedings.
The debtor must:
be able to present its assets and accounts to the court;
provide the court with a copy of its accounts and liabilities;
have a written agreement with the court on how the debtor will be able to dispose of its assets, liabilities and assets;
make available all the information necessary for the proper administration of the proceedings;
allow the court to decide whether the debtor is insolvent or not.
Procedure for lodging claims
Creditors have the right to lodge claims and to withdraw their claims. They may lodge their claims in the same day as the debtor. The creditor may also lodge claims in another EU country where the debtor has an establishment or has an office.
In addition, the creditor may lodge a claim in the country where it has its head office. The debtor must have the necessary authorisation before lodging its claims.
A court may decide to open a group coordination proceedings if it considers that a group of coordination proceedings would materially impair the interests of creditors whose dealings with the debtor took place prior to the relocation. The court decides whether to open such proceedings or not to do so, depending on the criteria set out in the regulation.
Coordination of proceedings
The court in which a coordination proceedings has been filed makes an assessment of those criteria prior to opening coordination proceedings. If the court finds that the centre of main interests is not located on its territory, it should not open coordination proceedings, except where it is necessary to protect employees and jobs.
If the court considers that coordination proceedings will materially impair creditors’ interests, it may open a secondary or third-country coordination proceedings instead.
Right to request the opening of a secondary proceedings
A debtor may request to be opened in another country. However, where the law applicable to the proceedings so requires, that debtor must obtain authorisation from the court in that country before making such a request. This authorisation is valid for a maximum of 3 years.
Exercise of creditors' rights
The creditor has the right:
to lodge claims;
to exercise their rights in the case of a third country; and
to have their claims heard in the EU country in which they have their head office or where they have an establishment.
Administrative powers
The proceedings are governed by the rules laid down by the European Court of Justice (ECJ). The ECJ is responsible for:
supervising the proceedings of the parties;
coordinating the conduct of hearings;
cooperating and communicating between the parties in order to ensure that the proceedings are properly administered;
approving the approval of protocols, where necessary;
exercising the rights and obligations of the creditor in the event of a breach of the rights of the creditors.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/852 has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Crossborder insolventcy’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2018-852 of 20 December 2018 on the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 183/2011 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the rules of the Union relating to insolvences (OJ L 347, 21.12.2018, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (ER) 2018 - amending act(s) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 04.01.2021



Summary 142:
Limits on liability for damage caused to passengers in maritime accidents
Limitations on liability in the event of damage caused by passengers in marine accidents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on liability rules for damage to passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the liability of the European Union (EU) Member States’ carriers and the International Maritime Organization (IMO) in the case of maritime accidents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the carriage of passengers by road, air, sea or on board a ship;
the transport of goods by road and on board ships.
It does not apply to the carriage of goods and passengers by rail.
Liability of the carrier
The carrier is liable for loss of or damage to luggage and vehicles. For the loss suffered as a result of the loss of cabin luggage, the carrier must be liable if the incident which caused the loss was due to the fault or neglect of the passenger or the carrier was negligent in its handling of the luggage.
For the loss caused by damage to a vehicle, the liability is based on the following factors:
damage caused by the accident was caused by negligence;
damage was caused due to fault or negligence.
The liability must be paid within 3 years from the date of the accident. This period may be extended by a declaration by the carrier or by agreement of the parties after the cause of action has arisen.
Claimants must be provided with:
a written notice of loss or damage;
a certificate of the incident.
a copy of the carriage and of the damage caused;
information on the loss and damage.
the liability of insurance and limitation of liability of insurers.
Insurance and limitation
Insurers must provide the claimant with sufficient insurance to cover the liability arising from an accident. The liability of an insurer must be limited to the liability caused by an accident or negligence and must not exceed the amount of the insurance. Insurance must be guaranteed by the EU country in which the accident took place.
Compulsory financial security
The claimant must be entitled to a compulsory financial security (i.e. This is a unit of account that can be used for the purpose of safe-keeping. The claimant must have sufficient financial security to cover all the costs and expenses of the claim, including:
fees and expenses related to the costs of insurance;
claimants’ expenses relating to the cost of insurance.
Limits of liability
The claim must be made against the carrier and the carrier’s insurance company. Insurance companies must:
ensure that the claimant is entitled to at least EUR 21 000;
provide a certificate in the official language or languages of the issuing country;
notify the claimant within 30 days of the date on which the incident took place;
give the claimant a copy of their insurance policy;
make available to the claimant all the information necessary to prove that they are entitled to compensation.
In addition, insurance companies must provide information on the liability and liability of their clients, including the number of passengers and the number and type of goods they have insured. The information must be in the most appropriate format.
Requests for compensation
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must contain the following information:
details of the type and quantity of compensation to be paid,
the nature and duration of the compensation and the reasons for the compensation;
any other relevant information requested by the claimant.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to ensure a high level of protection for passengers involved in maritime accident compensation. These measures include:
measures to improve the safety and security of maritime transport;
measures for the prevention and control of maritime pollution;
rules on liability, insurance and liability.
See also:
‘The Athens Convention’ on maritime accidents (European Maritime Safety Agency).
KEY TERMS
* Carriage: a means of transport by road or sea.
* Vehicle: a vehicle that carries passengers, goods or passengers on board.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 21 December 2006 on liability limits for damage causing to passengers (OJ L 131, 21.12.2006, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, E.U. No 550) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Establishment of rail freight corridors in the European Union (EU)
Establishment, management and operation of railway freight corridors
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 establishing a framework for the establishment, management, operation and management of freight corridors within the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework to establish, manage and operate a rail freight corridor in the EU.
It sets out the purpose and scope of the freight corridor.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
railways and intermodal freight terminals (i.e. railways used exclusively for the transport of goods);
railway infrastructure;
traffic management;
the management of the corridor;
quality of service;
investment in the infrastructure.
Management
The management board of a freight corridor:
draws up, regularly updates and publishes a document containing:
all the information contained in the network statement for national networks regarding the rail corridor, drawn up in accordance with the procedure set out in Article 3 of Directive 2001/14/EC on the establishment of rail corridors;
a list and characteristics of terminals, in particular information concerning the conditions and methods of accessing the terminals;
information concerning the procedures referred to in Articles 13 to 17 of this regulation;
an implementation plan for the corridor, drawing up by the management board.
Implementation plan
A management board must draw up and publish an implementation plan. This must include:
a description of the railway infrastructure,
the characteristics of the rail infrastructure and
the number and type of freight terminals,
information on the quality of service on the corridor
information about the number and types of freight trains.
An implementation plan must be submitted to the European Commission every 3 years.
The Commission must periodically examine the application of the regulation and present to the Commission the results of the implementation plans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has established a number of rail infrastructure corridors in its internal rail market. These are designed to provide a high-quality, safe, efficient and environmentally sound rail transport network for goods.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Freight corridor: a rail corridor is defined as a railway line which is designed to carry goods between the points of departure of a railway station and the point of arrival of a train. It may be used for the carriage of goods or for the movement of passengers.

Summary 144:
Road transport operators’ professional competence — common rules for the occupation of road transport operator
Road transport professionals‘professional competence’
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2010 — professional competence for the occupational occupation of the occupation OF road transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that road transport companies have the necessary professional competence to operate in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to undertakings which:
operate in road transport services exclusively for non-commercial purposes or which have a main occupation other than that of road passenger transport operator;
have at least the following subjects covered by the regulation:
the knowledge to be taken into consideration for the official recognition of professional competence by Member States;
the organisation and organisation of the organisation of a road transport company.
It does not apply to the following undertakings:
undertakings engaged in road passenger services exclusively by means of motor vehicles with a maximum authorised speed not exceeding 40 km/h;
operators engaged in the transport of goods by road;
other undertakings engaged solely in road haulage;
unions and social security organisations.
Member States must ensure that undertakings have an effective and stable establishment and that they meet the following conditions:
they have at all times been able to meet their financial obligations in the course of the annual accounting year;
they are at least able to carry out their business activities in accordance with the rules and regulations applicable in the Member States.
They must be able to:
have a profit and loss account with a very visible or very visible logo;
be able to reproduce or print a number of visible or visible symbols;
provide proof of their financial standing.
Certificates relating to financial standing
Where a Member State imposes on its nationals certain conditions relating to good repute, and proof that these conditions are met cannot be provided by a document, that Member State must accept as sufficient proof for nationals of other Member States a certificate issued by a competent judicial or administrative authority in the EU country where the transport manager or any other relevant person used to reside stating that the conditions have been met.
In addition, Member States must:
ensure that an undertaking has the following documents:
a certificate with a micro-number;
a document with a number;
an undertaking’s annual report on its financial standing;
documents relating to driving patterns;
certificates for the design and printing of road signs and markings;
rules relating to security.
An undertaking must also provide the competent authorities of the Member State in which it is based with a report on the number of vehicles it has on its road network.
The Commission must publish an electronic register of all undertakings that comply with the regulation. This must be made available to all Member States and to the European Commission. It must also be available to the public.
Report
The European Commission must submit a report every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation to the Council and the European Parliament on the quality of the road transport sector. This report must include information on:
road transport companies’ annual accounts and financial statements;
road traffic statistics;
traffic statistics and statistics on road traffic accidents;
information on road transport accidents; and
the number of road traffic fatalities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 4 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise the rules governing road transport. The main objectives of the package are:
to:
improve road safety;
reduce administrative costs;
improve the level of protection of road users;
promote the efficiency of the transport sector;
facilitate the exchange of information between Member States’ competent authorities;
strengthen administrative cooperation between them.
For more information, see:
‘Road transport’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Driver: a person who is responsible for driving a motor vehicle.
* Transport manager: the person responsible for the organisation, organisation and management of a transport company and for the management of its staff.

Summary 145:
Beneficiaries’ right to benefits in the event of sickness and unemployment in another EU Member State
Benefics’ rights in the case of illness and unemployment
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the application of social security rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the functioning of the social security system in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules for the processing of claims for social security and unemployment benefits.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who are entitled to benefit in kind and in cash from the EU’s social security systems.
It applies to:
beneficiaries of accidents at work and occupational diseases;
unemployed people who reside in a Member State other than the EU country where the legislation applies;
benefit claimants who are covered by the legislation of the Member State in which they are resident.
The rules cover:
the right to receive benefits in kind, in cash and in kind-in-kind, in accordance with the rules laid down in the basic regulation on social security;
the obligation to pay the cost of the scheduled treatment and the obligation to be reimbursed by the institution which bears the costs of the treatment.
Claims
Claimants must make a request for information concerning the legislation applicable to the person concerned and to the institution in which the activity is pursued. The applicant must provide the institution with all relevant documents and supporting evidence. The institution must inform the applicant of its decision within 2 months of receiving the request.
the institution must send a copy of the decision to the applicant.
If a claim is rejected, the applicant must be informed of the reason for the decision and the reasons for its rejection.
In the event that the claim is not successful, the claim must be made to the competent institution of that Member State. This institution must take a position on the provisional decision within two months.
Recovery of claims
The applicant must pay the full amount of the claim. If it is not possible to recover all or any of the claims via this offsetting procedure, the remainder of the amount due must be recovered by the Member States’ national social security authorities.
Benefits in kind
The Member States must ensure that the benefits provided in kind are reimbursed on the basis of a fixed amount established for each calendar year. The Member States are responsible for the calculation of the amounts to be paid.
Unemployed people
The benefits provided to the unemployed person must be paid in kind:
in accordance with Article 24(1) and Articles 25 and 26 of the basic Regulation;
at the rate set out in Article 27 of the regulation;
for the duration of the period of unemployment.
Member States may decide to extend the validity of these benefits if they consider it necessary to protect the health and safety of the applicant or to ensure the protection of the general public.
Non-compliance with the basic rules
The Commission may, in exceptional cases, adopt measures to prevent or reduce the number of claims that are rejected or to limit the amount of benefits that can be received. The Commission may also adopt measures that aim to improve cooperation between social security institutions.
Coordination measures
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating the implementation of the implementing regulation in Member States. It is assisted by the European Social Security Information System (ESIS).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
The Social Security system is one of the main elements of the European Pillar of Social Rights (PSR). The PSR is the cornerstone of social cohesion in the EU. It aims to ensure that all citizens have equal access to social security.
For more information, see:
‘Social security’ on the European Commission's website.

Statistics on health and social determinants of health: EU countries’ data
Statistics on healthcare and social aspects
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013/EU — EU countries' data on health
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical system for the collection and transmission of statistics on health.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries.
It covers:
the EU’s population;
the population of the EU countries;
health-related indicators;
social determinants* of health;
quality of health care.
Data collection
EU countries must collect data on all aspects of health, including:
physical and psychosocial health problems;
sensory and psychological health problems.
The data must be collected in the form of surveys, surveys and surveys on the basis of surveys or surveys on health-related issues.
They must cover the following areas:
personal health and well-being;
personal hygiene;
physical, psychological and social development;
developmental disabilities;
gender identity;
children and adolescents;
education and training;
economic activity;
environmental factors;
employment;
organisation of the enterprise;
participation of the population in the EU.
National data collection
The European Commission collects the data from administrative sources. It also collects data on those areas through other sources, such as national statistics and surveys.
the data are collected on the following:
health and social indicators,
health indicators, and surveys,
the number of people covered by the EU statistical system,
employment statistics,
social indicators and surveys;
public health indicators, including the number of pregnant women,
children under the age of 18,
persons with disabilities,
participants in education and training,
people in the labour market and
children in the care of the elderly.
In addition, EU countries must provide data on:
children,
women and adolescents,
infants,
e.g. neonatal deaths (deaths up to 28 days) and
infant deaths.
Quality of health and quality of care
The quality of healthcare must be considered in the data collection and the quality of the data collected.
Information must be made available to the European Commission and the European Statistical System (Eurostat).
The Commission must ensure that the data are treated as confidential within the meaning of Article 13 of the European Data Protection Regulation (EDR) No 322/97 (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 147:
EU statistics on job vacancies: quarterly production of statistics
EU studies on job vacancy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 322/97 laying down the requirements for the regular quarterly production and transmission of statistics on Community job vacancies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down EU-wide rules on the production of quarterly statistics on EU job vacancies.
It aims to improve the quality of European Union (EU) statistics by:
improving the reliability and comparability of the data;
ensuring the comparability between EU countries’ statistics;
providing a clearer picture of the EU’s labour market.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to:
produce quarterly data on the number of job vacancies in the EU;
use business surveys to produce these data; and
apply seasonal adjustment procedures to the quarterly job vacancy data.
They must transmit the data to the European Commission (Eurostat) every quarter.
The data must be collected using business surveys. They must include:
the number of jobs available in each EU country;
the average number of vacancies for each job;
a description of the nature and duration of the job vacancies;
information on the nature of the vacancies and the reasons for them.
These statistics must be transmitted to Eurostat, which transmits them to the Council of the European Union, the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) every 3 years.
Eurostat:
assesses the quality and reliability of the statistics transmitted by EU countries;
prepares a report on the data transmitted;
publishes the results of the studies;
establishes a list of the countries that have complied with the regulation;
assists the Commission in the preparation of the report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 24 June 1997.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation is part of the Action Plan on EMU Statistical Requirements, which was endorsed by the Council on 29 September 2000, and subsequent progress reports on the implementation of that plan, identified as a priority the development of a legal basis covering job vacancy statistics.

‘EU job vacancies’ on Eurostat's website.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?

KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:

hydrogen containers designed to contain liquid hydrogen.

Requirements for the installation of the hydrogen systems

install the hydrogen system and its components in the vehicle in accordance with the requirements laid down in Annex II of the directive;

ensure that the components are installed correctly and that they do not leak internally at a higher rate than a specified number of litres.
The test procedures for hydrogen containers must include:
a burst test;
a maximum filling level test; and
a leak test.

Summary 149:
Access to the internal market in natural gas
Assessment of the conditions for access to the natural gas transmission system in the European Union (EU)
Assessing the conditions to be met by transmission system operators
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1775/2005 on conditions for accessing to the transmission networks in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the access of natural gas system operators to the EU’s internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the transmission system operator (TSO) for natural gas;
the European Network of Transmission System Operators for Gas (ENTSO for Gas);
the natural gas market operator (NGO);
natural gas market participants.
It does not apply to pipelines.
Access to networks
Transmission system operators must:
provide information on the status and efficiency of the system;
make available on request to those affected by any disruption;
publish at least annually, by a predetermined deadline, all planned maintenance periods that might affect network users’ other areas and in order to ensure equal benefits with respect to security of supply including in relation to transit.
Provide and publish non-discriminatory and transparent congestion-management procedures to facilitate cross-border exchanges in gas on a non-discrimination, transparent and proportionate basis.
Ensuring transparency
Network users must publish and publish information on:
demand and supply statistics;
capacity allocation mechanisms and congestion management procedures;
planned maintenance periods;
network users who wish to re-sell or sublet their unused contracted capacity on the secondary market;
information on the reasons for movements in the wholesale price.
The European Commission may request the ENTSOgensat for Gas Agency to prepare a network code on the basis of a framework guideline. The Commission may launch a further consultation in the course of preparing a draft network code under this paragraph.
Network operators must ensure that:
information is made available to all market participants, including the public, on a daily basis;
transmission system users are informed of any planned maintenance or repair work;
all planned maintenance and repair work must be notified to the public;
any planned maintenance is notified to network users.
In the event of contractual congestion, transmission system users must be offered unused capacity on a day-ahead and interruptible basis, and network users are entitled to do so.
Tariffs for access
The EU must set tariffs for access. These tariffs must be:
fair and transparent;
not restrict market liquidity and distort trade across borders of different transmission systems.
They must be based on the following principles:
transparency;
non-discrimination;
equity of access;
fairness of access.
Regulatory authority
The regulatory authority:
must:
take the necessary measures to ensure that tariffs are transparent and fair;
ensure that tariffs do not discriminate between network users and do not hamper the entry of new market entrants;
take appropriate measures to prevent, deter or deter market abuse;
have a deterrent effect on market abuse.
Penalties for market abuse
The competent authorities must be given the competence to investigate effectively allegations of market abuse and to take appropriate action.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive 2009/73/EC has applied since 1 July 2006 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 July 2007.
BACKGROUND
The transmission system is one of the most important components of the EU gas market and is essential to ensure the smooth functioning of the internal gas market. It is also essential for the efficient and secure supply of gas.
For more information, see:
‘The internal market’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a natural gas network operator.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2009/74/EC of 1 July 2009 on the application of the Regulation on Conditions for Access to the Transmission Networks of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2009 concerning common rules for access and conditions for congestion management of the transmission systems in the Internal Market in Natural Gas.

Summary 150:
Judicial cooperation in civil matters between EU countries and non-EU countries: mutual recognition and mutual recognition agreements
Joint recognition agreements (JARs)
Judicial Cooperation in Civil Matters between EU States and third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on mutual recognition* agreements between Member States and countries of the European Union (EU)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal framework for the conclusion of bilateral agreements between the European Community (now the EU) and its Member States on matters of mutual recognition.
It aims to facilitate the exchange of information between the Commission and the Member States in order to improve the quality of judicial cooperation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following areas:
jurisdiction and jurisdiction of the Court of Justice of a Member State or of a third country;
judicial cooperation;
rules on the interpretation and application of the law of the Member State concerned.
Notification to the Commission
Member States must notify the Commission of ongoing or already announced negotiations with a view to obtaining authorisation to open formal negotiations on an agreement or to conclude an agreement. Such notification should be given by letter or by electronic means.
The Commission must assess whether the envisaged agreement meets the conditions set out in Article 4(2) of the regulation. If the Commission considers that the agreement meets these conditions, it may authorise it to open or continue formal negotiations.
Authorisation to conclude the agreement
The Member State must submit a report on the negotiations to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission within 90 days of receiving the notification. The Commission must then give its opinion on the agreement’s constitutionality.
If the Commission does not consider that a sufficient Community interest has emerged, it must decide whether to open the negotiations or not. If it considers that there is no Community interest, it will not authorise the agreement to be concluded. If, on the basis of its assessment, the Commission intends not to authorise conclusion of the negotiated agreement, it shall give an opinion to the EU country concerned, as well as to European Parliament and to the Council, within 30 days of its decision.
In addition, Member States must provide the Commission with information on:
the nature and scope of the agreement;
the number and duration of the negotiations; and
the reasons for not concluding the agreement.
Exceptions to the above rules
The Regulation does not apply to:
mutual recognition agreements;
joint declarations of interest;
exchange of information on the subject matter and scope;
cooperation agreements on civil matters.
Amendments to the regulation
The European Commission may amend or conclude agreements with third countries relating to areas of civil justice that come within the purview of Title IV of Part Three of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (see summary). It may also conclude new agreements with countries that fall within the scope of Article 11 of the Regulation.

Summary 151:
Statistics on asylum and migration
Statistics on migration
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 343/2003 on the collection and analysis of Community statistics in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, analysis and dissemination of EU statistics on migration and asylum.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) 311/76.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the number of requests for taking back or taking charge of an asylum seeker;
the provisions on which the requests are based;
all decisions taken in response to the requests referred to in point (a);
the numbers of transfers to which the decisions lead;
requests for information.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with:
information on the number of applications for asylum and residence permits and residence of third-country nationals;
data on the numbers of requests to take back or take charge of a non-EU country’s nationals.
The statistics must relate to reference periods of one calendar year and must be supplied to Eurostat within 6 months of the end of the reference year.
National authorities must:
provide the Commission with information on:
all requests for asylum;
number of applications to take-back or take-charge of an applicant;
total number of asylum applications received;
percentage of applications received by the Commission.
Data must be disaggregated by age and sex, and by the citizenship of the concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLACE APPLY?
From 20 August 2009.
BACKGROUND
Asylum and migration statistics are a key element of EU policy. They contribute to the development of the European asylum system.
For more information, see:
‘Asylum’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Asylum seeker: a person who is a national of an EU country and who has applied for asylum in another EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 152:
Auditing of public-interest entities: quality assurance and the appointment of statutory auditors and audit firms in the European Union (EU)
Auditing and the selection of statutory auditor and audit firm in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on the quality assurance of statutory audits of public interest entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of statutory audit* and to ensure the independence of audit firms.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the statutory audit of:
public interest entities;
public-interest companies;
financial institutions;
other bodies and bodies responsible for the financial management of the EU’s financial system.
It does not apply to statutory audits carried out by the European Banking Authority (EBA) or the European Insurance and Occupational Pension Fund (EIOPA).
It covers:
audits carried out in relation to the financial statements of the European Commission;
statutory audits of EU countries’ national central banks;
regulatory audits of financial institutions.
Quality assurance
Each statutory auditor or each audit firm carrying out audits of a public interest entity is subject to a quality assurance review on the basis of an analysis of the risks involved. The audit committee is established to secure the audit.
The quality control review must be organised in such a way as to ensure that the audit results are consistent with the objectives of the statutory audit and that they do not present a risk to the public interest.
Dismissal and resignation of the auditor or the audit firms
Any competent authority designated by an EU country must forward the information concerning the dismissal or resignation during the engagement and an adequate explanation of the reasons therefor to the competent authority referred to in Article 20(2) of the regulation. The competent authority of the other EU country will take appropriate action.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It applies from 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve audit quality in the financial sector.
For more information, see:
‘Auditing for the public’ on the EBA's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Statutory audit: an audit of a statutory entity. A statutory audit is an audit performed by a statutory auditor for the purpose of assessing the financial position of an entity. It is also important to provide for an appropriate period within which such statutory auditor may not carry out the same audit of the same entity.

Summary 154:
Common organisation of the markets in fishery and aquaculture products (CMO)
Common operation of the fisheries markets in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 establishing a common organisation of markets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the placing on the market of fishery products* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the conditions for these products to be marketed.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
producer organisations* and inter-branch organisations* recognised in at least one or more EU countries;
the European Commission and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) of the European Economic Area (EEA);
the Commission.
Objectives
The CMO is an integral part of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) and should contribute to achieving the objectives of the CFP. It is designed to foster market stability and a closer correlation between supply and demand.
The objective of the regulation is to ensure that:
the products are marketed in accordance with the best available scientific advice;
they comply with the conservation requirements and international obligations of the EU’s fisheries policy;
their quality, size, weight, packaging, presentation or labelling requirements are in line with those of the International Union for the Conservation of Wild Fauna and Flora (IUCF) and of the World Health Organization (WFO);
they meet the requirements of the Convention on the Law of the Functioning of the Sea (COS) and the International Labour Organization (COTOJHA) on the protection of wild fauna and flora and fauna;
there are minimum marketing sizes corresponding, where relevant, to minimum conservation reference sizes.
Requirements for producers
Producer organisations must:
prepare and submit to the competent authorities of their EU countries a production and marketing plan containing the measures necessary for them to fulfil their objectives;
provide the Commission with a copy of the plan;
submit to the EFSA of the EEA, the European Commission or the European Maritime and Fisheries Agency (EMSA) with the details of the products they intend to market;
ensure that their products meet the standards set out in the CMO;
promote the sustainable use of fish stocks;
make sure that their members’ products comply with all the requirements laid down in the regulation.
Member organisations
The European Commission may decide to extend the rules to non-EU countries.
Liability
The Member State concerned may decide that non-members are liable for the costs paid by the producer organisation or the Inter-Branch organisation as a result of the application of the rules that have been extended to them.
Authorisation
The Commission may authorise the Member States to extend these rules for a limited period of time, including by tacit agreement, without an explicit renewal of the authorisation. This authorisation is subject to authorisation by the Commission. The Member States must inform the Commission of the reasons for the extension.
Exemptions
Member States may exclude certain products from the scope of this regulation if they consider that they do not meet the following criteria:
they do not fulfil the objectives set out above;
those products do not comply with conservation requirements or international obligations; or
they are not in compliance with the standards laid down by the EUCF or the IUCF.
In addition, Member States may exempt certain products which are in breach of the following requirements:
fishery stocks have been used up.
Products which are not used up may not be marketed until such stocks are used up;
products that are in excess of the stocks covered by this regulation are not marketed until stocks are exhausted;
fishing stocks which are over-exploited or over-fished may be used up until stocks have not been exhausted.
Conservation requirements
Member countries must ensure that products are preserved and marketed according to conservation standards.
Restrictions on the marketing of preserved products
Member states may impose restrictions on the sale of preserved and preserved products in order to protect the species concerned. These restrictions may include:
limits on the number of products that may be sold;
restrictions of the quantity of products which may be marketed;
rules on the storage mechanism;
requests for the storage of products;
minimum marketing sizes;
exemptions of products from certain species;
prohibitions on the use of certain species.
Rules on the labelling of products.
Non-members may be liable for all or part of costs paid to the producer organisations or the inter-branch organisations for the equivalent of the costs incurred by members.

Act

Deadline for transposition in the Member State
Official Journal
Council Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009

OJ L 139, 30.4.



Summary 155:
Securities clearing and settlement systems: European Securities and Derivatives Departments (CSDs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the establishment of securities clearing systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the establishment and operation of securities settlement systems* by securities clearing and payment systems (CSD) in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that CSDs are able to:
operate in a transparent, efficient and transparent manner;
provide a high level of security and security-related services;
ensure that they comply with the requirements of the EU’s securities settlement system (SSS) rules;
have a clear and transparent governance structure.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CSDs established in the EU. It does not apply to CSDs operating in non-EU countries.
Authorisation
A CSD must be authorised to provide any banking-type ancillary services and a credit institution designated in accordance with Directive 2014/65/EU (see summary). It must comply with all the conditions laid down in the regulation.
It must:
be established in an EU country;
be based in that country and have its registered office in the country in which it is established;
not have its head office in another EU country.
Provide the competent authority with all information necessary for authorisation, including:
the name and address of the CSDATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE;
the number of employees;
a description of the company;
its assets and liabilities
the amount of capital and liabilities.
The competent to grant or refuse authorisation.
In addition, a CSD may provide non-banking-type services in addition to the services listed in the annex to the regulation, such as:
clearing and payment services,
investment management and risk management;
transmission of information to the competent authorities;
assistance in the implementation of the rules of the SSS;
access to its assets, where required;
capital, together with retained earnings and reserves, which must be proportional to the risks stemming from the activities of the institution.
EU countries’ competent authorities must ensure that:
a CSD has the necessary financial resources to meet its obligations;
it has sufficient capital and reserves to cover its liabilities and capital requirements;
there is sufficient liquidity to cover the risks arising from its activities.
An EU country must ensure the following:
its CSDs have sufficient liquidity;
they have sufficient capital to cover their liabilities;
their assets are in a sound and liquid state.
They have sufficient assets to meet their obligations, including their capital requirements, and they have sufficient reserves to meet the obligations arising from their activities;
in addition, they have enough assets to cover all their obligations arising out of their activities, including the capital requirements for:
capital requirements,
capital adequacy requirements and requirements for capital adequacy;
reserves,
assets and liabilities,
asset adequacy and liquidity requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 18 June 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 June 2015.
BACKGROUND
The CSD sector is one of the most important components of the European financial system and plays a key role in the functioning of the financial markets.
For more information, see:
Securities settlement systems (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Securities settlement system: a system where securities are traded on a regulated market.
* Settlement system: the system in which securities are settled by a centralised centralised settlement system.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2014/552/EU of 18 May 2014 establishing a framework establishing a securities clearing system (CSDS) (OJ L 127, 18.5.2014, pp. 1-2)

Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund for transport infrastructure projects — rules on the allocation of financial support (2014-2020)
Cohesion fund for transport Infrastructure projects — details on the application of the Cohesion Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1316/2013 establishing the European Regional Development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European regional development fund (ERDF) to support projects in the fields of environment and trans-European networks in the area of transport infrastructure.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is the main instrument of the EU’s social cohesion policy. It is designed to contribute to the sustainable development of the European Union (EU).
It is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Social Fund (ESF).
The main objective of the ERDF and the ESI is to:
reduce greenhouse gas emissions from transport infrastructure;
improve the environmental performance of the transport sector;
contribute to the creation of a low-carbon economy in Europe.
The objectives of the EDF are set out in Annex I to Directive 2003/87/EC on the reduction of greenhouse gas emission from activities listed in Annex II to the European Parliament and of the Council.
Specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the ESD are laid down in Annex III to the directive. These include:
transport infrastructure projects of European added value;
environmental projects;
trans-European transport networks;
traffic management projects.
Eligible projects
The ESD is open to projects of common interest.
Trans-European infrastructure projects are eligible for support under the Connecting Europe Facility.
Projects eligible for ESD
The European Commission may grant ESDF financial support to projects that meet the following criteria:
they must:
have an environmental impact of at least 0.5 % of the total gross domestic product (GDP) for the period 2014-2020;
have the potential to reduce GHG emissions by at least 1 % of total GDP.
they have to comply with the following requirements:
be based on a common and programme-specific output indicator;
be designed and implemented in line with the EU emissions reduction targets;
meet the requirements of the Paris Agreement on climate change.
In addition, projects must comply with:
the principles of sustainable development;
the objectives and principles of environmental protection;
measures to reduce emissions of greenhouse gases (GHG emissions) from transport;
projects to improve the efficiency of transport systems;
use of energy efficiency and energy efficiency technologies;
cost-effective and environmentally sound transport systems.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 21 December 2013. It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 (see summary).
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle climate change, in particular:
Directive 2003/88/EC establishing a European Green Deal (see Summary);
Directives 2003/89/EC and 2003/90/EC (see Annex II).
For more information, see:
European Green Deal on transport (European Commission).

Summary 157:
European Regional Development Fund
European regional development fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 establishing the European Regional development fund (ERDF)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the EU’s Economic and Social Fund, known as the ERDF.
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
The ERDF is the main instrument of the European territorial cooperation goal.
The aim of this goal is to:
reduce disparities between regions’ development levels and to reduce their backwardness;
improve the quality of life in the least developed regions;
contribute to the growth and jobs of the most disadvantaged regions; and
improve economic and social cohesion in the most deprived regions, in particular in the north and the south.
To achieve these objectives, the aim is to support the creation of an urban development network and the development of sustainable urban areas, as well as to promote the sustainable development of rural areas.
Specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the ERDP are set out in the thematic objectives. These include:
the creation of a sustainable urban area;
the development of urban areas and the sustainable urban environment
the sustainable urbanisation of regions
the implementation of urban development networks
the urban development of cities and towns
the management of the urban areas
the promotion of sustainable rural areas and of sustainable tourism and tourism.
In addition, the objectives of the EU territorial cooperation goals are:
to contribute to the creation and development of a more dynamic and dynamic economy;
to improve the quality and sustainability of the environment
to promote sustainable development in the south and south-eastern regions
to develop sustainable urban environments
to support the development and sustainable urbanization of the north, south and east.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDERDF APPLY?
The regulation applies from 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Regional Development fund was established under Article 174 TFEU of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU).
For more information, see:
European Territorial Development Fund (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Regional development funds: the European regional development funds are the main instruments of the common European territorial policy.
* Territorial cooperation goal: the aim of which is to promote regional cooperation between the EU countries.

Council Regulation (EC) No 1080/2006/EC of 31 October 2006 establishing the framework of the Economic, Social and Directives for Regional Development Funds (EURDF) (OJ L 304, 31.10.2006, pp. 1-2)

Summary 158:
The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the European Territorial Investment Fund (ETIF) (European territorial investment)
The European regional development fund (ERTF) and European territorial investment fund (EUTIF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European Regional development fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It sets out the rules for the EU’s regional development funds (ERGDF) which are designed to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is designed to:
contribute to the sustainable development of regions and areas in the European Union (EU);
provide financial support for the development of rural areas;
support rural areas and areas which are disadvantaged by the effects of industrial transition;
improve the quality of life of the most disadvantaged regions;
promote sustainable development in mountain regions and coastal regions, particularly in the south-eastern regions and in the north-western regions, in the Baltic Sea.
Objectives
Objective criteria for designating eligible regions
The objective criteria are set out in Annex I of the criteria for the ERDF.
The criteria include:
the geographical coverage of the regions and the geographical area;
the development of urban areas,
the quality of living conditions
the economic and social situation in urban areas
the socio-economic situation in the most deprived regions
the level of development in the least disadvantaged regions
development of the rural areas
quality of life in the coastal areas
development in the mountain regions
economic development in coastal regions
integrated territorial investment.
Regional cooperation
The aim of regional cooperation programmes is to promote regional cooperation between EU countries and the regions of the EU.
Cooperation programmes are organised in the form of:
interregional cooperation programmes;
cooperation programmes with non-EU countries and non-EEA countries.
Eligible regions
EU countries must:
designate regions with a population density of at least 1.5 % of the total population of an EU country,
participate in a regional cooperation programme with at least 2 EU countries,
support the implementation of projects and programmes designed to improve the quality and sustainability of the environment and the quality, safety and health of the people in the regions,
develop and implement joint action plans.
In addition, the EU countries must ensure that:
projects and programmes are designed and implemented in line with the objectives of the programme;
programmes are designed with a specific thematic concentration, i.e. the programme is based on a specific objective;
projects are designed for specific geographical areas and regions, such as mountain regions, coastal regions and maritime regions, and
the programme is designed with the aim of promoting sustainable development.
Management and evaluation
The managing authority of an ERDF programme is responsible for the management and implementation of an integrated territorial investment (ITI) programme. It is assisted by a group of auditors composed of a representative from each EU country or third country participating in the cooperation programme and carrying out the functions provided for in Article 127 of the regulation. The managing authority must ensure the implementation and monitoring of the ITI programme, including:
monitoring and evaluation of the results of the investment;
implementing the programme and the evaluation plan;
drawing up, evaluating and monitoring the progress of projects;
preparing and implementing the implementation plan.
Monitoring and monitoring evaluations
The management authority must submit annual reports to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions. These reports must include a description of the programmes and projects that have received ERDF support and their results. The ERDF must also submit a report on the effectiveness, efficiency and impact of those programmes.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?

  It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the largest economy in terms of gross domestic product (GDP) and has the largest territorial area (GNDP) in the world.
For more information, see:
‘Regional development funds’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Decision 2014/852/EU of 21 December 2014 on the establishment of the European Rural Development Fund and establishing an Interregional Investment Fund for Europe (EURDF).
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 852/2014 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.



Summary 159:
Smart grids: smart energy infrastructure
Smart energy infrastructure: smart grids
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on smart grids and energy infrastructure — smart infrastructure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that smart grids* are built and operated in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the criteria for the selection of smart grids (i.e. energy infrastructure) projects and the conditions under which they can be approved.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to projects falling under the categories ‘smart grids’, ‘transmission infrastructure’ and ‘interoperability’.
It does not apply to:
the transmission infrastructure of the EU Member States;
the energy infrastructure of non-EU countries;
energy infrastructure for the EU’s internal market.
The rules apply to projects in the following categories:
smart grids;
transmission and interconnectivity;
electricity;
natural gas transmission infrastructure;
gas transmission infrastructure.
Decision-making procedure
The European Commission and the European Agency for the Safety of Oil and Gas (ESA) are responsible for the decision-making process for smart grids. They must ensure that the project is taken within the time limit set out in Article 10(1) of the regulation.
They must:
take into account the public interest;
ensure that the decision is taken in the best interest of the country in which the project will be located;
take account of the environmental and social and economic aspects;
economic considerations;
make the best interests of the project;
assess the impact of the projects on the economy and the environment;
apply the criteria listed in Annex II.
Cost allocation
The cost of the smart grid projects must be borne by the relevant TSOAs or the project promoters of the transmission infrastructure in the Member States to which the smart grids will provide a net positive impact, and, to the extent not covered by congestion rents or other charges, be paid for by network users through tariffs for network access in that or those Member States.
A cost allocation decision must be published in the Official Journal of the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission. It must contain the following information:
net positive impact;
cost-benefit analysis;
impact assessment;
environmental impact assessment.
Implementation and monitoring
EU countries must ensure the following measures are in place to ensure the proper implementation and monitoring of the regulations:
monitoring of the implementation of the rules;
implementing the regulation;
regular reporting to the European Commission on the implementation, monitoring and monitoring measures taken by the Commission, the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom), the European Energy Agency (EEA) and the ENTSO for Gas.
Monitoring and monitoring activities
The EEA and the EEA are required to report to the Commission every 3 years on the progress made on the regulation and to the EU countries on the monitoring measures they have taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 September 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 September 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
The Regulation is part of the Europe 2020 strategy, which aims to reduce energy consumption in the EU by 20 % by 2020.
For more information, see:
Smart grids (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Smart grids: a network of interconnected energy infrastructure that is designed and operated by a single company or group of companies.
* Interoperability: the ability of a network to operate in different parts of the world, such as the transmission and distribution of electricity, gas, water and thermal energy.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 529/2013 of 30 June 2013 establishing a European smart grid (OJ L 131, 30.6.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) No 530/2013 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 160:
Consumers’ Rights Online (DRO) platform
Consumers' Rights Online(DRO)* platform
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the consumer rights online (DROE) platform and on the complaint procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that consumers’ rights online are respected.
It sets up the Online Consumer Rights (ODR) platform.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all online transactions in the EU, including:
online sales and services;
online rentals;
services and products offered on the internet.
EU countries must ensure that the ODR platform is available to all consumers.
Complaints
ADR entities to which a complaint has been transmitted must:
provide the complainant with a complaint form;
ensure that the complainant has access to a case management tool;
make the complainant’s name and contact details available to the ADR entity.
The complaint form must be easily accessible for consumers. It must contain:
the name and address of the complainant;
the date and time of the complaint;
a description of the goods and services involved;
details of the product or service offered;
information on the conditions and conditions applicable to online sales and service contracts.
Dispute resolution mechanism
The complainant must submit a dispute resolution mechanism to ADR entities. This mechanism must be:
based on the information provided by the consumer;
specifically on the nature and content of the dispute;
made up of a link between the complaint and the complaint mechanism;
comprising at least 2 ADR contact points.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
It has applied since 9 July 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 July 2014.

The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect consumers online. These include:
Directive (EU) 2017/852 on consumer rights (see summary);
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 on consumer data protection (see overview);
Directives (EU and Euratom, Euratox) 2018 and 2018/955 on the protection of consumer data (see review).
For more information, see:
‘Online consumer rights’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 161:
Single payment scheme for the Common Agricultural Policy (CAP) for small and medium-sized enterprises (SAPs)
Single payment system for the common agricultural policy (CAPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the Single Payment Scheme (SPS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a single payment scheme* for the European Union (EU)’s (EU's) common agriculture policy.
It sets out the rules for the allocation of payment entitlements and the convergence of these entitlements.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to:
the EU countries that do not have a national reserve;
the countries which do not participate in the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) or the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EURDF);
the European Commission.
EU countries must apply the single payment system in accordance with the following rules:
a declaration of eligible hectares by the farmer. The declaration must include information on the regions targeted, the selected types of farming or sectors, and the level of support to be granted;
a description of the type of farming and the number of hectares to be covered by the scheme.
A notification must be sent to the European Commission by the EU country concerned.
The notification must include:
information on the number and type of land covered;
details of the types of cropland for which are eligible for the scheme;
an estimate of the amount of the total amount of land to be provided by the country concerned;
information about the type and quantity of land for which is eligible.
An EU country must also send a notification to the Commission, which must provide the Commission with a copy of the declaration and a description of how the scheme works.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The SPS applies from 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has been implementing the Common Agriculture Policy since 2007. It was amended by Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/2013, which introduced the Single payment scheme. The regulation is the first of its kind in the EU.
For more information, see:
The Single Payment System (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Single payment system: a system of payments to farmers for the use of their agricultural land.
* Regional reserve: an EU country is required to establish a regional reserve to provide financial support to farmers in its territory.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC, EC) No 550/2013 of 1 January 2013 establishing a Single Payment scheme (OJ L 183, 1.1.2013, pp. 1-2)
last update 14.02.2016



Summary 162:
Support programme for the smaller Aegean islands (SAAG)
Support Programme for the small and medium-sized economies of the eastern Mediterranean
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the support programme for small, medium and micro-sized enterprises in the Eastern Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) support programme to help the smaller and medium‑sized economies (SMEs) of the east Mediterranean, particularly those in the Greek islands, which are part of the EU’s common foreign and security policy (CFSP).
KEY POINTS
The SAAG is designed to:
support the development of the economy of the smaller islands;
promote the growth and diversification of the sector;
improve the quality of life of the population;
contribute to the sustainable development of local economies;
provide for the improvement of the conditions of living, working and working conditions of the people on the islands.
The programme covers:
the supply of agricultural products;
the preservation and development of small-scale agricultural production;
research and development projects;
exchange of information and best practices;
training and technical assistance;
monitoring of the implementation of the programme.
Scope
The support programme covers the following areas:
agriculture
the provision of products and services;
development of the local economy;
protection of the environment;
environmental protection.
Specific supply arrangements
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts for specific to the specific supply arrangements.
Sustainable development measures
The Commission may authorise the provision of certain agricultural products, services, including:
exports of certain essential products, such as fertilisers and pesticides, and
exchanges of information on the production and use of those products.
EU countries must ensure that the measures covered by the programme are compatible with EU law and that they comply with the relevant EU legislation.
In addition, the Commission may approve measures for financing studies, demonstration projects, training and technological assistance, provided that they are carried out in conformity with this regulation.
Approval and amendments
The regulation sets out the conditions for the approval and amendments to the programme, as well as the conditions under which the programme may be amended.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been providing financial assistance to the EU countries which are members of the European Economic Area (EEA).
For more information, see:
‘The European Neighbourhood’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* State aid: the financial assistance provided by the EU to an EU country.

Summary 163:
Statistics on road transport: EU countries’ statistical data on journeys between EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 on the collection of statistical data for road transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of statistics on the road transport of goods by road in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the transport of the goods (all the basic goods transport operations);
the journeys made by the vehicles carrying out this transport, with details of vehicle capacities and utilisation of these capacities (laden journeys with utilisation coefficient; unladen journeys).
It covers:
vehicle capacities;
load capacity;
the number of kilometres covered during the survey period;
total kilometres covered;
number of countries crossed in transit;
country codes used for the coding of countries crossing in transit (NUTS codes).
The data collected by the EU countries must be transmitted to the European Commission (Eurostat).
EU countries must transmit the data to Eurostat by means of the following methods:
transmission of the NUTS code, as specified in Annex III of the regulation;
data on the number and type of vehicles used;
information on the type of journey;
type of journey in accordance with the nomenclature in Annex IV.
National authorities must refrain from carrying out any formalities at frontiers between Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACE INTO FORCE?
The regulations apply from 1 January 1 to 31 December 2011.
The first transmission cover the first quarter of 1999 and the second quarter of 2000.

Summary 164:
Transmission and service of information and documents between EU countries (Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000 on the transmission and the service of documents between Member States (OJ L 347, 29.11.2000, pp. 1-2)
Transmission of information (EC, No 1349/2000)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EC, Euratom) No 1303/2002 establishing a European area for the transmission of information, documents and services of documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a European Area for the Transmission and Service of Information and Documents (Euratom).
It aims to facilitate the exchange of information between the European Union (EU) Member States on matters such as:
trade in goods and services between the Member States;
transmission of documents;
communication and publication of information.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
information transmitted by the transmitting agency to the receiving agency;
documents transmitted to the sending agency.
The transmitting agency must ensure that:
the information transmitted is in a standard form;
the document is accompanied by a translation into a language provided for in the regulation’s annexes;
it has been transmitted in a language of the Member State in which it is addressed;
there is no reason to believe that the information or document has not been obtained through the competent authorities or bodies of that Member State addressed.
In exceptional cases, at the request of a transmitting agency, a request for service may be made to the competent receiving agency. The request must be made in the standard form in which the information is transmitted.
If a document has been refused to accept the document pursuant to paragraph 1, the service must be remedied through the service on the addressee in accordance with the provisions of this regulation.
When a document is refused to be accepted, it must be provided to the transmitting or receiving agency by the sender. The sending agency must inform the transmitting and receiving agencies by the swiftest possible means in order to secure the missing information or documents.
Information transmitted must be protected by:
a confidentiality clause;
a non-exhaustive list of the information to be protected;
protection of the identity of the sender and the recipient;
protecting the confidentiality of the documents and information to which it has been provided.
Reporting
Reporting must be carried out in a manner that is consistent with the rules laid down in the annexes to the regulation. The reporting must be done in a way that ensures the confidentiality and integrity of information transmitted and documents. It must be transmitted in such a way as to ensure that it does not disclose confidential information or information that is not necessary for the proper functioning of the internal market.
Report
The Commission has prepared a report on the application of the regulation and its subsequent amendments. The report concludes that the regulation has generally improved and expedited the transmission* and service* of information to and from Member States since its entry into force in 2001, but that nevertheless the implementation of certain provisions is not fully satisfactory.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise EU law in the field of trade and services.
For more information, see:
Trade and services (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Transmission agency: the body that transmits information to or receives documents from another Member State.
* Document: a document that has been sent to or received by a Member State to be served by another Member States’ authorities.

Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition on the market
